The Legal Ruling of Munazarah: Three Levels
Author discusses the legal ruling (hukm) of munazarah, mentioning three levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three legal rulings (jawaz, istihbab, fard kifayah) that apply to munazarah based on its purpose and context.

Core Idea
Munazarah can be permissible, recommended, or even a collective obligation depending on the need to defend truth.

In this passage, the author grounds the legitimacy of munazarah in the Qur'an, then outlines three possible legal rulings for it. Understanding these levels is crucial for a debater to know when engagement is optional and when it becomes a communal duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qur'anic basis
Read the three verses cited. Consider what they command or permit regarding argumentation.
The verses emphasize arguing in the best manner, which sets conditions for munazarah.
2
Distinguish the three rulings
Understand the gradation: jawaz is the minimum (permissible), istihbab is recommended (rewardable), and fard kifayah is a collective obligation (required of the community).
The third ruling applies specifically to refuting doubts of opponents of truth.
3
Apply the ruling to context
Given a situation, determine which ruling applies. Is the debate merely permissible? Encouraged? Necessary for defending truth?
The author finds the view of collective obligation 'not far-fetched'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في مشروعيتها قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَجَادِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [النحل/ ١٢٥] وقوله: ﴿ وَلَا تُجَادِلُوا أَهْلَ الكِتَابِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [العنكبوت/ ٤٦] وقوله: ﴿ وَلَا يَأْتُونَكَ بِمَثَلٍ إِلَّا جِئْنَاكَ بِالْحَقِّ وَأَحْسَنَ تَفْسِيرًا ﴾ [الفرقان/ ٣٣]. فأقل مراتب حكمها الجواز إن كانت على الوجه المطلوب، وقال بعضهم باستحبابها . وقيل إن القدر الذي يلزم لإبطال شبه خصوم الحق فرض كفاية، وليس ببعيد والله أعلم.
The basis for its legitimacy is the saying of Allah, the Exalted: 'And argue with them in the way that is best' [An-Nahl 16:125], and His saying: 'And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the way that is best' [Al-Ankabut 29:46], and His saying: 'And they do not come to you with any parable except that We bring you the truth and the best interpretation' [Al-Furqan 25:33]. So the least level of its ruling is permissibility if it is in the desired manner, and some said it is recommended. And it is said that the extent necessary to refute the doubts of opponents of the truth is a collective obligation, and that is not far-fetched, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains the legal ruling (hukm) of munazarah (debate). The author first cites its basis in Islamic law, quoting three Qur'anic verses that command or permit disputation in the best manner: Surah An-Nahl 16:125 (argue with them in the best way), Surah Al-Ankabut 29:46 (do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way), and Surah Al-Furqan 25:33 (they bring parables, but we bring truth and best interpretation). After grounding it in revelation, the author presents three possible rulings. The minimum is permissibility (jawaz), provided the debate is conducted in the proper manner. Some scholars say it is recommended (istihbab). Others go further: the amount of debate necessary to refute the doubts of the opponents of truth is a collective obligation (fard kifayah) — meaning that if some qualified individuals undertake it, the obligation is fulfilled for the community; otherwise, all are sinful. The author finds this view plausible ('not far-fetched'), while acknowledging that Allah knows best.

Key Terms
جواز
استحباب
فرض كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the minimum ruling of munazarah?
A) Forbidden (haram)
B) Permissible (jawaz)
C) Recommended (istihbab)
D) Obligatory (fard kifayah)

Key Takeaway
Munazarah is not inherently blameworthy; its ruling varies from permissible to recommended to obligatory depending on the need to defend truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a situation where a group of Muslims is being attacked by false allegations against the Qur'an. Which ruling for munazarah applies here and why? Use the criteria from the passage.
?
Identify which of the three rulings (jawaz, istihbab, fard kifayah) would apply to the following scenarios: (a) A student debates a peer purely for practice, (b) A scholar refutes a heretical book that is gaining traction, (c) Two friends discuss a trivial difference of opinion.

Identifying the Subject of an Art: The Case of Debate
Author defines the subject matter of debate and valid refutation types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of any Islamic art (fann) by locating its universal investigations and distinguishing acceptable from unacceptable objections.

Core Idea
Every art has a subject whose essential attributes it investigates; debate's subject is universal dialectical investigations.

The author defines the art of debate by first establishing its subject matter, drawing on Quranic injunctions to argue well. Understanding the subject helps us see what counts as a valid objection and what does not, and it shows how every art has a defined scope.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the art's subject
Find where the author explicitly states 'the subject of this art is...' (وموضوع هذا الفن الأبحاث الكلية). Notice that the definition includes both universal investigations and their partial instances.
2
Identify the universal and partial investigations
The author lists universal investigations: contradiction (al-munaqaḍa), refutation (al-naqḍ), and opposition (al-muʿāraḍa). The partial investigations are specific instances like opposing a particular proof or refuting a specific evidence.
This shows that the art covers both general rules and concrete applications.
3
Distinguish acceptable and unacceptable refutations
The author says that every refutation by showing failure (al-takhalluf) or implying absurdity (istilzām al-fasād) is acceptable. Invalidating a premise without proof is called 'usurpation' (ghaṣb) and is unacceptable.
This distinction is crucial for valid dialectical practice.
4
Apply the general principle to other arts
The author gives examples: medicine studies the human body, arithmetic studies numbers, inheritance law studies estates. Each art investigates the essential attributes of its subject. Apply this to any Islamic science to determine its subject matter.
Essential attributes are properties that belong to the subject by virtue of its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[النحل/ ١٢٥] وقوله : ( وَلَا تُجَادِلُوا أَهْلَ الْكِتَابِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ) [العنكبوت/ ٤٦] وقوله : ﴿وَلَا يَأْتُونَكَ بِمَثَلٍ إِلَّا جِئْنَاكَ بِالْحَقِّ وَأَحْسَنَ تَفْسِيرًا﴾ [الفرقان/ ٣٣].
فأقل مراتب حكمها الجواز إن كانت على الوجه المطلوب، وقال بعضهم باستحبابها. وقيل إن القدر الذي يلزم لإبطال شبه خصوم الحق فرض كفاية، وليس ببعيد والله أعلم.
وموضوع هذا الفن الأبحاث الكلية التي تندرج تحتها أبحاث جزئية من حيث هي موجهة مقبولة، أو غير مقبولة، فالأبحاث الكلية : كالمناقضة والنقض والمعارضة الكليات، والأبحاث الجزئية المندرجة تحت هذه كمعارضة دليل بعينه، وكنقض دليل خاص.
وقبول ذلك وعدمه يعرف من هذا الفن، فكل نقض بالتخلف أو استلزام الفساد فهو وظيفة مقبولة، وكل إفساد للمقدمة، قبل إثباتها مع إقامة دليل الإفساد فهو غصب غير مقبول.
وقد علمت معنى الموضوع في المقدمة، وبه تعلم أن موضوع كل فن ما يُبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية كما أشرنا له الآن بالأمثلة، فموضوع علم الطب بدن الإنسان، وموضوع الحساب الأعداد، وموضوع الفرائض التركات، وهكذا.
وموضوع هذا الفن الأبحاث الكلية المشتملة على الأبحاث الجزئية من حيث إنها موجهة مقبولة، أو غير موجهة ولا مقبولة.
[An-Nahl/125] And His saying: "And do not argue with the People of the Scripture except in a way that is best" [Al-Ankabut/46] and His saying: "And they do not come to you with an example except that We bring you the truth and the best explanation" [Al-Furqan/33]. So the least rank of its ruling is permissibility if it is in the required manner, and some said it is recommended. And it is said that the amount necessary to invalidate the doubts of the opponents of truth is a communal obligation, and it is not far-fetched, and God knows best. And the subject of this art is the universal investigations under which fall partial investigations, in terms of being directed and accepted, or not accepted. So the universal investigations: like contradiction, refutation, and universal opposition. And the partial investigations that fall under these: like opposing a specific proof, and refuting a specific evidence. And the acceptance or non-acceptance of that is known from this art. So every refutation by failure or implication of corruption is an accepted function, and every invalidation of a premise before it is proven while providing evidence of invalidation is a usurpation that is not accepted. And you have known the meaning of subject in the introduction, and by it you know that the subject of every art is what is investigated for its essential attributes, as we just indicated with examples. So the subject of medicine is the human body, the subject of arithmetic is numbers, the subject of inheritance law is estates, and so on. And the subject of this art is the universal investigations that encompass the partial investigations, in terms of being directed and accepted, or not directed and not accepted.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of the art of debate (adab al-bahth). It begins by citing Quranic verses that establish the permissibility and even virtue of debate when conducted in the best manner: Surah An-Nahl 125, Surah Al-Ankabut 46, and Surah Al-Furqan 33. The author then explains the legal ruling: the minimum is permissibility if done properly, some say it is recommended, and the necessary amount to refute falsehood can even be a communal obligation. Next, he defines the subject of this art: 'universal investigations that encompass partial investigations' – these are the general categories of dialectical moves (contradiction, refutation, opposition) and their specific instances (opposing a particular proof, refuting a specific evidence). The acceptance or rejection of these moves is determined by the rules of this art. He distinguishes acceptable refutations (those based on failure of the argument or implication of absurdity) from unacceptable ones (invalidating a premise without proof). He then generalizes: every art has a subject investigated for its essential attributes, giving examples from medicine, arithmetic, and inheritance law. Finally, he reiterates the subject of debate as the universal investigations containing the partial ones, considered from the perspective of being properly directed and acceptable.

Key Terms
الموضوع
النقض
المعارضة
العوارض الذاتية

Checkpoint

A) Invalidating a premise before it is proven
B) Refutation by showing the argument fails to produce the consequent (al-takhalluf)
C) Refutation by showing the argument leads to absurdity (istilzām al-fasād)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The subject of an art determines what it investigates; in debate, only refutations by takhalluf or istilzam al-fasad are accepted.

?

Full Compound: Insha'i vs Khabari and Transmission Debate
Author discusses full compound sentences, distinguishing insha'i and khabari, and rules for debating transmitted insha'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish insha'i and khabari compounds and apply the rule that transmitted insha'i statements require verification of transmission before debate.

Core Idea
Performative sentences from the speaker are not debatable, but when transmitted they need transmission verification.

This passage completes the discussion of compound sentences by focusing on the full compound (al-murakkab al-tamm). The author distinguishes between performative (insha'i) and declarative (khabari) compounds. Crucially, he explains that while an insha'i sentence uttered by the speaker himself is not subject to debate, if it is transmitted from another source—such as a prophetic hadith—the narrator must verify the transmission. This clarification resolves a disagreement among scholars and has direct application in the practice of munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of full compounds
First, locate the definitions of 'complete performative compound' (insha'i) and 'complete declarative compound' (khabari). Note that insha'i includes commands, prohibitions, interrogatives, and wishes—statements that perform an action rather than stating a fact.
Insha'i is not truth-apt; it cannot be true or false. Khabari is truth-apt and is the standard proposition.
2
Understand the debate rule for insha'i from the speaker
The author states: 'if the speaker brings it from himself, then no debate occurs in it.' This means if someone issues a command or makes a wish on his own behalf, you cannot challenge its truth—it is not a claim. Debate only applies to propositions that can be true or false.
3
Recognize the rule for transmitted insha'i
If the insha'i is transmitted from someone else (e.g., narrating a hadith with a command), the narrator must verify the transmission (tashih al-naql). Once verified, he is discharged. This is because the authority of the statement depends on correct transmission.
The example given is prophetic hadiths that contain performative meanings.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Some scholars held that insha'i is never a subject of debate—neither from the speaker nor transmitted. The author rejects this, saying the first view (requiring verification for transmitted insha'i) is the most apparent and correct.
The disagreement is about whether transmitted insha'i can be investigated. The author insists it can, but only regarding transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب الإنشائى التام كجملة الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والتمني ونحو ذلك: إن جاء به المتكلم من قبل نفسه فإنه لا تجري فيه المناظرة، وإن نقله عن غيره طولب بتصحيح النقل، فإن صححه خرج من العهدة، وهذا هو الأظهر ؛ لأن الأحاديث النبوية التي يطالب من حَدَّث بها بتصحيحها منها ما معناه إنشائي كما هو معلوم، خلافا لجماعة قالوا : إن المركب الإنشائى لا يكون محلاً للبحث أصلاً، ولا يكون منقولاً حتى يطالب صاحبه بتصحيح النقل، والأول هو الصواب إن شاء الله - تعالى -. وأما المركب الخبري التام - وهو ماقدمنا أنه هو المسمى عند النحويين، وبالتصديق، وبالقضية، وأوضحنا معناه في المقدمة (٢)
And the complete performative compound (al-murakkab al-insha'i al-tamm) is like the sentence of command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, and similar: if the speaker brings it from himself, then no debate occurs in it, but if he transmits it from someone else, he is required to verify the transmission; if he verifies it, he is discharged (from responsibility). This is the most apparent view; because the prophetic hadiths which the narrator is required to verify include those whose meaning is performative, as is known. Contrary to a group who said: the performative compound is not a locus for investigation at all, nor is it transmitted such that its transmitter is required to verify the transmission. The first view is correct, God willing. As for the complete declarative compound (al-murakkab al-khabari al-tamm)—which we previously mentioned is what is called by grammarians 'the sentence', and is associated with assent (tasdiq) and with proposition (qadiyya), and we clarified its meaning in the introduction (2).
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion of compound speech by addressing the full compound (al-murakkab al-tamm), which is a complete sentence. The author first discusses the performative compound (insha'i), such as commands, prohibitions, interrogatives, and wishes. If the speaker utters such a sentence on his own authority (e.g., giving a command), it is not subject to debate because it is not a declarative statement that can be true or false. However, if the speaker transmits a performative statement from someone else (e.g., narrating a hadith that contains a command), then the transmission must be verified. This is because the authority of the statement depends on correct transmission. The author gives the example of prophetic hadiths, where a narrator must verify the chain of transmission (isnad). He notes that this is the most apparent opinion, contrary to some who claim that performative compounds are never a subject of investigation and cannot even be transmitted. The author asserts that the first view is correct. Then he introduces the complete declarative compound (khabari), which is the standard proposition that can be true or false, and refers back to the introduction for its definition.

Key Terms

المركب الخبري التام
تصحيح النقل

Key Takeaway
In Islamic debate, performative compounds (insha'i) from the speaker are not debatable, but transmitted performative statements require verification of their transmission before they can be used as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Subjects of Debate
Author classifies the locus of debate into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three loci of debate a given dialectical claim belongs to: proposition, division, or definition.

Core Idea
Every debate is about a proposition, a division, or a definition.

Before you engage in a dialectical exchange, you must know what exactly is being debated. This passage from the Adab al-Bahth tradition gives you the three categories: a complete declarative sentence (like a legal ruling), a division (like 'Halal is divided into obligatory and recommended'), or a definition (like 'Riba is an increase without compensation'). Knowing the category tells you how to argue correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim type
Read the statement being debated. Is it a full sentence that can be true or false? Then it's a proposition. If it lists parts or species, it's a division. If it defines a term, it's a definition.
2
Check if it is complete
A proposition must be a complete declarative sentence (murakkab khabari tamm). Incomplete expressions cannot be debated directly.
For example, 'Nabidh is intoxicating' is complete; 'Nabidh' alone is not.
3
Distinguish division from definition
Division (taqsim) breaks a concept into its parts or kinds, e.g., 'Ijtihad is either correct or mistaken.' Definition (ta'rif) states the essence, e.g., 'Ijtihad is expending effort to derive a ruling.'
In a debate, these require different argument strategies.
4
Apply the classification
Given a real debate topic from fiqh or usul, decide which of the three loci it falls under. This determines how to formulate the dispute and what evidence is relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتحصل أن محل المناظرة ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : المركب الخبري التام، وهو محلها الأصلي. الثاني : التقسيم . الثالث : التعريف، وقد أوضحنا وجهة المناظرة فيهما آنفا .
Thus it is established that the locus of debate is three divisions: First: the complete declarative compound, which is its primary locus. Second: division. Third: definition, and we have already clarified the aspect of debate in both of these earlier.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the subjects (or loci) of debate (mahall al-munazara). The author states that there are three types. The first is the complete declarative compound (al-murakkab al-khabari al-tamm), which is the primary locus because debates often center on affirming or denying a proposition. The second is division (taqsim), meaning the classification of a concept into its parts or species. The third is definition (ta'rif), which involves clarifying the meaning of a term. The author notes that the manner of debating about division and definition has already been explained earlier in the text. This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
محل المناظرة
المركب الخبري التام
التقسيم
التعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Division
B) Definition
C) Complete declarative compound
D) Incomplete expression

Key Takeaway
The locus of debate is always one of three: a proposition (primary), a division, or a definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Key Terminology of Division (Taqsim)
Author defines the terminology of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the four key terms related to division: muqassam, aqsam, qasim, and wasita.

Core Idea
Division requires precise terms for the whole, parts, and omitted intermediates.

Before using division as a logical tool, you must master its vocabulary. This lesson unpacks the specific terms that govern every act of dividing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the muqassam (المقسم)
The muqassam is the thing being divided, also called mawrid al-qisma. In any division, first find what is being divided.
2
Identify the aqsam (الأقسام)
The aqsam are the parts into which the muqassam is divided. They are the result of the division.
3
Understand qasim (القَسيم)
Each part relative to another is called qasim. This relational term helps in checking whether parts are properly distinguished.
4
Recognize wasita (الواسطة)
Wasita refers to intrinsic parts of the muqassam that are not mentioned in the division. These are omitted but still belong to the whole.
In jadal, wasita can be a missing category that invalidates a division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التقسيم اعلم أولاً أن الشيء المنقسم يسمى مقسما، ومورد القسمة، والأجزاء المنقسم هو إليها تسمى أقسامًا، وكل قسم بالنسبة إلى الآخر يسمى قسيما له . والأجزاء الداخلة في المقسَّم ولم تذكر في الأقسام تسمى واسطة . والتقسيم في اللغة جعل الشي أقسامًا، أي أجزاء . وهو في اصطلاح أهل هذا الفن ينقسم إلى نوعين : الأول : تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه . الثاني : تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته . أما تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه فهو تحصيل الحقيقة المركبة بذكر جميع أجزائها التي تتركب منها كقولك : الكرسي خشب ومسامير، والشجرة جذع وأغصان . وأما تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته، فضابطه ذكر أفراد الكلي التي هو قدر مشترك بينها بأداة تقسيم كـ «إما » و «أو» ، بشرط كون الأفراد متباينة أو متخالفة
Chapter on Division. Know first that the thing being divided is called 'muqassam' and 'mawrid al-qisma', and the parts into which it is divided are called 'aqsam', and each part with respect to the other is called 'qasim' for it. And the parts that enter into the muqassam but are not mentioned in the divisions are called 'wasita'. Division in language is making a thing into parts, i.e., portions. In the terminology of the practitioners of this art, it is divided into two types: First: Division of the whole into its parts. Second: Division of the universal into its particulars. As for division of the whole into its parts, it is obtaining the composite reality by mentioning all its parts that constitute it, like your saying: 'The chair is wood and nails', and 'The tree is a trunk and branches'. As for division of the universal into its particulars, its rule is mentioning the individuals of the universal, which is a common meaning among them, by means of a dividing tool such as 'either' and 'or', with the condition that the individuals be mutually exclusive or different.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on division (taqsim), a key concept in dialectic and logic. The author begins by defining the terminology: the thing being divided is the muqassam (also called mawrid al-qisma), the parts resulting from division are aqsam (plural of qism), and each part relative to another is a qasim. Any part that is intrinsic to the divided thing but not listed in the division is called wasita (intermediary). The linguistic definition is straightforward: division means splitting into parts. Technically, division is of two types. The first type is division of a whole into its constituent parts, i.e., analyzing a composite entity into its components. Example: a chair is made of wood and nails; a tree consists of a trunk and branches. This type aims to reveal the structure of a composite reality. The second type is division of a universal into its particulars, i.e., listing the instances that fall under a common concept. The rule is to mention the individuals (afrad) of the universal using a dividing word like 'either' or 'or', and crucially, the individuals must be mutually exclusive (mutabayina) or at least different (mukhalifa). This ensures the division is exhaustive and non-overlapping. The footnote references an earlier discussion on the types of mutual exclusivity.

Key Terms
مقسم (muqassam): The thing being divided; also called 'mawrid al-qisma'. [Note: In later logic, this term is used specifically for the divisible whole, not for the universal.]
أقسام (aqsam): The parts or categories into which the muqassam is divided. [Note: In mantiq, aqsam often refer to species of a genus.]
قسيم (qasim): Each part relative to another; a correlative term. [Note: This term is used in jadal to denote a co-division.]
واسطة (wasita): An intrinsic part of the divided thing that is not mentioned in the division; an omitted intermediate. [Note: In logic, wasita can also refer to the middle term of a syllogism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Qasim
D) Wasita

Key Takeaway
Division requires a clear muqassam, disjoint aqsam, and awareness of wasita.

Two Types of Division: Whole into Parts vs. Universal into Particulars
Author distinguishes two types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between division of a whole into parts and division of a universal into particulars, and identify the condition of mutual exclusivity for the latter.

Core Idea
Dividing a tree into trunk and branches is not the same as dividing 'animal' into species.

The author presents two fundamentally different kinds of division. One breaks a composite into its constituents; the other lists instances that share a common concept. The second type has a critical condition: the instances must be mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The passage divides division into: (1) taqsim al-kull ila ajza'ihi (division of a whole into its parts) and (2) taqsim al-kulli ila juz'iyyatihi (division of a universal into its particulars). Recognize each by its purpose and examples.
2
Understand Type 1: Whole into parts
This type reveals a composite reality (haqiqa murakkaba) by listing all constituent parts. Example: 'Tree is trunk and branches'. The parts together make up the whole.
3
Understand Type 2: Universal into particulars
This type lists individuals (afrad) that fall under a universal concept. It uses dividing words like 'either' or 'or'. The individuals must be mutually exclusive (mutabayina) or at least different (mukhalifa).
Mutabayina means no overlap; mukhalifa allows partial difference but still requires clear distinction.
4
Apply the condition of mutual exclusivity
For a valid division of a universal, check that the parts do not overlap. If they do, the division is flawed. Test this on examples from fiqh or usul.

Chapter on Division. Know first that the thing being divided is called 'muqassam' and 'mawrid al-qisma', and the parts into which it is divided are called 'aqsam', and each part with respect to the other is called 'qasim' for it. And the parts that enter into the muqassam but are not mentioned in the divisions are called 'wasita'. Division in language is making a thing into parts, i.e., portions. In the terminology of the practitioners of this art, it is divided into two types: First: Division of the whole into its parts. Second: Division of the universal into its particulars. As for division of the whole into its parts, it is obtaining the composite reality by mentioning all its parts that constitute it, like your saying: 'The chair is wood and nails', and 'The tree is a trunk and branches'. As for division of the universal into its particulars, its rule is mentioning the individuals of the universal, which is a common meaning among them, by means of a dividing tool such as 'either' and 'or', with the condition that the individuals be mutually exclusive or different.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on division (taqsim), a key concept in dialectic and logic. The author begins by defining the terminology: the thing being divided is the muqassam (also called mawrid al-qisma), the parts resulting from division are aqsam (plural of qism), and each part relative to another is a qasim. متخالفة (mukhalifa): Different: individuals are distinct, though may share some attributes.

The Harm of Confusing Inductive and Rational Divisions in Debate
Author warns against confusing the two types of division and explains the harmful effect in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the harmful effect of presenting an inductive division as if it were rational in a debate (jadal) and explain why the reverse confusion is harmless.

Core Idea
Confusing inductive with rational division harms the divider; the reverse is harmless.

In debates, divisions must be presented correctly according to their type. Mistaking an inductive division for a rational one invites a specific objection from the opponent, forcing the divider into unnecessary clarification. Understanding this asymmetry helps you avoid a common pitfall in munazara and strengthens your dialectical skills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of division
Recognize that rational divisions are exhaustive by intellect (e.g., dividing 'knowledge' into 'necessary' and 'acquired'), while inductive divisions are based on empirical survey and are not necessarily exhaustive.
2
Analyze the harmful confusion
If an inductive division is presented in the form of a rational division, the opponent, assuming it is rational, will object that the intellect allows another division (tajwiz al-'aql). The divider is forced to clarify, which harms his position.
This is the author's main warning.
3
Check the harmless confusion
If a rational division is presented as inductive, no harm occurs because rational division is already exhaustive; the opponent cannot object by positing another division.
The asymmetry is key.
4
Apply the lesson to debate strategy
In a debate, ensure you present divisions in their correct form. If your opponent misrepresents an inductive division as rational, you can use the principle of tajwiz al-'aql to object effectively.
This gives you a dialectical advantage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا جاء صاحب التقسيم بكل نوع من هذين النوعين على ماهو الأصل فيه لم يلتبس أحدهما بالآخر، وإذا جاء بالاستقرائي في صورة العقلي، أو بالعقلي في صورة الاستقرائي التبس أحدهما بالآخر . والتباس الاستقرائي بالعقلي يضر صاحبه ؛ لأن خصمه يتوهم أ) أن التقسيم عقلي فيعترض عليه بتجويز العقل قسما آخر، الذي هو مفروض في الاستقرائي، فيضطر إلى بيان أنه استقرائي جيء به في صورة العقلي. بخلاف التباس العقلي بالاستقرائي ، فلا يضر ؛ لأن العقل فيه لا يجوز قسما آخر .
So when the divider brings each of these two types according to what is its origin, one does not become confused with the other. But if he brings the inductive in the form of the rational, or the rational in the form of the inductive, then one is confused with the other. And the confusion of the inductive with the rational harms its author, because his opponent supposes that the division is rational and thus objects by positing another division through the intellect, which is assumed in the inductive, so he is forced to explain that it is inductive brought in the form of rational. Unlike the confusion of the rational with the inductive, it does not harm, because the intellect does not allow another division in it.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage focuses on the practical consequences of mixing the two types of division in argumentation. If the divider uses each type in its original form, there is no confusion. But if he presents an inductive division as if it were rational (i.e., using tardedid), then the opponent, thinking it is a rational division, will object that the intellect can conceive of another division (since rational division must be exhaustive of all logical opposites). In reality, the inductive division is not intended to be exhaustive in that sense, so the divider is forced to clarify. This harms the divider by putting him on the defensive. On the other hand, if a rational division is presented as inductive, no harm occurs because rational division is already exhaustive; the opponent cannot object by positing another division. Thus, the asymmetry: only the misrepresentation of inductive as rational is problematic.

تجويز العقل

Roles in Dialectic of Division and Its Conditions
Defines roles and conditions for division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objector and defender in a jadal exchange about division and evaluate whether a division satisfies the two conditions (jami' mani' and mubayin).

Core Idea
In jadal about division, the objector challenges by pointing out violation of the two conditions.

This passage clarifies the roles in a dialectical exchange about logical division. The one who objects to a division is called 'mustadill' and the defender is 'mani'' — a reversal of the usul al-fiqh convention. The author then restates the two essential conditions for a correct division of a whole into parts: comprehensiveness and exclusivity (jami' mani'), and mutual distinctness of parts in predication (mubayin). Any valid objection must target a breach of one of these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two participants in the division debate
The objector (mustadill) is the one who raises an objection (naqd) against the division. The defender (mani') is the one who makes the division or defends it by refuting objections. Note the reversal from usul al-fiqh: here mustadill objects, mani' defends.
This reversal is purely terminological; no dispute over the convention.
2
Recall the two conditions for division of a whole into parts
First condition: The division must be jami' (comprehensive, covering all intended parts) and mani' (exclusive, excluding non-parts). Second condition: Each part must be mubayin (distinct) from every other part and from the whole itself in terms of predication (al-haml), not in terms of actual existence.
Mubayin here means non-overlapping in linguistic or conceptual predication.
3
Map an objection to a condition violation
Given an objection, determine which condition is allegedly violated: e.g., 'This division leaves out some parts' violates jami'; 'This division includes something that is not a part' violates mani'; 'These two parts overlap' or 'A part is not truly subordinate to the whole' violates mubayin.
Passage states objection arises only due to one of these breaches.
4
Evaluate the defender's response
The defender (mani') must show that the condition is actually satisfied or that the objection misinterprets the division. The debate continues until one side fails to provide a satisfactory refutation.
The passage does not detail responses, but this is the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أوجه الاعتراض على التقسيم وبيان طرفي المناظرة في التقسيم
اعلم أولاً أنه قد اشتهر عند علماء هذا الفن أن الذي يعترض على التقسيم بأحد وجوه النقض الآتية يسمى مستدلاً، وأن صاحب التقسيم أو الذي يبين بطلان الاعتراضات عليه يسمى مانعا، عكس الاصطلاح الأصولي المعروف، ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح.
وقد عرفت مما قدمنا أن تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته تشترط لصحته ثلاثة شروط، وقد قدمناها قريباً موضحة، وأن تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه له شرطان وقد قدمناهما موضحين قريباً، وبينا أن الاعتراض على التقسيم إنما يتوجه بسبب اختلال أحد الشروط المذكورة، وقد قدمنا أمثلة اختلالها، وورود الاعتراض على التقسيم بسبب اختلال أي واحد من الشروط، ومثلنا لذلك كله في تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته، وسنذكره المثال هنا في تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه.
وإيضاحه أنك قد علمت مما مر أن الشرطين المشترطين لصحة تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه الأول منهما كونه جامعا مانعا، والثاني كون كل قسم من أقسامه مباينا لما عداه منها، ومباينا أيضاً للمقسم بالنظر إلى الحمل، لا بالنظر إلى التحقق، وبذلك تعلم أن الاعتراض يتوجه إليه باختلال أحد الشرطين كأن يكون غير جامع أو غير مانع، أو يكون بعض أقسامه غير مباين لبعض، أو غير مباين للمقسم من حيث الحمل.
Chapter on explaining the aspects of objection to division and explaining the two sides of the dialectical exchange in division. Know first that it is well-known among scholars of this art that the one who objects to a division by one of the following modes of refutation is called 'mustadill', and the one who makes the division or who demonstrates the invalidity of the objections against it is called 'mani''—the opposite of the well-known usul al-fiqh convention, and there is no dispute over terminology. You have known from what we previously presented that dividing a universal into its particulars requires for its validity three conditions, which we have recently explained, and that dividing a whole into its parts has two conditions which we have recently explained clearly, and we showed that objection to a division is directed only due to the violation of one of the mentioned conditions, and we have previously presented examples of their violation and the occurrence of objection to the division due to violation of any one condition, and we exemplified all that in the division of a universal into its particulars, and we will now mention the example here in the division of a whole into its parts. Its clarification: you have known from what has passed that the two conditions required for the validity of dividing a whole into its parts: the first of them is that it be comprehensive and exclusive, and the second is that each part of its parts be distinct from what is other than it, and also distinct from the divided whole with respect to predication, not with respect to actual existence. And by that you know that the objection is directed at it due to the violation of one of the two conditions, such as if it is not comprehensive or not exclusive, or if some of its parts are not distinct from some others, or not distinct from the divided whole in terms of predication.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the two participants in a dialectical exchange about division: the one who raises an objection to a division is called 'مستدل' (objector), and the one who defends the division by showing the objections are invalid is called 'مانع' (defender). Note that this is the reverse of the standard usul al-fiqh terminology, where 'مستدل' is the one providing evidence and 'مانع' is the one objecting. The author clarifies that there is no dispute over this conventional usage. He then reminds the reader of the two conditions for a correct division of a whole into its parts: first, the division must be comprehensive (جَامِع) and exclusive (مَانِع), meaning it includes all intended parts and excludes everything else; second, each part must be distinct (مُبَايِن) from the other parts and also distinct from the whole itself in terms of predication (الحمل), not in terms of actual existence. Any valid objection to a division arises from the violation of one of these two conditions: either the division is not comprehensive, not exclusive, some parts are not distinct from each other, or a part is not distinct from the whole in predication.

مانع
جامع مانع
مباين

Key Takeaway
The validity of a division of a whole into parts depends on two conditions: comprehensiveness and exclusivity (jami' mani') and mutual distinctness of parts in predication (mubayin); objections must target violations of these.

Identifying Violations in Division Conditions
Four objections to division, each violating one condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which condition of a valid division (comprehensiveness, exclusivity, distinctness of parts from each other and from the whole) is violated in a given classification.

Core Idea
A valid division must be both comprehensive and exclusive, with distinct parts.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), a proper division of a whole into its parts must satisfy two conditions: comprehensiveness (jami') ensures all parts are included, and exclusivity (mani') prevents non-parts from entering. Additionally, the parts must be distinct (mubayin) from each other and from the whole in predication. This lesson walks through four examples of flawed divisions, showing how each condition can be violated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division and its parts
Look at the proposed division of a whole (e.g., a chair) into its components. List the parts mentioned and understand the whole being divided.
2
Check comprehensiveness (jami')
Determine whether the division includes every necessary part. If a part is omitted, the division is not comprehensive.
Example: 'chair is gold and nails' omits wood.
3
Check exclusivity (mani')
Examine whether any part's term is so broad that it includes items that are not parts of the whole. If so, the division is not exclusive.
'Extended thing' (mutahayyiz) includes stones, not just the chair's wood.
4
Check distinctness between parts (mubayin)
See if any two parts share a common genus such that one could be considered a subset of the other. If they overlap in essence, they are not distinct.
'Iron pieces' and 'nails' are both iron, so not distinct.
5
Check distinctness of parts from the whole (mubayin fi al-haml)
Test whether any part can be predicated of the whole. If you can say 'the whole is this part', that part is not distinct from the whole in predication.
'Something to sit on' can be predicated of the chair itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمثال ورود الاعتراض عليه بكون غير جامع قولك في كرسي مركب من ذهب وخشب ومسامير: هذا الكرسي ذهب ومسامير)؛ لأن ذلك لم يشمل الجزء الخشبي، فهو غير جامع لجميع الأجزاء التي هي أقسامه.
ومثال كونه غير مانع قولك: الكرسي المتحيز والمسامير)، تعني: الجزء الخشبي ومسامير؛ لأن لفظ المتحيز وإن كان صادقا بالجزء الخشبي فإنه لا يمنع غير أجزاء الكرسي من الدخول كالحجر لأنه متحيز.
ومثال توجه الاعتراض إليه بكون بعض الأقسام غير مباين لبعضها الآخر قولك في كرسي مركب من خشب وصفائح حديد ومسامير: (هذا الكرسي قطع حديد - تعني الصفائح - وخشب ومسامير)؛ لأن قطع الحديد لا تباين المسامير؛ لأن كل واحد منها قطعة من حديد في الجملة، وإن كانت قطع الحديد المذكورة تختلف هيآتها؛ لأن اختلاف الهيات لا يغير الحقيقة، فلا شك في أنه يصدق على كل من الصفائح والمسامير أنه قطع من حديد.
ومثال توجه الاعتراض عليه بكون بعض الأقسام غير مباين للمقسم من حيث الحمل قولك: الكرسي مسامير وشيء يجلس عليه) تعني الجزء الخشبي؛ لجواز الحمل في قولك الكرسي شيء يجلس عليه.
An example of an objection being directed at it due to being not comprehensive is your saying regarding a chair composed of gold, wood, and nails: 'This chair is gold and nails' because that did not include the wooden part, so it is not comprehensive of all the parts that are its divisions.
An example of it being not exclusive is your saying: 'The chair is the extended thing and nails' meaning the wooden part and nails. Because the term 'extended thing' even if true of the wooden part, it does not prevent non-parts of the chair from entering, such as a stone, because it is extended.
An example of an objection being directed at it due to some parts not being distinct from other parts is your saying regarding a chair composed of wood, iron plates, and nails: 'This chair is pieces of iron' meaning the plates, 'and wood and nails' because the pieces of iron are not distinct from the nails, because each one of them is a piece of iron in general, even if the mentioned iron pieces differ in their forms, because difference in forms does not change the essence, so there is no doubt that it is true of both the plates and the nails that they are pieces of iron.
An example of an objection being directed at it due to some parts not being distinct from the divided whole in terms of predication is your saying: 'The chair is nails and something to sit on' meaning the wooden part, because of the permissibility of predication in your saying 'The chair is something to sit on'.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage provides four concrete examples of objections to the division of a whole into its parts, each corresponding to the violation of one of the two conditions mentioned earlier. Example 1: The division is not comprehensive (غير جامع). If someone divides a chair made of gold, wood, and nails by saying 'This chair is gold and nails', the wood part is omitted, so the division fails to include all parts. Example 2: The division is not exclusive (غير مانع). If someone says 'The chair is the extended thing (المتحيز) and nails', the term 'extended thing' is true of the wood part but also applies to non-parts like a stone, so it does not exclude extraneous items. Example 3: Some parts are not distinct from each other (غير مباين لبعضها). If someone divides a chair into iron pieces (plates), wood, and nails, the iron plates and nails are both iron, so they are not distinct categories; difference in form does not change the essence. Example 4: A part is not distinct from the whole in predication (غير مباين للمقسم من حيث الحمل). If someone says 'The chair is nails and something to sit on', the phrase 'something to sit on' can be predicated of the chair itself, so the part is not distinct from the whole in predication, even if it is distinct in existence.

Key Terms
غير جامع
غير مانع
غير مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In dividing the obligatory prayers into their components (ruku', sujud, qira'ah, etc.), which condition is violated if one says 'Prayer consists of ruku' and sujud' only?
A) Not comprehensive
B) Not exclusive
C) Parts not distinct from each other
D) Part not distinct from the whole

Key Takeaway
A valid division must be comprehensive (include all parts), exclusive (exclude non-parts), and have parts that are distinct from each other and from the whole in predication.

First Response to Classification Objections: Taḥrīr al-Murād of the Divided Term
Author describes the first type of response to classification objections: clarifying the meaning of the divided term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a response to a classification objection by clarifying the intended meaning of the divided term (taḥrīr al-murād min al-muqsam).

Core Idea
Clarifying what the divided term actually means can make a classification exhaustive and repel objections.

In dialectical debates (munāẓara), classifications (taqsīmāt) are often challenged for being incomplete. The first type of response, as explained here, is to clarify exactly what the speaker meant by the term being divided (al-muqsam). By showing that the term was intended in a specific sense, the classification can become fully comprehensive for that intended meaning. This lesson walks through an example from Arabic grammar to see how this works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original classification
Find the statement that divides a term into categories. In the example: 'The word (kalima) is either a noun or a verb.'
2
Identify the objection
What is the challenge? The objector says: 'It is an inductive division that does not encompass all parts because it misses the particle (ḥarf).' The objection is that the classification is non-comprehensive (ghayr jāmiʿ).
3
Identify the response via clarification
Look for the speaker's clarification: He says he meant by 'word' 'what indicates a meaning in itself (mā dalla ʿalā maʿnan fī nafsihi).' Particles do not indicate a meaning in themselves, so they are excluded from his intended meaning.
4
Check if the objection is repelled
Does the clarification make the division exhaustive for the intended meaning? Yes, because for that definition, noun and verb are the only categories. The classification is now complete for the specified sense of 'word'.

فصل في الأجوبة عن الاعتراضات الموجهة إلى التقسيم
أن
وهي راجعة إلى ما يسمونه تحرير المراد، وضابطه عندهم يوضح صاحب التقسيم مراده الذي قصده في تقسيمه بما يدفع عنه الاعتراض، وهو أربعة أنواع :
الأول : تحرير المراد من المقسم ؛ بأن يوضح أنه قصد به معنى لا
يتوجه إليه الاعتراض، وأنه غير المعنى الذي ظنه السائل .
ومن أمثلته أن يقول صاحب التقسيم الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل .
فيعترض المستدل هذا التقسيم فيقول : هو تقسيم استقرائي غير حاصر للأقسام الموجودة في الخارج ؛ لأنه لم يشمل الحرف، وهو قسم من أقسام الكلمة، وكل تقسيم غيرُ جامع ـ أي حاصر لجميع الأقسام ـ فهو فاسد .
فيجيب صاحب التقسيم - فيقول : أمنعُ كونه غير حاصر - بتحرير مراده، ويوضح أن مراده بالمقسَّم الذي هو الكلمة في المثال المذكور معنى خاص، وهو ما دل عليه معنى في نفسه والحرف لا يدل على معنى في نفسه بل في غيره، وحصر أقسام المقسم حاصل على المعنى الذي قصده، وبين أنه هو مراده، فيندفع عنه الاعتراض بتحرير مراده في المقسم .
Chapter on the responses to objections directed to classification. They refer to what they call 'clarification of the intended meaning' (taḥrīr al-murād). Its rule with them is that the author of the classification clarifies his intended meaning which he aimed at in his classification in a way that repels the objection. It is of four types: First: Clarification of the intended meaning from the divided term; by making clear that he intended by it a meaning to which the objection does not apply, and that it is not the meaning which the questioner supposed. An example of it is that the author of the classification says: 'The word is either a noun or a verb.' The objector then objects to this classification, saying: 'It is an inductive division that does not encompass the parts existing in external reality, because it did not include the particle, which is a part of the word, and every division that is non-comprehensive — i.e., not encompassing all parts — is invalid.' The author of the classification responds by saying: 'I deny that it is non-comprehensive' — by clarifying his intended meaning, and he explains that his intended meaning by the divided term, which is 'word' in the mentioned example, is a specific meaning, namely what indicates a meaning in itself, and the particle does not indicate a meaning in itself but in something else. The enumeration of the parts of the divided term is achieved according to the meaning he intended, and he makes clear that this is his intended meaning. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intended meaning in the divided term.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the responses (ajwiba) to objections directed at classification (taqsīm). The responses are collectively called 'clarification of the intended meaning' (taḥrīr al-murād). The author explains the general principle: the person presenting the classification clarifies what he meant by his division in a way that repels the objection. There are four types. Here, only the first type is detailed: clarifying the intended meaning of the divided term (al-muqsam). An example is given: one says 'the word (kalima) is either a noun or a verb.' An objector may claim this is an inductive division that is not comprehensive because it misses the particle (ḥarf), which is a part of speech. The responder replies by clarifying that by 'word' he means 'what indicates a meaning in itself' (mā dalla ʿalā maʿnan fī nafsihi), and the particle does not indicate a meaning in itself but in something else. Thus, for that specific meaning, the division is indeed comprehensive. The objection is then repelled by clarifying the intended meaning of the divided term.

تحرير المراد
المقسم
المقسَّم

Key Takeaway
A classification objection based on non-comprehensiveness can be answered by clarifying the specific intended meaning of the divided term, making the division exhaustive for that meaning.

Tahrir al-Murad: Clarifying the Divided Whole
First type: clarifying the divided whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first type of tahrir al-murad by clarifying the meaning of the divided whole to rebut an objection of incompleteness.

Core Idea
Defining the divided whole narrowly can make a division complete.

When someone objects that your division misses a part, you may clarify what exactly you are dividing. By specifying the meaning of the divided whole, you can show that the supposed missing part is not included in your definition. This is the first type of tahrir al-murad, a key dialectical tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims the division of 'word' into noun and verb is incomplete because it omits the particle. Read the objector's statement and note the type of objection (non-enumerative).
2
Find the defender's clarification
The defender clarifies that by 'word' he means 'that which signifies a meaning in itself'. Check if the particle meets this definition. It does not, so it is not part of the divided whole.
3
Explain why the objection is rebutted
Since the divided whole is redefined, the division becomes enumerative relative to that definition. The objector's assumption about the divided whole was wrong.

Source Text

ومن أمثلته أن يقول صاحب التقسيم الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل .

ومثاله أن يقول صاحب التقسيم : الإنسان إما ذكر وإما أنثى .
فيقول المستدل : هذا تقسيم استقرائي غير جامع للأقسام الموجودة في الخارج ؛ لأنه لا يشمل الخنثى المشكل، وهو قسم من أقسام الإنسان موجود في الخارج.
فيجيب صاحب التقسيم فيقول : أمنع كونه غير جامع؛ لأني أردت بتقسيمي الأقسام المشهورة الغالبة في الوجود، ولم أرد ما يشمل القسم النادر وجوده الذي لا يكاد يخطر بالبال في أغلب الأوقات، وتقسيمي حاصر على ما أوضحت من مرادي بالأقسام

كأن يقول صاحب التقسيم : العنصر إما تراب أو لا، وهذا إما هواء أو لا، وهذا إما ماء أو لا، وهو النار كما تقدم إيضاحه .
فيقول المستدل : هذا تقسيم ظاهره أنه عقلي؛ للترديد فيه بين النفي والإثبات، وهو تقسيم غير جامع ؛ لأن العقل يجوز قسما خامسًا وسادسًا .. إلخ من أقسام العنصر، وكل تقسيم كان غير جامع فهو فاسد .
فيجيب صاحب التقسيم فيقول : أمنعُ قولك إن كل تقسيم جوز فيه العقل قسمًا آخر غير مذكور فاسد ؛ لأن محل ذلك في التقسيم العقلي خاصة، وأما التقسيم الاستقرائي فلا يبطل بمجرد تجويز العقل قسما آخر غير مذكور، بل لا يبطل إلا بوجود قسم في الخارج غير مذكور في التقسيم كما تقدم إيضاحه .
فاندفع عنه الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من التقسيم، وأن مراده
Chapter on the responses to objections directed against division. They go back to what they call 'clarifying the intent' (tahrir al-murad). Its rule according to them: the author of the division clarifies his intended meaning in his division in a way that repels the objection. It has four types: First: clarifying the intent regarding the divided whole (al-muqsam); by clarifying that he meant by it a meaning that the objection does not apply to, and that it is different from the meaning that the questioner assumed. An example: the author of the division says: 'The word (kalima) is either a noun or a verb.' The opponent objects: 'This is an inductive division that does not enumerate all existing divisions, because it does not include the particle (harf), which is a type of word. Every division that is non-comprehensive—i.e., that does not enumerate all divisions—is invalid.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny that it is non-enumerative' by clarifying his intent. He explains that by 'divided whole' (al-muqsam)—which is the word (kalima) in the mentioned example—he means a specific meaning: that which signifies a meaning in itself. The particle does not signify a meaning in itself but in another. The enumeration of the divisions of the divided whole is achieved according to the meaning he intended, and he makes clear that this is his intent. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intent regarding the divided whole. Second type: clarifying the intent regarding the parts (al-aqsam). An example: the author of the division says: 'Man is either male or female.' The opponent says: 'This is an inductive division that does not enumerate all existing divisions, because it does not include the hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), which is a type of man that exists in reality.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny that it is non-enumerative, because I intended in my division the well-known, prevalent parts, and I did not intend to include the rare part that seldom occurs to mind most of the time. My division is enumerative according to what I have clarified of my intent regarding the parts.' Third type: clarifying the intent regarding the division itself. For instance, the author of the division says: 'The element is either earth or not; this is either air or not; this is either water or not; and that is fire' as was explained earlier. The opponent says: 'This division appears to be rational (aqli) because of the alternation between negation and affirmation, and it is a non-enumerative division because the intellect allows fifth and sixth etc. types of element. Every division that is non-enumerative is invalid.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny your statement that every division in which the intellect allows another unmentioned part is invalid, because the scope of that is specifically in rational division. As for inductive division, it is not invalidated merely by the intellect allowing another unmentioned part; rather it is only invalidated by the existence of a part in reality that is not mentioned in the division' as was explained earlier. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intent regarding the division, and that his intent is...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical treatment of how to defend a classification (taqsim) against objections. It presents a technique called 'tahrir al-murad' (clarifying one's intended meaning). The author explains that there are three ways to clarify intent to rebut an objection. The first is clarifying intent regarding the divided whole (al-muqsam). The example: if someone divides 'word' into noun and verb, an objector says it is incomplete because it misses the particle. The defender responds by clarifying that by 'word' he meant 'that which signifies a meaning in itself,' which excludes the particle. Since the particle signifies a meaning in another, it is not part of the intended divided whole. The division is then complete relative to that definition. The second is clarifying intent regarding the parts (al-aqsam). Example: dividing humans into male and female. An objector points out the hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) as a missing part. The defender responds that he intended only the well-known prevalent parts, not rare exceptions. The division is enumerative for that intent. The third is clarifying intent regarding the division itself. Example: dividing elements into earth, air, water, and fire. An objector says it is a rational division that the intellect can extend to other elements. The defender distinguishes between rational and inductive divisions: rational divisions aim to exhaust all logical possibilities, so any unmentioned part invalidates; but inductive divisions only require covering actual existent parts, not every logical possibility. The objection fails because the division is inductive, not rational. The passage thus teaches a key dialectical skill: when faced with an objection about incompleteness, one can clarify what was intended by the divided whole, the parts, or the nature of the division itself.

Key Terms
تحرير المراد
المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first type of tahrir al-murad, why does the objector's objection about the particle fail?
A) Because the particle is not a real part of language.
B) Because the defender redefined the divided whole to exclude the particle.
C) Because the division is rational, not inductive.
D) Because the objector admitted the particle is a word.

Key Takeaway
The first type of tahrir al-murad rebuts objections of incompleteness by redefining the divided whole so that the alleged missing part falls outside its scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar dialectical exchange: propose a division of 'hukm shar'i' (e.g., wajib, haram, mandub) and anticipate an objection that it misses a category like 'mubah'. Then use tahrir al-murad by clarifying the definition of 'hukm shar'i' (e.g., requiring a demand) to exclude mubah.

Tahrir al-Murad: Clarifying the Parts
Second type: clarifying the intent regarding the parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the second type of tahrir al-murad by clarifying that the intended parts are only the prevalent ones, excluding rare exceptions.

Core Idea
A division limited to prevalent parts can still be valid despite rare exceptions.

Sometimes an objector points out a rare case that your division misses. The second type of tahrir al-murad teaches you to clarify that your division covers only the well-known, common parts, not every conceivable or actual rare case. This defense preserves the validity of the division for practical purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rare case objection
The objector notes that the division 'man is male or female' misses the hermaphrodite. Recognize that this is a rare, exceptional case.
2
Understand the defender's limitation
The defender responds that he intended only the prevalent parts. This is an explicit statement of his intent. Note that he does not deny the existence of hermaphrodites; he simply restricts his division.
3
Evaluate the validity under this intent
Under the clarified intent, the division indeed enumerates all the intended (common) parts. The objection is rebutted because it assumed a different intent.

Its rule according to them: the author of the division clarifies his intended meaning in his division in a way that repels the objection. The enumeration of the divisions of the divided whole is achieved according to the meaning he intended, and he makes clear that this is his intent. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intent regarding the divided whole. Second type: clarifying the intent regarding the parts (al-aqsam). An example: the author of the division says: 'Man is either male or female.' The opponent says: 'This is an inductive division that does not enumerate all existing divisions, because it does not include the hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), which is a type of man that exists in reality.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny that it is non-enumerative, because I intended in my division the well-known, prevalent parts, and I did not intend to include the rare part that seldom occurs to mind most of the time. My division is enumerative according to what I have clarified of my intent regarding the parts.' Third type: clarifying the intent regarding the division itself. For instance, the author of the division says: 'The element is either earth or not; this is either air or not; this is either water or not; and that is fire' as was explained earlier. The opponent says: 'This division appears to be rational (aqli) because of the alternation between negation and affirmation, and it is a non-enumerative division because the intellect allows fifth and sixth etc. types of element. Every division that is non-enumerative is invalid.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny your statement that every division in which the intellect allows another unmentioned part is invalid, because the scope of that is specifically in rational division. As for inductive division, it is not invalidated merely by the intellect allowing another unmentioned part; rather it is only invalidated by the existence of a part in reality that is not mentioned in the division' as was explained earlier. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intent regarding the division, and that his intent is...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical treatment of how to defend a classification (taqsim) against objections. It presents a technique called 'tahrir al-murad' (clarifying one's intended meaning). The author explains that there are three ways to clarify intent to rebut an objection. The first is clarifying intent regarding the divided whole (al-muqsam). The example: if someone divides 'word' into noun and verb, an objector says it is incomplete because it misses the particle. The defender responds by clarifying that by 'word' he meant 'that which signifies a meaning in itself,' which excludes the particle. Since the particle signifies a meaning in another, it is not part of the intended divided whole. The division is then complete relative to that definition. The second is clarifying intent regarding the parts (al-aqsam). Example: dividing humans into male and female. An objector points out the hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) as a missing part. The defender responds that he intended only the well-known prevalent parts, not rare exceptions. The division is enumerative for that intent. The third is clarifying intent regarding the division itself. Example: dividing elements into earth, air, water, and fire. An objector says it is a rational division that the intellect can extend to other elements. The defender distinguishes between rational and inductive divisions: rational divisions aim to exhaust all logical possibilities, so any unmentioned part invalidates; but inductive divisions only require covering actual existent parts, not every logical possibility. The objection fails because the division is inductive, not rational. The passage thus teaches a key dialectical skill: when faced with an objection about incompleteness, one can clarify what was intended by the divided whole, the parts, or the nature of the division itself.

Tahrir al-Murad: Distinguishing Rational vs. Inductive Division
Third type: clarifying the nature of the division itself (rational vs. inductive).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational and inductive division and apply the third type of tahrir al-murad by clarifying the nature of the division.

Core Idea
Rational divisions must exhaust all possibilities; inductive divisions only cover actual existents.

An objector may claim a division is incomplete because the intellect can conceive of additional parts. The third type of tahrir al-murad teaches you to respond by clarifying that your division is inductive, not rational. Inductive divisions are only invalidated if an actual part is missing, not a merely conceivable one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection based on rational division
The objector assumes the division of elements is rational because it uses negation/affirmation. He argues the intellect can conceive of more elements, so it is incomplete.
2
See the defender's distinction
The defender clarifies that his division is inductive (based on observation), not rational. For inductive divisions, only actual missing parts matter, not conceivable ones.
3
Apply the principle
Since no other element was known to exist beyond the four, the division is complete for inductive purposes. The objection based on mere conceivability fails.

Chapter on the responses to objections directed against division. They go back to what they call 'clarifying the intent' (tahrir al-murad). Its rule according to them: the author of the division clarifies his intended meaning in his division in a way that repels the objection. The particle does not signify a meaning in itself but in another. The enumeration of the divisions of the divided whole is achieved according to the meaning he intended, and he makes clear that this is his intent. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intent regarding the divided whole. Second type: clarifying the intent regarding the parts (al-aqsam). An example: the author of the division says: 'Man is either male or female.' The opponent says: 'This is an inductive division that does not enumerate all existing divisions, because it does not include the hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), which is a type of man that exists in reality.' The author of the division responds: 'I deny that it is non-enumerative, because I intended in my division the well-known, prevalent parts, and I did not intend to include the rare part that seldom occurs to mind most of the time. Responding to Incompleteness in Inductive Division
Author illustrates how to rebut an objection that a division omits a rare case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the technique of 'tahrir al-murad' to defend an inductive division against an objection of incompleteness.

Core Idea
An inductive division is only as exhaustive as its intended scope.

In dialectical debates, one common objection is that a division is incomplete because it omits a rare case. This lesson presents a classic exchange where the division's author clarifies that his division is inductive, not rational, and only covers common cases. Understanding this move allows you to distinguish between legitimate and illegitimate objections to definitions and divisions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find in the Arabic where the objector (al-mustadill) claims the division is not encompassing (ghayr jami'). Note the example used: 'al-khuntha al-mushkil' (ambiguous hermaphrodite).
The objection assumes the division should cover all existing cases.
2
Locate the respondent's defense
Find the phrase 'Amana' kawnehu ghayra jami'' (I deny that it is not encompassing). This is the direct response.
The respondent does not add new cases but redefines the division's scope.
3
Extract the key move: tahrir al-murad
The respondent clarifies that his division is inductive (taqsim istiqra'i) and he only intended the well-known predominant divisions (al-aqsam al-mashhura al-ghaliba). This is the central dialectical technique.
Inductive divisions are not required to cover rare cases.
4
Assess the validity of the response
Once the intention is clarified, the objection fails because it attacked a scope the division never claimed. The division is exhaustive (hasir) within its intended meaning.
This demonstrates how clarification of intent can neutralize an objection.

The division's author responds: I deny that it is not encompassing, because by my division I intended the well-known predominant divisions, and I did not intend to include the rare division whose existence scarcely comes to mind most of the time. My division is exhaustive as I clarified from my intended meaning of the divisions.

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange typical of jadal. The objector claims that a division (e.g., of human into male and female) is incomplete because it omits the ambiguous hermaphrodite. The respondent clarifies that his division is inductive (istiqra'i), not rational ('aqli). In inductive divisions, one only intends to cover common cases. The objection is thus invalid because the division is not meant to be exhaustive in a strict rational sense. The key move is 'tahrir al-murad' (clarification of intent) which transforms the objection.

Clarifying Intention: Rational vs. Inductive Division
Author defines the third type of clarifying intention and gives an elemental division example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational and inductive divisions in a dialectical exchange and construct a response that clarifies intention.

Core Idea
A rational division requires exhaustive possibility; an inductive division only requires empirical coverage.

In dialectical debate, an opponent may attack a division as incomplete because reason can conceive of a missing part. But this objection only applies to rational divisions (taqsim 'aqli). If the division is inductive (taqsim istiqra'i), based on observed reality, the objection is invalid unless an actual case is found. This lesson examines how clarifying the type of division repels the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's assumption
The objector assumes the division is rational because it uses disjunction (either/or). He then argues that reason can imagine other parts (e.g., fifth element), so the division is incomplete and invalid.
Objection: 'This division appears rational; it is not exhaustive, so it is invalid.'
2
Identify the respondent's clarification
The respondent denies that every division allowing reason to conceive another part is invalid. He distinguishes rational from inductive divisions: rational divisions must be exhaustive for reason; inductive divisions only require covering actual existing parts.
Response: 'I deny your claim; that only applies to rational divisions, not inductive ones.'
3
Distinguish the two division types
Rational division (taqsim 'aqli) is based on logical conceptual analysis and must exhaust all conceptually possible parts. Inductive division (taqsim istiqra'i) is based on empirical observation and only needs to cover all actually existing parts.
Key point: Mere conceivability of a missing part does not invalidate an inductive division.
4
Apply the move in dialectic
When objecting to a division, first determine whether it is rational or inductive. When defending, clarify the intended type. This move repels objections that are appropriate only for rational divisions.
This is the third type of tahrir al-murad (clarifying intention).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : تحرير المراد من التقسيم . كأن يقول صاحب التقسيم : العنصر إما تراب أو لا، وهذا إما هواء أو لا، وهذا إما ماء أو لا، وهو النار كما تقدم إيضاحه . فيقول المستدل : هذا تقسيم ظاهره أنه عقلي؛ للترديد فيه بين النفي والإثبات، وهو تقسيم غير جامع ؛ لأن العقل يجوز قسما خامسًا وسادسًا . . . إلخ من أقسام العنصر، وكل تقسيم كان غير جامع فهو فاسد . فيجيب صاحب التقسيم فيقول : أمنعُ قولك إن كل تقسيم جوز فيه العقل قسمًا آخر غير مذكور فاسد ؛ لأن محل ذلك في التقسيم العقلي خاصة، وأما التقسيم الاستقرائي فلا يبطل بمجرد تجويز العقل قسما آخر غير مذكور، بل لا يبطل إلا بوجود قسم في الخارج غير مذكور في التقسيم كما تقدم إيضاحه . فاندفع عنه الاعتراض بتحرير مراده من التقسيم، وأن مراده التقسيم الاستقرائي الذي لا يبطل بمجرد تجويز العقل قسما آخر، لا التقسيم العقلي كما ظنه المستدل .
The third type: Clarifying the intended meaning of the division. For example, the division's author says: The element is either earth or not, and this is either air or not, and this is either water or not, and it is fire as previously explained. The arguer says: This is a division that appears to be rational because of the disjunction between negation and affirmation, and it is a division that is not encompassing because reason allows a fifth and sixth division etc. from the divisions of element, and every division that is not encompassing is invalid. The division's author responds: I deny your saying that every division in which reason allows another unmentioned division is invalid, because that is specifically the case for rational divisions. As for inductive divisions, they are not invalidated merely by reason allowing another unmentioned division; rather, they are invalidated only by the existence of a division in the external world that is unmentioned in the division, as previously explained. Thus the objection is repelled by clarifying his intended meaning of the division, and that his intended meaning is the inductive division, which is not invalidated merely by reason allowing another division, not the rational division as the arguer thought.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of 'tahrir al-murad' (clarification of intention) specifically for divisions. The author gives an example of a division of elements into earth, air, water, and fire. The objector mistakes it for a rational division (taqsim 'aqli) because it uses disjunction (either...or). He then objects that reason can conceive of other elements, so the division is incomplete. The respondent clarifies that his division is not rational but inductive (istiqra'i). Inductive divisions are based on empirical observation, not pure reason. Therefore, the objection fails because inductive divisions are only invalidated when an actual existing case is omitted, not when reason can hypothesize one. The key is to differentiate between the two types of division and clarify the author's intention.

Key Takeaway
Clarifying that a division is inductive, not rational, nullifies objections based on mere conceivability of additional parts.
