Responding to Non-Overlap Objections: Real vs. Conceptual Division
Author shows how to clarify a division as conceptual rather than real to rebut a non-overlap objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a division is conceptual (i'tibari) versus real (haqiqi) and correctly deploy this distinction to rebut an objection of non-exclusivity in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Conceptual divisions only need conceptual distinction; real divisions demand external exclusivity.

In dialectical debate, an opponent may object that the parts of your division are not mutually exclusive, assuming you intended a real division. This passage shows how to clarify that your division is conceptual, defusing the objection without needing to prove external distinctness. Understanding this move is essential for defending divisions in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate where the opponent claims the parts are not distinct in concept and external existence (takhaluf in both). This is the core challenge.
The opponent assumes the division is real, thus demanding external exclusivity.
2
Recognize the assumption
Notice that the opponent is interpreting the division as a real division (taqsim haqiqi), which requires parts to be distinct both in meaning and in the world.
This assumption is the source of the objection.
3
Formulate the response
The respondent clarifies that the division is conceptual (taqsim i'tibari), not real. This means the division is based on mental considerations, not external distinctness.
The response directly addresses the assumption.
4
Justify the response
For conceptual divisions, only conceptual difference (takhaluf) is needed, i.e., distinction in the mind alone. There is no requirement for external exclusivity.
The passage references earlier clarification and examples, such as dividing 'ilm into qadim and hadith.
5
Relate to real examples
Consider examples from usul al-fiqh: dividing 'illah into 'illah qiyasi and 'illah 'aqli, or dividing hukm into taklifi and wad'i. These are conceptual because the categories differ in attribute, not in separate external existence.
Applying the concept to real cases solidifies understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل في هذا النوع أن يظن المستدل التقسيم حقيقيا، فيُعترض عليه بأن الأقسام ليست متباينة في المفهوم والماصدق، فيجيب صاحبه بأن التقسيم اعتباري لا حقيقي ولا يشترط فيه تباين الأقسام في العقل والخرج، بل يكفي فيه تخالفها، وهو تباينها في العقل فقط دون الخارج، كما تقدم إيضاحه وأمثلته .
And included in this type is that the arguer thinks the division is real, so he objects that the divisions are not distinct in concept and referent, so its author responds that the division is conceptual, not real, and it is not required in it that the divisions be distinct in mind and external existence; rather, their difference suffices, which is their distinction in mind only, not in external existence, as previously explained with examples.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage extends the third type to a scenario where the objector mistakenly treats a conceptual division (i'tibari) as a real division (haqiqi). Real divisions require parts to be mutually exclusive both conceptually and externally. Conceptual divisions only require conceptual distinction, even if the parts overlap in external reality. The respondent clarifies that his division is i'tibari, thus defusing the objection. This is a common maneuver in usul al-fiqh, e.g., dividing 'ilm into qadim and hadith.

Key Terms

A) Real divisions are based on Shari'ah; conceptual divisions are based on reason.
B) Real divisions require parts to be distinct both in concept and externally; conceptual divisions require only conceptual distinction.
C) Real divisions are always exhaustive; conceptual divisions are not.
D) Conceptual divisions are invalid; real divisions are valid.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, clarifying that a division is conceptual refutes the objection that the categories are not externally exclusive.

The Fourth Type: Clarifying Doctrinal Basis
Author defines the fourth type with a dialectical example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a division is based on a specific madhhab and respond to objections by clarifying that doctrinal basis.

Core Idea
Divisions presuppose doctrinal commitments; exposing them neutralizes objections.

The fourth type of tahrir al-murad is about clarifying the doctrinal basis (madhhab 'ilmi) underlying a division. The passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objector claims a division is incomplete because it omits a category, but the division's author defends it by revealing the doctrinal assumption that excludes that category. Understanding this type helps you see that many logical divisions are theory-laden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division being offered
The division's author says: 'The known is either existent or non-existent.' This is a binary division.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says this division is not encompassing because it omits 'hal' (state), which theologians consider a mediating entity between existence and non-existence.
The objector's criticism is based on assuming 'hal' is a genuine category.
3
Identify the basis of the objection
The objector assumes a theological position that affirms the reality of 'hal' as a third category.
This is a key point: the objection is not neutral but grounded in a specific madhhab.
4
Identify the respondent's defense
The respondent clarifies that his division is built on the doctrine that 'hal' has no reality; sound reason restricts the known to a thing and its opposite.
By exposing his own doctrinal basis, he shows the objection is irrelevant.
5
Evaluate the dialectic
The objection fails because it does not respect the doctrinal framework of the division. The respondent wins by 'tahrir al-murad'.
This illustrates the fourth type: clarifying the intended doctrinal basis repels the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه صاحب التقسيم تقسيمه . ومثاله أن يقول صاحب التقسيم المعلوم إما موجود وإما معدوم . فيقول المستدل : هذا تقسيم عقلي غير جامع؛ لأن ما يسميه المتكلمون الحال سواء كانت معنوية أو نفسية قسم من أقسام المعلوم لم يذكر في التقسيم وهي واسطة ثبوتية بين الوجود والعدم، فلا هي من قسم الموجود ولا هي من قسم المعدوم وكل تقسيم غير جامع فهو فاسد . فيجيب صاحب التقسيم فيقول : أمنع كون تقسيمي غير جامع؛ لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب من يقول إن الحال المذكورة لا أصل لها، وإنما هي خيال وهمي محض، وأن العقل الصحيح يحصر المعلوم في الشيء ونقيضه حصرًا لا شك فيه .
The fourth type: Clarifying the intended meaning of the doctrinal position upon which the division's author based his division. Its example is that the division's author says: The known is either existent or non-existent. The arguer says: This is a rational division that is not encompassing, because what the theologians call the state (hal), whether attribute-of-state or psychological, is a division of the known not mentioned in the division, and it is a mediating entity between existence and non-existence; it is neither from the part of existence nor from the part of non-existence, and every division that is not encompassing is invalid. The division's author responds: I deny that my division is not encompassing, because I built my division on the doctrine of those who say that the mentioned state has no reality, and that it is a pure imaginary fantasy, and that sound reason restricts the known to a thing and its opposite without any doubt.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth type of tahrir al-murad: clarifying the doctrinal basis (madhhab 'ilmi) of a division. The example concerns the division of 'known' (ma'lum) into existent and non-existent. The objector, who likely follows the theologians (mutakallimun) that posit 'hal' (state) as a third category, objects that the division is incomplete because it omits 'hal'. The respondent clarifies that his division is based on the doctrine that rejects 'hal' as a real entity; only existence and non-existence are accepted. By clarifying his doctrinal commitment, the objection is repelled. This shows that divisions are often theory-laden.

Key Terms
المذهب العلمي
الحال
واسطة ثبوتية

The Fourth Objection to Division: Clarifying Doctrinal Basis
Fourth objection type to division: clarifying doctrinal basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the fourth type of objection to a division in dialectic by clarifying the doctrinal basis.

Core Idea
A division is only exhaustive within the doctrine it presupposes.

In dialectical debates, an objector may challenge the exhaustiveness of a division. But the divider can defend by clarifying that the division is built on a specific doctrinal basis. This lesson walks through the fourth type of objection: tahrir al-murad min al-madhhab al-'ilmi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divider's claim and division
The divider says 'The known is either existent or non-existent.' Note the terms and the exhaustive claim.
This is a standard dichotomous division.
2
Recognize the objector's challenge
The objector says the division misses a category: the 'state' (hal), which is neither existent nor non-existent.
The objector invokes a theological concept to show non-exhaustiveness.
3
Understand the objector's basis
The objector presupposes that the 'state' is a real entity (wasita thubutiyya) that must be included.
This assumption comes from certain theological schools.
4
See the divider's response
The divider denies the objection by clarifying his doctrine: he rejects the reality of states; for him, everything is either existent or non-existent.
The response is 'I built my division on the doctrine that states are illusory.'
5
Evaluate the response's effectiveness
The objector's criticism only holds if his own doctrine is accepted; the divider shows the objection is irrelevant because it imports a foreign assumption.
Thus the division is exhaustive within its own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه صاحب التقسيم تقسيمه . ومثاله أن يقول صاحب التقسيم المعلوم إما موجود وإما معدوم . فيقول المستدل : هذا تقسيم عقلي غير جامع؛ لأن ما يسميه المتكلمون الحال سواء كانت معنوية أو نفسية قسم من أقسام المعلوم لم يذكر في التقسيم وهي واسطة ثبوتية بين الوجود والعدم، لا هي من قسم الموجود ولا هي من قسم المعدوم وكل تقسيم غير جامع فهو فاسد . فيجيب صاحب التقسيم فيقول : أمنع كون تقسيمي غير جامع؛ لأني بنيت تقسيمي على مذهب من يقول إن الحال المذكورة لا أصل لها، وإنما هي خيال وهمي محض، وأن العقل الصحيح يحصر المعلوم في الشيء ونقيضه حصرًا لا شك فيه . فقد أبطل الاعتراض الوارد على تقسيمه بتحرير المراد الذي قصده من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه تقسيمه، وقد أوضحنا في غير هذا أن الحال المذكورة خيال وهمي لا حقيقة له البتة، وأن كل ماليس بموجود فهو معدوم قطعا ، وكلَّ ما ليس بمعدوم فهو موجود قطعا كما لا يخفى
The fourth type: clarifying the intended meaning of the doctrinal basis on which the divider built his division. An example: the divider says 'The known is either existent or non-existent.' The objector says: 'This is a rational division that is not exhaustive, because what the theologians call 'state' (al-hal), whether moral or psychological, is a part of the known that is not mentioned in the division. It is a mediating entity between existence and non-existence; it is neither of the category of existent nor of the category of non-existent. Every non-exhaustive division is invalid.' The divider responds: 'I deny that my division is non-exhaustive, because I built my division on the doctrine of those who say that the aforementioned state has no basis and is pure imaginary illusion, and that sound reason limits the known to a thing and its contradictory without any doubt.' Thus he invalidated the objection to his division by clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine on which he built his division. And we have clarified elsewhere that the aforementioned state is an imaginary illusion having no reality at all, and that whatever is not existent is definitely non-existent, and whatever is not non-existent is definitely existent, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of objection to a division (taqsim) in dialectic. The objection is called 'clarifying the intended meaning of the scientific doctrine' on which the divider based his division. The author provides an example: the divider says 'The known is either existent or non-existent.' The objector argues that this division is not exhaustive because theologians posit a third category called 'state' (hal), which is neither existent nor non-existent. The divider responds by clarifying that his division is built on a specific doctrine that denies the reality of such states, holding that whatever is not existent is non-existent, and vice versa. Thus the objection fails because it imports a doctrine not assumed by the divider. The author then affirms that the state is indeed an illusion, reinforcing the defense. The key logical move is that a division can be defended by specifying the doctrinal framework within which it is made; the objector's criticism must respect that framework.

مذهب علمي

Procedure for Objecting and Responding to Divisions
Procedural rules for objections and responses to divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the correct procedural steps for objecting to and defending a division in a dialectical debate, based on whether the divider commits to its correctness.

Core Idea
The validity of an objection depends on the divider's commitment level.

In dialectical debates, divisions are common tools for analysis. However, not every objection is permissible at every stage. This passage from the adab al-bahth tradition establishes a clear procedure: the first step is to determine whether the divider is simply transmitting a division or endorsing it. Only then can the proper objections and responses follow. Understanding this sequence ensures that debates remain structured and fair.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divider's stance
Read the passage and note the two cases: the divider may be a mere transmitter (naqil) or someone who commits to correctness (multazim).
This determines the scope of permissible objections.
2
Determine permissible objections for a transmitter
If the divider is a transmitter without commitment, the only valid objection is to challenge the accuracy of the transmission (source verification).
Other objections (e.g., non-exhaustiveness) are premature.
3
Determine permissible objections for a committer
If the divider commits, first allow clarification (istifsar) for unclear terms. If terms are clear, then objections to the division's validity (as previously taught) are allowed.
The divider must respond; if unable, he must propose a new division.
4
Apply the outcome rule
If all objections are answered satisfactorily, the objector must accept the division. If the divider fails, he must move to another division.
This ensures resolution or progression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ما ذكرنا من الاعتراضات على التقسيم والأجوبة عنها ينبغي أن ينظر قبله هل صاحب التقسيم ناقل له عن غيره أو لا؟ فإن كان عن غيره ولم يلتزم صحته لم يتوجه إليه إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل عن المصدر الذي نقل عنه، من كتاب أو راو مثلا . وإن كان جاء التقسيم من تلقاء نفسه أو نقله عن غيره والتزم صحته فإن كان في كلامه شيء غير واضح فللسائل الاستفسار عنه، وعليه البيان ،والإيضاح، وإن كانت الكلمات لا إجمال فيها ولا لبس ولا اشتراك فللسائل الاعتراض على التقسيم بالأوجه التي ذكرنا، ولخصمه الجواب عنها بالأجوبة التي بينا وإن عجز عن الجواب عن أحد الاعتراضات المذكورة فعليه الانتقال إلى تقسيم آخر، وإن وجد تقسيمه صحيحًا لا خلل فيه وجب عليه تسليمه والاعتراف بصحته، وهذا هو ترتيب المناظرة في التقسيم .
Know that regarding the objections we have mentioned about division and the answers to them, one must first consider whether the divider is transmitting it from someone else or not. If it is from someone else and he does not commit to its correctness, then only the demand to verify the transmission from the source — from a book or narrator for example — is directed at him. If the division comes from himself or he transmitted it from someone else and committed to its correctness, then if there is something unclear in his speech, the questioner may ask for clarification, and the divider must explain and clarify. If the words have no ambiguity, obscurity, or equivocation, then the questioner may object to the division in the ways we have mentioned, and his opponent must answer with the answers we have explained. If he is unable to answer one of the mentioned objections, then he must move to another division. If he finds his division correct without defect, then he must accept it and acknowledge its correctness. This is the procedure of disputation concerning division.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage lays out the procedural rules for objecting to and defending a division in dialectical debate. The author first distinguishes two cases: (1) The divider is merely transmitting a division from someone else without endorsing it. In this case, the only valid objection is to challenge the accuracy of the transmission, i.e., whether he correctly quoted the source. (2) The divider either originated the division himself or transmitted it while committing to its correctness. In this case, the objector may first ask for clarification if any part is unclear. If the terms are clear, the objector may raise objections using the types previously discussed (e.g., not exhaustive, categories overlap, etc.). The divider must respond with the corresponding answers. If the divider cannot answer an objection, he must abandon his division and propose another. If the division withstands all objections, the objector must accept it. This is the proper order (tartib) of disputation regarding divisions. The passage emphasizes that the debate must be procedurally fair and structured.

Key Terms
ناقل
ملتزم
استفسار
ترتيب المناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debate, the validity of objections to a division depends on the divider's commitment: if merely transmitting, only source accuracy can be challenged; if committed, substantive objections follow after clarifications.

Rules for Objecting to a Logical Division
Author explains the proper procedure for objecting to a division in dialectical exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the correct procedure for objecting to a taqseem in a debate, distinguishing between a transmitter and an originator, and responding appropriately at each stage.

Core Idea
Object to the division, not the divider—unless he owns it.

When you hear a division of a concept into parts, your first instinct might be to jump in with objections. But the adab al-bahth tradition teaches us to pause and ask: Is the speaker merely quoting someone, or has he committed to this division himself? The answer determines what kind of objection is legitimate. In this lesson, we will walk through the step-by-step protocol for objecting to a division, from verifying transmission to, if necessary, proposing an alternative division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divider's stance
First, determine whether the person presenting the division is a naqil (transmitter) or is the originator/endorser. If he is just transmitting, your objection is limited to demanding proof of accurate transmission. If he endorses it, you may proceed to substantive objections.
The passage contrasts 'nāqil 'an ghayrihi' (transmitter from another) with 'jā'a bihi min tilqā'i nafsihi' (came up with it himself).
2
Check for ambiguity in expression
If the divider endorses the division and his words have any vagueness (ijmāl), obscurity (labs), or homonymy (ishtirāk), the questioner must first ask for clarification. Only after terms are clear may the questioner raise standard objections.
Keywords: ijmal, labs, ishtirak. The divider must then explain and clarify.
3
Raise a standard objection if terms are clear
If the terms are clear, the questioner may object using the typical methods: e.g., the division is not exhaustive, the parts overlap, or a part does not belong to the concept. These objections correspond to the earlier lessons on valid division criteria.
4
Respond or move to a new division
The opponent (divider) must answer each objection using the established answers. If he cannot answer a valid objection, he must abandon that division and propose another. If the division is sound, he must accept it.
The passage ends with wa hādhā huwa tartīb al-munāẓara fī al-taqsīm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد أبطل الاعتراض الوارد على تقسيمه بتحرير المراد الذي قصده من المذهب العلمي الذي بني عليه تقسيمه، وقد أوضحنا في غير هذا أن الحال المذكورة خيال وهمي لا حقيقة له البتة، وأن كل ماليس بموجود فهو معدوم قطعا ، وكلَّ ما ليس بمعدوم فهو موجود قطعا. واعلم أن ما ذكرنا من الاعتراضات على التقسيم والأجوبة عنها ينبغي أن ينظر قبله هل صاحب التقسيم ناقل له عن غيره أو لا؟ فإن كان عن غيره ولم يلتزم صحته لم يتوجه إليه إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل عن المصدر الذي نقل عنه، من كتاب أو راو مثلاً . وإن كان جاء التقسيم من تلقاء نفسه أو نقله عن غيره والتزم صحته فإن كان في كلامه شيء غير واضح فللسائل الاستفسار عنه، وعليه البيان ،والإيضاح، وإن كانت الكلمات لا إجمال فيها ولا لبس ولا اشتراك فللسائل الاعتراض على التقسيم بالأوجه التي ذكرنا، ولخصمه الجواب عنها بالأجوبة التي بينا وإن عجز عن الجواب عن أحد الاعتراضات المذكورة فعليه الانتقال إلى تقسيم آخر، وإن وجد تقسيمه صحيحًا لا خلل فيه وجب عليه تسليمه والاعتراف بصحته، وهذا هو ترتيب المناظرة في التقسيم .
He invalidated the objection raised against his division by clarifying the intended meaning of the doctrinal school upon which his division was based. And we have explained elsewhere that the mentioned state is an imaginary illusion with no reality whatsoever, and that everything that does not exist is absolutely non-existent, and everything that is not non-existent absolutely exists. And know that regarding the objections we have mentioned on the division and the answers to them, one should first consider whether the author of the division is transmitting it from someone else or not. If it is from someone else and he did not commit to its correctness, then nothing is directed at him except demanding verification of the transmission from the source from which he transmitted, such as a book or a narrator. And if he came up with the division himself or transmitted it from someone else and committed to its correctness, then if there is something unclear in his speech, the questioner may seek clarification, and it is upon him to explain and clarify. And if the words contain no ambiguity, no obscurity, and no homonymy, then the questioner may object to the division using the methods we have mentioned, and his opponent may answer using the answers we have explained. And if he is unable to answer one of the mentioned objections, then he must move on to another division. And if he finds his division correct with no flaw, then he must submit to it and acknowledge its correctness. This is the order of debate concerning division.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from Adab al-Bahth outlines the protocol for objecting to a logical division (taqseem) during a debate. It begins by referencing a previous objection that was invalidated by clarifying the author's intended meaning. Then it makes a metaphysical side-note: everything is either existent or non-existent, with no third state. The core instruction follows: before objecting, determine whether the person presenting the division is merely transmitting it (like citing a book) or if he originated it himself or endorses its correctness. If he is just a transmitter without endorsement, the only valid objection is to question the accuracy of his transmission. If he is originator or committed to the division, then the questioner first asks for clarification of any ambiguous terms. If the terms are clear, the questioner can raise the standard objections (e.g., overlap, incompleteness), and the opponent must respond. If the opponent cannot answer, he must propose an alternative division. If the division is sound, the opponent must accept it. This procedure ensures orderly debate and prevents premature dismissal of valid divisions.

Key Terms
تقسيم
اعتراض
ناقل
إجمال
لبس
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a debater presents a division of acts into obligatory, recommended, and permissible, but it is clear he is only quoting Imam al-Ghazali without endorsing the list, what is the appropriate objection?
A) Object that the division is incomplete because it omits prohibited acts.
B) Demand that he verify the transmission from al-Ghazali's original text.
C) Ask him to clarify the meaning of 'recommended'.
D) Accept the division as it stands.

Key Takeaway
The legitimacy of an objection to a division depends first on whether the divider is a mere transmitter or an endorser, and then on whether the terms are clear—this ensures disciplined, fair debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Your opponent says the division is incomplete because there is also water that is pure but questionable (mashkūk). Following the rules from this passage, what should you do next? Taqsim and Sabr for Identifying the 'Illa
Author illustrates taqsim and sabr with the Bedouin example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply taqsim (division) and sabr (correct investigation) to isolate the effective cause ('illa) of a legal ruling.

Core Idea
Division narrows possibilities; investigation eliminates the false, leaving the true cause.

In usul al-fiqh and jadal, identifying the 'illa of a ruling requires systematic elimination of alternatives. This lesson uses the famous story of the Bedouin who had intercourse during Ramadan to demonstrate how taqsim and sabr work together to pinpoint the cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Start with the known ruling: expiation is freeing a slave. The question is: what attribute (illa) triggers this ruling?
2
List possible attributes (taqsim)
Enumerate all attributes that could plausibly be the cause: being a Bedouin, having a wife (not concubine), the act of intercourse during Ramadan.
The division should be exhaustive.
3
Eliminate by sabr
Test each attribute: is it present in all cases where the ruling applies? 'Being Bedouin' fails because a city dweller has the same ruling. 'Having a wife' fails because intercourse with a concubine also requires expiation.
Sabr checks whether the attribute is distinctive and causative.
4
Conclude the remaining attribute
After elimination, only 'intercourse during Ramadan' remains. Therefore, it is the cause.
The conclusion is a tasdiq, and the division is part of its proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Measured and not preserved nor stored, but edible, so taste is determined as the cause. We intend absolute example, not to discuss the proofs of opinions. Its examples, among them: the story of the Bedouin who came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said, 'I am ruined, I had intercourse with my wife during the day of Ramadan.' So he said to him, 'Free a slave.' We say: The cause of freeing the slave is either his being a Bedouin, or the woman being a wife not a concubine, or the intercourse during the day of Ramadan. Then we return to the categories by correct investigation and we find that his being a Bedouin is not valid for causal explanation because the ruling of intercourse of a city dweller in Ramadan is like the ruling of intercourse of a Bedouin. And the woman being a wife is not valid because the intercourse with his concubine during Ramadan is like the ruling of intercourse with his wife. So remaining is intercourse during Ramadan, so it is determined as the cause. So our statement 'the cause of expiation is intercourse' is an assent (tasdiq), and the division is part of its mentioned proof. And among its historical dialectical examples of creedal nature is the statement of Abdullah ibn Muhammad (al-Adhrami) to Ahmad ibn Abi Du'ad in the presence of al-Wathiq Billah, the summary of which is that this is the proof. The gist of what he said to him: 'This statement of yours which you call people to — i.e., that the Qur'an is created — either the Prophet and his rightly-guided caliphs knew about it or not. This is a correct rational division because it restricts to a thing and its negation. Then we return to the two mentioned categories by correct investigation and we find, O Ibn Abi Du'ad, that you are wrong in all possibilities. On the assumption that the Prophet and his rightly-guided caliphs were not aware of it, then you cannot be aware of it, and if so you are calling people to what you have no knowledge of, and this is wrong of you. On the assumption that they were aware of it and did not call people to it, then you, O Ibn Abi Du'ad, are bound in the nation of Muhammad by what bound him and his rightly-guided caliphs, so you are wrong in your call to something that the Prophet and his companions knew and they left calling people to it, because you are bound by what bound them.' We have expanded discussion on this story and mentioned that it is narrated from al-Khatib that al-Wathiq was present for this speech, and Ibn Abi Du'ad fell from his eye, and he released the Syrian sheikh, and after that no one was tested about the createdness of the Qur'an, and that this proof became the first source for curbing the rebellion of the statement of the createdness of the Qur'an. Whoever wants expanded discussion of this proof should look at our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan' on the discussion of the verse of Maryam to which we referred. And we have sufficed with these few words showing that division can be part of a proof for an assent (tasdiq).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage illustrates the method of division (taqsim) and correct investigation (sabr) as part of dialectical proof. It provides two examples: one from fiqh (the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan) and one from historical creedal debate (the createdness of the Qur'an). The author begins with a brief remark about taste as a cause (possibly from a different context) and clarifies that the purpose is merely to illustrate the method, not to argue about the merits of different legal opinions.

The first example: The Prophet (peace be upon him) told the Bedouin to free a slave as expiation. To identify the cause (the effective attribute, 'illa) for this ruling, one divides the possibilities: either it is because he is a Bedouin, or because he had intercourse with his wife (not a concubine), or because it was intercourse during Ramadan. Then one investigates each category. The Bedouin-attribute is eliminated because a city dweller in the same situation would have the same ruling. The wife-attribute is eliminated because intercourse with a concubine also requires expiation. Thus only 'intercourse during Ramadan' remains, so that is the cause. The author notes that the statement 'the cause of expiation is intercourse' is a tasdiq (assent, a proposition to be affirmed), and the division (taqsim) is part of its proof (dalil).

The second example is a historical dialectical argument from Abdullah ibn Muhammad (al-Adhrami) against Ahmad ibn Abi Du'ad, who claimed the Qur'an is created. The argument begins with a division: either the Prophet and the rightly-guided caliphs knew that the Qur'an is created, or they did not. This is a rational division (taqsim 'aqli) because it covers both possibilities (truth and its negation). Then the correct investigation (sabr) is applied: if they did not know, then Ibn Abi Du'ad cannot know it either, so he is calling to what he has no knowledge of, which is wrong. If they did know but did not call people to it, then the correct action is to follow their example and remain silent, so he is wrong by calling to it. Thus in either case, his position is wrong.

The author concludes by noting that this proof had a historical impact: al-Wathiq was convinced, Ibn Abi Du'ad fell out of favor, and the inquisition (mihna) over the createdness of the Qur'an was curtailed. The author refers to his own work 'Adwa' al-Bayan' for more details. The key point is that division (taqsim) can serve as part of a proof for a tasdiq, which is the main lesson for the student of dialectic.

السبر
العلة
تصديق

Taqsim and Sabr in Dialectical Proof of Creed
Author presents the historical dialectical argument on the createdness of the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a dialectical argument using taqsim (division) and sabr (correct investigation) to refute a theological position.

Core Idea
A correct rational division to a thing and its negation, combined with investigation, can prove an opponent wrong in all possibilities.

During the mihna (inquisition) over the createdness of the Qur'an, Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Adhrami famously refuted Ahmad ibn Abi Du'ad using taqsim and sabr. His argument so impressed Caliph al-Wathiq that it ended the persecution. This lesson analyzes that powerful dialectical proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The claim to be refuted: 'The Qur'an is created' (and people should believe this).
2
Propose a rational division (taqsim)
Divide the possibilities: either the Prophet and the rightly-guided caliphs knew this doctrine, or they did not. This is an exhaustive division (thing and its negation).
The division must cover all possibilities to be effective.
3
Apply sabr to the first possibility
If they did not know, then Ibn Abi Du'ad cannot know it either (since he is not a prophet). Therefore, he is calling people to what he has no knowledge of, which is wrong.
4
Apply sabr to the second possibility
If they did know but did not call people to it, then we must follow their example. Ibn Abi Du'ad is bound by what bound them, so his call is wrong.
5
Conclude that the opponent is wrong in all scenarios
Since both possibilities lead to the conclusion that Ibn Abi Du'ad is in error, his position is refuted.
This is a decisive refutation: it shows the opponent cannot be correct under any assumption.

Logical Division as a Proof Component in Dialectical Argument
Author refutes Ibn Abi Du'ad's claim by logical division and shows division's role in proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct an exhaustive logical division (taqsim) covering a thing and its negation, and apply it in a dialectical argument to refute a position.

Core Idea
Logical division is not just classification; it can be the backbone of a conclusive proof.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a powerful dialectical tool: logical division (taqsim). By dividing the possibilities exhaustively into two contradictory cases, he refutes Ibn Abi Du'ad's position on the createdness of the Quran. This shows that division is not merely a classification exercise but can serve as a component of a proof that yields assent (dalil tasdiqi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
The author divides the possibility into two: either the Prophet and the caliphs knew the truth about the Quran's status or they did not. This is a division into the thing and its negation.
The division is stated: 'إما أن يكون النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وخلفاؤه الراشدون عالمين بها أو لا'.
2
Verify exhaustiveness
The division is exhaustive because it covers the thing and its opposite (حصره في الشيء ونقيضه). There is no third possibility.
An exhaustive division is necessary for a valid proof.
3
Apply investigation (sabr)
The author examines each case: if they did not know, Ibn Abi Du'ad cannot know; if they knew but did not call to it, he should not call to it either.
Sabr here means testing each branch of the division.
4
Derive the conclusion
In both cases, Ibn Abi Du'ad is wrong. Thus the division leads to a refutation of his claim.
The conclusion forces the opponent into a contradiction.
5
Recognize division as proof component
The author explicitly states that this demonstrates division can be part of a tasdiqi proof (دليل تصديقي).
Division is not just a classification tool; it can yield certain assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومضمونه هو هذا الدليل فخلاصة ما قاله له مقالتك هذه التي تدعو الناس إليها ـ وهي كون القرآن مخلوقا - إما أن يكون النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وخلفاؤه الراشدون عالمين بها أو لا، وهذا تقسيم عقلي صحيح؛ لحصره في الشيء ونقيضه، ثم نرجع للقسمين المذكورين بالسبر الصحيح فنجدك يا ابن أبي دؤاد على غير الصواب في جميع الاحتمالات، فعلى تقدير أن النبي وخلفاءه الراشدين كانوا غير عالمين بها فلا يمكن أن تكون عالما بها، وإذا فأنت تدعو الناس لما لا علم لك به، وهذا غير صواب منك . وعلى تقدير أنهم كانوا عالمين بها ولم يدعوا الناس إليها فإنك يا ابن أبي دؤاد يسعك في أمة محمد ما وسعه هو وخلفاءه الراشدين، فأنت على غير صواب في دعائك إلى شيء علمه النبي وأصحابه وتركوا دعوة الناس له ؛ لأنك يسعك ما وسعهم . وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذه القصة، وذكرنا أنه روي عن الخطيب (1) أن الواثق بالله كان حاضرًا لهذا الكلام، وسقط من عينه ابن أبي دؤاد، وأطلق سراح الشيخ الشامي، ولم يمتحن بعد ذلك أحدًا بخلق القرآن وأن هذا الدليل صار هو أول مصدر لكبح جماح فتنة القول بخلق القرآن . ومن أراد بسط الكلام في هذا الدليل فلينظر كتابنا أضواء البيان، في الكلام على آية مريم التي أشرنا إليها . وقد اكتفينا بهذه الكلمات القليلة المبينة أن التقسيم قد يكون جزء دليل تصديقي.
And its content is this proof. So the summary of what he said to him: 'This statement of yours, by which you call people to — that the Quran is created — either the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his rightly guided successors knew it or not. This is a correct rational division because it confines it to the thing and its negation. Then we return to the two mentioned divisions by sound investigation and find you, O Ibn Abi Du'ad, wrong in all possibilities. So on the assumption that the Prophet and his rightly guided successors were not knowing it, then it is impossible for you to be knowing it. Thus you call people to what you have no knowledge of, and this is wrong of you. And on the assumption that they were knowing it and did not call people to it, then you, O Ibn Abi Du'ad, are allowed in the nation of Muhammad what was allowed for him and his rightly guided successors. So you are wrong in your call to something which the Prophet and his companions knew and left off calling people to, because you are allowed what they were allowed. And we have expanded the speech on this story and mentioned that it is narrated from al-Khatib that al-Wathiq billah was present for this speech, and Ibn Abi Du'ad fell from his eye, and he released the Syrian sheikh, and did not test anyone after that regarding the creation of the Quran, and that this proof became the first source to curb the rebellion of the fitna of the saying that the Quran is created. And whoever wants expanded speech on this proof, let him look at our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan' on the commentary on the verse of Maryam to which we referred. And we have sufficed with these few words indicating that division may be part of a tasdiqi proof.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an illustration of a dialectical argument (jadal) showing how logical division (تقسيم) functions as part of a proof (دليل تصديقي). The author recounts the historical debate over the createdness of the Quran. He presents a division: either the Prophet and the caliphs knew the truth about the Quran's status or they did not. This division is exhaustive (حصره في الشيء ونقيضه). Then, by sound investigation (سبر), the author examines both cases: if they were unaware, Ibn Abi Du'ad cannot know it either; if they were aware but did not call to it, then Ibn Abi Du'ad is not obliged to go beyond them. In either case, his position is invalid. Thus, the division leads to a decisive refutation. The author concludes that this demonstrates that division can be a component of a proof that yields assent (دليل تصديقي). All examples here are from Islamic theology and history.

Key Terms
تقسيم (taqsim): A rational division that exhaustively covers the thing and its negation, used as a method of proof.
دليل تصديقي (dalil tasdiqi): A proof that yields assent (tasdiq), i.e., a conclusive argument that establishes truth.
سبر (sabr): Investigation or testing of the possible cases in a division to determine the correct conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the story of Ibn Abi Du'ad demonstrate about logical division?
A) It is a type of definition.
B) It can be part of a proof that yields assent.
C) It is only used in rhetoric.
D) It is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Logical division, when exhaustive, can serve as a component of a demonstrative proof (dalil tasdiqi) in dialectical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a contested theological issue (e.g., whether divine attributes are eternal) and construct an exhaustive logical division (taqsim) that covers the thing and its negation. Then apply sabr to each case and derive a conclusion that refutes one side.
?
Identify the logical division in the following argument from Usul al-Fiqh: 'Either the ruling is derived from the text or from analogy. It is not derived from the text, so it must be from analogy.' Is this division exhaustive? Why or why not?

Conditions of Goodness (Husn) in Definitions: When an Objection is Valid
Author lists conditions of goodness (husn) for definitions and distinguishes them from validity (sihha).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conditions of goodness (husn) a definition fails to meet and determine whether the objection targets validity (fasid) or goodness (ghayr hasan).

Core Idea
A definition can be logically valid yet still defective if it fails linguistic clarity conditions.

In dialectical debate, an objection to a definition does not always claim invalidity; it may claim that the definition is not 'good' (hasan). The author lists specific verbal clarity conditions that make a definition good. Understanding these conditions helps you evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's basis
Determine whether the objection claims the definition is invalid (fasid) or not good (ghayr hasan). The author distinguishes between logical invalidity and lack of goodness.
Validity (sihha) concerns coextensiveness; goodness (husn) concerns clarity.
2
Check the goodness conditions
Which of the five mentioned conditions might be violated? (metaphorical/ambiguous/strange word, suggestive wording, verbal error).
Two conditions (metaphorical/ambiguous without clue) are considered conditions of validity by logicians.
3
Evaluate the seriousness
Assess if the flaw is merely a lack of goodness or a true validity issue. The author notes that metaphor without a clue is considered a validity condition by logicians.
4
Determine the appropriate response
If the objection is based on a goodness condition, the defender may need to clarify wording rather than revise the logical content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض الجدليين، فإنه يتوجه إليه الاعتراض من الجهات الأربع المذكورة التي يُعترض بها على الحقيقي والاسمي. وقد عرفت مما مر أن الاعتراض يتوجه إلى التعريف من حيث إنه غيرُ حسن وإن لم يكن فاسدا وذلك بتخلف شرط من شروط الحُسن المذكورة، ككونه مشتملاً على بعض الأغلاط اللفظية، وكأن يكون في ألفاظ التعريف لفظ مجازي دون قرينة تعيّن المراد، أو لفظ مشترك بين معنيين فأكثر دون قرينة تعيّن المقصود، وقد قدمنا أن هذين شرطان لصحته عند المنطقيين وهذا أظهر وكأن يكون في ألفاظ التعريف لفظ غريب غير ظاهر الدلالة على معناه المقصود، أو موهم لمعنى غيرِ المراد . وإذا عرفت أوجه الاعتراض على التعريف، فلا يخفى عليك أن الطريقة المعهودة في ذلك هي أن تجعل وجه الاعتراض مقدمة صغرى من دليل المعترض، وهو قياس اقتراني من الشكل الأول، وتضم إليها كبرى صورتها مثلاً : وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد، أو فهو غير حسن . كقولك : هذا التعريف الحقيقي غير مانع، وكل تعريف حقيقي كان كذلك فهو فاسد ينتج : هذا التعريف الحقيقي فاسد، وهو المراد، وهكذا. وكقولك : هذا التعريف في بعض ألفاظه غلط، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن ينتج من الشكل الأول: هذا التعريف غير حسن، وهكذا.
And some of the dialecticians, the objection is directed at him from the four aforementioned aspects by which objections are made to the real (definition) and the nominal. And you have known from what has passed that the objection is directed to the definition inasmuch as it is not good, even if not invalid, due to the absence of one of the conditions of goodness mentioned, such as it containing some verbal errors, or there being in the wording of the definition a metaphorical word without a clue specifying the intended meaning, or a word shared between two or more meanings without a clue specifying the intended meaning, and we have already stated that these two are conditions for its validity according to the logicians, and this is more evident. And (such as) there being in the wording of the definition a strange word not clearly indicating its intended meaning, or suggestive of a meaning other than the intended one. And if you have known the aspects of objection to the definition, it is not hidden from you that the customary method in that is to make the aspect of objection a minor premise of the objector's proof, which is a categorical syllogism in the first figure, and to attach to it a major premise whose form is, for example: 'Every definition that is thus is invalid', or 'thus is not good'. As you say: 'This real definition is non-exclusive, and every real definition that is thus is invalid', producing: 'This real definition is invalid', which is the intended. And likewise. And as you say: 'This definition has an error in some of its wording, and every definition that is thus is not good', producing from the first figure: 'This definition is not good'. And so on.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage explains how to formulate objections to definitions in a dialectical context. The author begins by referencing that some dialecticians (al-jadaliyyin) are subject to objection from the four aspects (mentioned earlier) that apply to both real (haqiqi) and nominal (ismi) definitions. Then the author clarifies that an objection can target a definition for not being 'good' (hasan) even if it is not invalid (fasid), due to failure to meet certain conditions of 'husn' (goodness). These conditions include: (1) absence of verbal errors (aghlat lafziyya), (2) no metaphorical word (lafz majazi) without a clarifying clue (qarina), (3) no ambiguous word (lafz mushtarak) without a clue, (4) no strange word (lafz gharib) unclear in meaning, and (5) no word suggestive of unintended meaning (muhim li-ma'na ghayr al-murad). The author notes that conditions (2) and (3) are considered conditions of validity (sihha) by logicians, and are more apparent than the others. Next, the author explains the method of formalizing an objection: take the aspect of objection (wajh al-i'tirad) as the minor premise (muqaddima sughra) of a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in the first figure (shakl awwal), and attach a suitable major premise (kubra) such as 'Every definition that is thus is invalid' or 'is not good'. Two examples are given: (a) 'This real definition is non-exclusive (ghayr mani')' and 'Every real definition that is non-exclusive is invalid' yields 'This real definition is invalid'. (b) 'This definition has an error in its wording' and 'Every definition with an error is not good' yields 'This definition is not good'. This demonstrates how dialectical objections are systematically reduced to syllogistic form.

Key Terms
الاعتراض
الحسن
الفاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dialectical method described, if a definition uses a metaphorical word without a clarifying clue, what type of objection is that?
A) The definition is invalid (fasid) because it fails to be exclusive (mani').
B) The definition is not good (ghayr hasan) because it violates a condition of linguistic clarity.
C) The definition is invalid because it is not comprehensive (jami').
D) The definition is not good because it contains a logical contradiction.

Key Takeaway
A definition can be rejected as 'not good' (ghayr hasan) for linguistic flaws even if it is logically valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition 'Salah is a set of utterances and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' – identify if there is any violation of the goodness conditions mentioned in the passage. State whether the objection would be on grounds of invalid (fasid) or not good (ghayr hasan).

Structuring Objections as Categorical Syllogisms in the First Figure
Author explains the method of formalizing objections as first-figure categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure that represents a dialectical objection to a definition.

Core Idea
Every objection to a definition can be expressed as a first-figure categorical syllogism.

The author provides a method to formalize objections: take the specific flaw as the minor premise and a general rule as the major premise. This ensures the objection is logically structured. Mastering this technique allows you to present objections clearly in debate.

Guided Path
1
Extract the specific flaw (wajh al-i'tirad)
Identify the exact defect in the definition, e.g., it is non-exclusive (ghayr mani') or contains a metaphorical word without a clue.
2
Formulate the minor premise
State the flaw as a proposition about the definition under debate, e.g., 'This real definition is non-exclusive'.
The minor premise is the specific observation about the definition.
3
Formulate the major premise
State a general rule that connects the flaw to a verdict, e.g., 'Every real definition that is non-exclusive is invalid'.
The major premise is a universal statement provided by the objector.
4
Combine in first figure
Ensure the middle term (the flaw) is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. This is the first figure.
The first figure is the most straightforward and yields a direct conclusion.
5
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion follows necessarily: the definition is invalid or not good, as desired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And some of the dialecticians, the objection is directed at him from the four aforementioned aspects by which objections are made to the real (definition) and the nominal. And you have known from what has passed that the objection is directed to the definition inasmuch as it is not good, even if not invalid, due to the absence of one of the conditions of goodness mentioned, such as it containing some verbal errors, or there being in the wording of the definition a metaphorical word without a clue specifying the intended meaning, or a word shared between two or more meanings without a clue specifying the intended meaning, and we have already stated that these two are conditions for its validity according to the logicians, and this is more evident. And (such as) there being in the wording of the definition a strange word not clearly indicating its intended meaning, or suggestive of a meaning other than the intended one. And if you have known the aspects of objection to the definition, it is not hidden from you that the customary method in that is to make the aspect of objection a minor premise of the objector's proof, which is a categorical syllogism in the first figure, and to attach to it a major premise whose form is, for example: 'Every definition that is thus is invalid', or 'thus is not good'. As you say: 'This real definition is non-exclusive, and every real definition that is thus is invalid', producing: 'This real definition is invalid', which is the intended. And likewise. And as you say: 'This definition has an error in some of its wording, and every definition that is thus is not good', producing from the first figure: 'This definition is not good'. And so on.

Procedure of Disputation in Definitions
Author outlines the proper sequence for disputing a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the procedural steps of disputing a definition in Islamic dialectics and apply them to a sample definition from fiqh or usul.

Core Idea
Disputing a definition follows a strict order: first check transmission, then clarity, then soundness.

When two scholars debate a definition, they cannot simply object randomly. There is a fixed procedure that ensures fairness and rigour. The author here, drawing on the structure already explained for division (taqsim), spells out exactly what steps the disputants must follow. Understanding this sequence is crucial for participating in or evaluating any formal dialectical exchange (munazara) on definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the status of the definition
First, ask: Is the proponent quoting this definition from someone else (naqil) or originating it himself? This determines the next step.
The source distinguishes between transmitting and authoring.
2
Check commitment to correctness
If the definition is quoted, does the proponent commit to its correctness (multazim sihhatihi) or not? If he is just quoting without commitment, the only valid demand is to correct the transmission; no substantive objection can be made yet.
If he commits or is the author, proceed to wording.
3
Examine clarity of wording
Look at every term in the definition. If any word is unclear (ghayr wadih al-ma'na), the opponent must request clarification (istifsar) before any other objection. The proponent must then explain.
The author gives an example: 'al-zakhikh' needs clarification.
4
Evaluate freedom from objections
Once all terms are clear, check if the definition is free from all aspects of objection (wujuh al-i'tirad). If it is, it must be accepted (taslim). If there is a flaw, object using one of the established objection types.
These objection types are explained earlier in the text.
5
Respond to objections
If an objection is raised, the proponent must answer with one of the established answers (ajwiba muqarrara) in this art. The exchange follows fixed rules until resolution.
This ensures the debate remains disciplined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن ترتيب المناظرة في التعريف كترتيبها في التقسيم، وقد أوضحناه في الكلام على التقسيم ، و به تعلم أن أول ما يبدأ به المناظر في التعريف هو أن ينظر في كلام خصمه هل هو ناقل له عن غيره أو لا؟ وإن كان ناقلاً فهل هو ملتزم صحته أو لا . فإن كان ناقلا ولم يلتزم الصحة فلا يتوجه إليه إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل، وإن كان ملتزما صحة ما نقل أو غير ناقل عن غيره بل هو آت بالتعريف من تلقاء نفسه فإن كان في ألفاظ تعريفه لفظ غير واضح المعنى استفسر عنه وعليه البيان . كأن يعرف موجِبَ القصاص في النفس فيقول : هو القتل بما يقتل غالبًا كالزخيخ، فلخصمه أن يقول : وما مرادك بالزخيخ؟ وعليه أن يبين أن مراده به النار . وإن كانت ألفاظ تعريفه واضحة المعاني فإن كانت سالمة من أوجه الاعتراض وجب تسليمها وقبولها وإن كان فيها خلل اعترض عليه بأحد أوجه الاعتراض التي بيّنا وعليه أن يجيب عن الاعتراض عليه بأحد الأجوبة المقررة في هذا الفن.
And know that the order of disputation in the definition is like its order in division, and we have clarified it in the discussion on division. By it you know that the first thing that the disputant begins with in the definition is to look at the speech of his opponent: is he transmitting it from another or not? If he is transmitting, then does he commit to its correctness or not? If he is transmitting and does not commit to correctness, then nothing is directed at him except demanding correction of the transmission. And if he commits to the correctness of what he transmitted, or he is not transmitting from another but rather produces the definition on his own, then if in the wording of his definition there is a word whose meaning is not clear, he is asked for clarification (istifsar) and the explanation is upon him. As if he defines 'the cause of retribution in life' (mujib al-qisas fi al-nafs) and says: 'It is killing by that which kills mostly, like al-zakhikh.' Then his opponent may say: 'What do you mean by al-zakhikh?' And he must clarify that he means fire. And if the wording of his definition is clear in meaning, then if it is free from the aspects of objection, it must be accepted and admitted; if there is a flaw in it, he is objected to by one of the aspects of objection that we have explained, and he must answer the objection with one of the established answers in this art.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage explains the procedural steps (tartib al-munazara) for disputing a definition. The author begins by stating that the order in definition is like that in division (taqsim), which was already clarified. Then the first step (awwal ma yabda' bihi al-munazir) is to examine the opponent's statement: is the definition quoted (naqil) from someone else or not? If it is quoted, we must check whether the opponent commits to its correctness (multazim sihhatihi) or not. If he is simply quoting without commitment, then the only valid demand is to correct the transmission (tasih al-naql). If he commits to its correctness or produces the definition independently (min tilqa'i nafsihi), we proceed to examine the wording. If any word is unclear (ghayr wadih al-ma'na), the opponent requests clarification (istifsar) and the proponent must explain (bayan). An example is given: defining 'mujib al-qisas fi al-nafs' (the cause of retribution in life) as 'al-qatl bima yaqtul ghaliban ka-al-zakhikh' (killing by that which kills mostly, like al-zakhikh). The opponent can ask: 'What do you mean by al-zakhikh?' The proponent must explain that he means fire (nar). If the wording is clear, then we check if it is free from all aspects of objection (wujuh al-i'tirad). If it is, it must be accepted (taslim) and admitted (qabul). If there is a flaw, the opponent objects using one of the previously explained objection types, and the proponent must answer with one of the established answers (ajwiba muqarrara) in this art.

Key Terms
المناظرة
الناقل
الاستفسار
البيان
التسليم
الاعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the procedure described, what should the opponent do if the proponent defines 'mujib al-qisas fi al-nafs' as 'al-qatl bima yaqtul ghaliban ka-al-zakhikh' and the meaning of 'al-zakhikh' is unclear?
A) Immediately object that the definition is not good (hasan) because it contains an obscure word.
B) Request clarification by asking 'What do you mean by al-zakhikh?' and wait for the proponent to explain.
C) Demand that the proponent provide a different definition altogether.
D) Accept the definition because the overall meaning is clear enough to proceed.

Key Takeaway
The procedure for disputing a definition ensures that debates are orderly and fair: first address the source of the definition, then clarify unclear terms, and only then evaluate its soundness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is the proponent committed to its correctness? Are any terms unclear (e.g., 'excess' or 'similar')? If clear, check it against the aspects of objection (e.g., it may be too narrow if it excludes hidden riba). Construct the debate exchange accordingly.

Responding to Objections of Lack of Comprehensiveness and Exclusiveness
Author explains how to respond to objections of lack of comprehensiveness or exclusiveness in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a response (tahrir al-murad) to an objection against a definition for lacking comprehensiveness or exclusiveness.

Core Idea
Clarify the intended scope: the definer's meaning beats the objector's literal objection.

In dialectical debates, definitions are often attacked for not covering all cases (lack of jam') or for including unwanted cases (lack of man'). The author teaches a powerful tool: tahrir al-murad min al-mu'arraf—clarifying the intended meaning from the definer. By showing that the definition targets only predominant or typical instances, the definer can neutralize the objection without changing the definition. This lesson walks through two real juristic examples to practice this technique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Determine if the objection is about comprehensiveness (jam') or exclusiveness (man'). In the first example, the objection is lack of comprehensiveness: the definition does not include all cases of semen exit. In the second, it is lack of exclusiveness: the definition includes elephants, which should be excluded.
2
Locate the definer's intended scope
Look for clues in the definer's statement about predominant usage or typical cases. The definer often intends only common or frequent instances, not rare outliers.
The definer uses phrases like 'the intended meaning' or 'the predominant type.'
3
Formulate the tahrir al-murad
State explicitly that the definition is meant to cover only the common or intended cases, not rare exceptions. For example: 'I intended by water the semen that exits predominantly, not the rare case.'
This response does not change the definition but clarifies its intended interpretation.
4
Apply the response to a new example
Practice with a similar definition from fiqh. For instance, consider the definition of 'traveler' for shortening prayer. Formulate a tahrir al-murad response to an objection of lack of exclusiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أجوبة صاحب التعريف عن أوجه الاعتراض الأربعة المذكورة، التي يعترض بها عندهم على التقسيم الحقيقي والاسمي، حدًا كان كل واحد منهما أو رسما ، تاما كان كل واحد منهما أو ناقصاً.
أما الاعتراض على التعريف بعدم الطرد أو العكس - أعني بهما الجمع والمنع كما أوضحناه مرارا - فالجواب عنه بتحرير المراد، وهو أربعة أنواع : الأول : تحرير المراد من المعرّف - بالفتح ، ومثاله أن تعرف الماء المستوجب للماء في حديث إنما الماء من الماء بأنه المني الخارج بلذة معتادة .
فيقول المستدل هذا التعريف غير منعكس، أعني غير جامع لجميع أقسام المعرَّف ؛ لأنه لم يشمل خروج المني بغير لذة معتادة، كخروجه بسبب لدغ عقرب له في ذكره، وكخروجه بسبب نزوله في ماء حار، أو هزة دابة له، ونحو ذلك، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد .
فيجيب صاحب التعريف فيقول : أمنع قولك: إن هذا التعريف غير جامع ؛ لأن المراد بالماء في التعريف المني الغالب نزوله، ولم يرد به النادر الذي هو الخارج بدون لذة معتادة، وهذا على قول من يقول : إن الغسل لا يجب من خروج المني إلا إذا كان خارجا بلذة معتادة كمالك وأصحابه ومن وافقهم.
ومثاله في عدم المنع أن يعرف ذا الخف الذي تجوز المسابقة عليه بجعل بأنه الإبل خاصة .
فيقول خصمه : هذا التعريف غير مانع ؛ لأن ذا الخف يدخل فيه الفيل، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد .
فيقول صاحب التعريف : أمنع قولك : إنه غير مانع ؛ لأني أردت بذي الخف النوع الغالب المتعارف ، ولم أرد الفرد النادر الذي يندر ركوبه ولا يكاد يخطر بالبال وهو الفيل .
واعلم أن مثالنا هذا لا يعترض بدعوى كون هذه التعريفات من قبيل اللفظي، وأكثرهم لا يشترطون فيه الجمع والمنع؛ لأنا نقول : هي من قبيل التعريف بالرسم ؛ لأنها عرّفت بخواص لتلك الأشياء، ولو سلمنا أنها لفظية، فالمثال يكفي فيه وروده على قول ولو غير قوي، بل يكفي فيه الفرض والاحتمال كما هو معروف، قال صاحب مراقي السعود: والشأن لا يُعترض المثال إذ قد كفى الفرض والاحتمال
وكلا المثالين المذكورين جواب بتحرير المراد من المعرف بالفتح ، الأول عن عدم الجمع والثاني عن عدم المنع ، وضابط تحرير المراد من المعرّف - بالفتح - هو تفسير صاحب التعريف الحقيقة
Chapter on the responses of the author of the definition to the four mentioned aspects of objection, which are used to object to real and nominal divisions, whether each is a definition or description, complete or incomplete.
As for the objection to the definition by lack of comprehensiveness or exclusiveness - meaning by them comprehensiveness and exclusiveness as we have clarified repeatedly - the response to it is by clarifying the intended meaning. It is of four types: The first: clarifying the intended meaning from the definer (with fatha). Its example is that you define the water that necessitates water in the hadith 'Verily water is from water' as the semen that exits with customary pleasure.
Then the objector says: This definition is not exclusive, meaning not comprehensive of all parts of the defined, because it does not include the exit of semen without customary pleasure, like its exit due to a scorpion sting on his penis, or its exit due to descending into hot water, or a riding animal's jolt, and so on. Every definition that is like this is corrupt.
Then the author of the definition responds: I deny your statement that this definition is not comprehensive, because the intended meaning of 'water' in the definition is the semen that exits predominantly, and it does not intend the rare one that exits without customary pleasure. This is according to the one who says that the ritual bath is not obligatory from the exit of semen except if it exits with customary pleasure, like Malik and his companions and those who agree with them.
Its example regarding lack of exclusiveness is that you define the animal with a hoof that is permissible to race with a stake as specifically camels.
Then his opponent says: This definition is not exclusive, because the animal with a hoof includes the elephant, and every definition like this is corrupt.
Then the author of the definition says: I deny your statement that it is not exclusive, because I intended by 'animal with a hoof' the predominant and customary type, and I did not intend the rare individual that is rarely ridden and hardly crosses the mind, which is the elephant.
Know that this example of ours is not objected to by claiming that these definitions are of the verbal type, and most of them do not require comprehensiveness and exclusiveness in it; because we say: They are of the type of definition by description, because they defined by characteristics for those things. And even if we concede that they are verbal, the example suffices by its occurrence on a view, even if weak; rather, supposition and possibility suffice as is known. The author of Maraqi al-Sa'ud said: 'The matter is not objected to by the example, for supposition and possibility suffice.'
Both the mentioned examples are responses by clarifying the intended meaning from the definer (with fatha): the first about lack of comprehensiveness and the second about lack of exclusiveness. The rule for clarifying the intended meaning from the definer (with fatha) is: the author of the definition's interpretation of the reality.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of how to respond to two specific objections against definitions: lack of comprehensiveness (al-jam') and lack of exclusiveness (al-man'). The author teaches that the proper response is 'tahrir al-murad min al-mu'arraf'—clarifying the intended meaning from the definer (the person giving the definition). He gives two examples from Islamic jurisprudence. The first example concerns the definition of 'water' in the hadith 'Verily water is from water' (meaning that ritual ablution is required when seminal fluid is discharged). The definer defines it as semen that exits with customary pleasure. The objector says the definition is not comprehensive because it excludes rare cases like semen exiting due to a scorpion sting. The defender responds by clarifying that the definition intends only the predominant type of semen, not the rare outlier. This aligns with the juristic view of Imam Malik and others that ritual bath is only obligatory when pleasure is present. The second example concerns defining 'animal with a hoof' for the purpose of racing by wager. The definer says it means specifically camels. The objector says it is not exclusive because the definition includes elephants. The defender responds by clarifying that he meant the common and well-known type (camels), not the rare elephant. The author then notes that these definitions are not purely verbal (lafzi) but are definitions by description (rasm), because they define by characteristics. Even if one considers them verbal, the examples are still valid as hypothetical cases, as stated by the author of Maraqi al-Sa'ud. The lesson shows that context and intended scope are crucial in dialectical defense of definitions.

Key Takeaway
Tahrir al-murad is a key dialectical tool to defend definitions by specifying the intended scope, usually the predominant cases, against objections of lack of comprehensiveness or exclusiveness.

Defending Definitions: Tahrir al-Murad from the Defined Term
Author illustrates tahrir al-murad to answer objection of lack of exclusivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of tahrir al-murad from the defined term to defend a definition against the objection of lack of exclusivity.

Core Idea
When a definition is attacked for lacking exclusivity, restrict the term's meaning to the common type.

In dialectical practice, a definition must be both comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'). An opponent may object that the definition includes things outside the intended category (lack of exclusivity). The definer can respond by clarifying his intended meaning of the defined term—this is tahrir al-murad from the defined term. In this lesson, we explore a classic example: defining 'possessor of a hoof' (dhu al-khuff) as camels. The opponent objects that elephants also have hooves. How does the definer defend? Let's see.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and the objection
Read the example: someone defines 'dhu al-khuff' (upon which racing with a stake is permissible) as 'camels specifically.' The opponent says this definition is not exclusive because 'dhu al-khuff' also includes the elephant. Notice that the opponent uses a rare case to challenge the definition.
The objection targets exclusivity (al-man').
2
Examine the definer's response
The definer responds by denying the objection and clarifying his intended meaning: 'By dhu al-khuff, I meant the common, well-known type—camels—not the rare individual (the elephant).' This is tahrir al-murad: the definer restricts the meaning of the defined term to what is typical.
The response does not change the definition but explains the intended scope.
3
Understand the logical basis
The response is valid because definitions in such contexts are based on common usage, not rare exceptions. The author notes that even if the definition were verbal (lafzi), the example suffices as a hypothetical case. The key is that the definer's intended meaning makes the definition equivalent to the definiendum.
The author cites Maraqi al-Su'ud: hypothesis and possibility suffice in examples.
4
Formulate the general rule
The rule (dabit) is: the definer interprets the reality being defined by a meaning he intends—either more general or more specific than the apparent meaning—so that the defined term (al-mu'arraf) becomes equivalent to the definition (al-mu'arif).
This is one type of tahrir al-murad: from the defined term itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله في عدم المنع أن يعرف ذا الخف الذي تجوز المسابقة عليه بجعل بأنه الإبل خاصة . فيقول خصمه : هذا التعريف غير مانع ؛ لأن ذا الخف يدخل فيه الفيل، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد . فيقول صاحب التعريف : أمنع قولك : إنه غير مانع ؛ لأني أردت بذي الخف النوع الغالب المتعارف ، ولم أرد الفرد النادر الذي يندر ركوبه ولا يكاد يخطر بالبال وهو الفيل . واعلم أن مثالنا هذا لا يعترض بدعوى كون هذه التعريفات من قبيل اللفظي، وأكثرهم لا يشترطون فيه الجمع والمنع؛ لأنا نقول : هي من قبيل التعريف بالرسم ؛ لأنها عرّفت بخواص لتلك الأشياء، ولو سلمنا أنها لفظية، فالمثال يكفي فيه وروده على قول ولو غير قوي، بل يكفي فيه الفرض والاحتمال كما هو معروف، قال صاحب مراقي السعود والشان لا يُعترض المثال إذ قد كفى الفرض والاحتمال وكلا المثالين المذكورين جواب بتحرير المراد من المعرف بالفتح ، الأول عن عدم الجمع والثاني عن عدم المنع ، وضابط تحرير المراد من المعرّف - بالفتح - هو تفسير صاحب التعريف الحقيقة المعرفة بمعنى يقصده هو أعم أو أخص من المعنى المتبادر منها؛ ليكون بذلك المعرَّف - بالفتح - مساويا للمعرف ـ بالكسر - .
And an example of it in lack of exclusivity is that one defines 'possessor of a hoof' upon which racing for a stake is permissible as being specifically camels. Then his opponent says: 'This definition is not exclusive because the elephant enters into 'possessor of a hoof', and every definition that is like that is invalid.' Then the definer says: 'I deny your statement that it is not exclusive, because I intended by 'possessor of a hoof' the common, well-known type, and I did not intend the rare individual whose riding is rare and hardly occurs to the mind, which is the elephant.' And know that this example of ours is not objectionable by the claim that these definitions are of the verbal type, and most of them do not require comprehensiveness and exclusivity; because we say: they are of the type of definition by description (rasm), because they defined by specific properties of those things. And if we concede that they are verbal, the example suffices in its occurrence upon a view even if not strong; rather, hypothesis and possibility suffice, as is well-known. The author of Maraqi al-Su'ud said: 'And the matter is not that the example is objected to, since hypothesis and possibility suffice.' And both of the mentioned two examples are a response by clarifying the intended meaning from the defined term (with fatha), the first regarding lack of comprehensiveness and the second regarding lack of exclusivity. And the rule for clarifying the intended meaning from the defined term (with fatha) is the definer's interpretation of the reality being defined by a meaning he intends that is more general or more specific than the apparent meaning from it, so that by that the defined (with fatha) is equivalent to the definition (with kasra).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of how a definer can defend his definition against the objection of lack of exclusivity (adam al-man') by clarifying his intended meaning (tahrir al-murad) from the defined term itself. The author provides an example: someone defines 'possessor of a hoof' (dhu al-khuff) upon which racing with a stake is permissible as 'camels specifically.' The opponent objects that this definition is not exclusive because 'possessor of a hoof' includes the elephant, which is not a camel, so the definition would wrongly include elephants. The definer responds by stating that he intended by 'possessor of a hoof' the common, well-known type — i.e., camels — and not the rare individual like the elephant, which is seldom ridden and rarely comes to mind. This is an example of tahrir al-murad: the definer specifies that his intended meaning is narrower than the literal apparent meaning. The author then addresses a potential objection: that such definitions might be considered merely verbal (lafzi) and not subject to conditions of comprehensiveness and exclusivity. He responds that these are definitions by description (rasm), not verbal definitions, but even if they were verbal, the example is sufficient because it is based on a valid opinion, even if weak, and hypothesis and possibility suffice. He cites a verse from Maraqi al-Su'ud affirming that examples are not invalidated merely by being hypothetical. Finally, he states the rule (dabit) for this type of tahrir al-murad: the definer interprets the thing being defined by a meaning that he intends, which is either more general or more specific than the apparent meaning, so that the defined term becomes equivalent to the definition. This is the first type: clarifying the intended meaning from the defined term itself.

المعرَّف
المعرف

Key Takeaway
Tahrir al-murad from the defined term allows a definer to restrict the intended meaning to the common type, thereby saving a definition from the objection of lack of exclusivity.
