Tahrir al-Murad from Parts of the Definition
Author explains the second type of tahrir al-murad and gives examples for lack of comprehensiveness and exclusivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the second type of tahrir al-murad to rescue a definition from objections of non-comprehensiveness or non-exclusivity.

Core Idea
Reinterpret a term in the definition to make it include everything it should and exclude what it shouldn't.

In dialectical exchanges, a definition faces two classic attacks: it either fails to include all members of the definiendum (adam al-jam') or fails to exclude non-members (adam al-man'). The second type of tahrir al-murad offers a powerful response: the definer clarifies that a particular term in the definition was meant in a specific sense that achieves equivalence. This lesson walks through the two examples from the text, showing how reinterpretation can salvage definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Determine whether the opponent claims the definition is not comprehensive (lacks inclusivity) or not exclusive (allows outsiders). In the first example, the objection is 'non-comprehensive'; in the second, it is 'non-exclusive'.
2
Locate the problematic term
Find which part of the definition the opponent argues causes the failure. In the animal example, it is 'thinking' (al-mufakkir); in the human example, it is 'having no feathers' (la rish lahu).
3
Formulate the reinterpretation
Propose a meaning for that term that is either broader (to include more) or narrower (to exclude more) than the apparent meaning. For 'thinking', the definer reinterprets it as 'moving by will' (al-mutaharrik bi-l-irada), which is broader and includes all animals.
The reinterpretation must be plausible within the context of the debate.
4
Test the revised definition
Check if the new interpretation makes the definition both comprehensive and exclusive. With 'moving by will', horses and mules are included, and non-animals are still excluded, so the definition becomes comprehensive.
5
Assess the dialectical strength
Evaluate whether the reinterpretation is a legitimate move in the dialectic. The definer is allowed to clarify his intended meaning, as long as it is not ad hoc to the point of absurdity. The opponent may then challenge the plausibility of the reinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف، وضابطه تفسير صاحب التعريف بعض أجزائه بمعنى يقصده هو أعم أو أخص من المعنى المتبادر منه ؛ ليكون المعرّف والمعرف متساويين . ومثاله في الاعتراض بعدم الجمع : تعريف الحيوان بأنه (الجسم النامي الحساس المفكر ) . فيقول :المستدل : هذا التعريف غير جامع ؛ لأن قيد المفكر مخرج غير المفكر، كالفرس والبغل مثلاً مع دخولهما في الحيوان، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو فاسد . فيقول صاحب التعريف: أمنع قولك: إن هذا التعريف غيرُ جامع، ويفسر المفكر بأن مراده به المتحرك بالإرادة؛ لأنه فكر في الحركة وأرادها ،ففعلها، والمفكر على هذا التفسير شامل لجميع أنواع الحيوان، فهو جواب عن عدم الجمع بتحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف . ومثال الجواب عن عدم المنع بتحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف أن يقال في تعريف الإنسان : هو (حيوان منتصب القامة يمشي على اثنين لا ريش له . فيقول المستدل : هذا التعريف غيرُ مانع ؛ لأن الديك مثلاً إذا نُزع ريشه كان حيوانًا منتصب القامة يمشي على اثنين لا ريش له، وكل
The second type: clarifying the intended meaning from some parts of the definition. Its rule is: the definer's interpretation of some of its parts by a meaning he intends that is more general or more specific than the apparent meaning from it, so that the definiendum and definition are equivalent. An example of it in the objection of lack of comprehensiveness: the definition of animal as 'a growing, sentient, thinking body'. The arguer says: 'This definition is not comprehensive because the condition 'thinking' excludes the non-thinking, like the horse and the mule for example, while they are included in animal, and every definition like that is invalid.' The definer says: 'I deny your statement that this definition is not comprehensive,' and he interprets 'thinking' as meaning by it 'that which moves by will,' because it thinks about motion and wills it, then does it, and 'thinking' in this interpretation includes all types of animal. So it is an answer to lack of comprehensiveness by clarifying the intended meaning from some parts of the definition. And an example of the answer to lack of exclusivity by clarifying the intended meaning from some parts of the definition is that it is said in the definition of human: 'an animal of upright stature, walking on two, having no feathers.' The arguer says: 'This definition is not exclusive because a rooster, for example, if its feathers are removed, is an animal of upright stature walking on two without feathers, and every...'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of tahrir al-murad: clarifying the intended meaning from some parts of the definition (tahrir al-murad min ba'd ajza' al-ta'rif). The rule is that the definer interprets a part of the definition by a meaning he intends that is either more general or more specific than the apparent meaning, so that the definiendum and the definition become equivalent. Two examples are given: one for lack of comprehensiveness (adam al-jam') and one for lack of exclusivity (adam al-man'). In the first example, someone defines 'animal' as 'a growing, sentient, thinking body'. The opponent objects that the condition 'thinking' excludes non-thinking animals like horses and mules, so the definition is not comprehensive. The definer responds by interpreting 'thinking' (mufakkir) as 'that which moves by will' (al-mutaharrik bi-l-irada). He argues that such animals think about motion and will it, so they are included. This reinterpretation makes 'thinking' cover all animals, thus answering the objection. In the second example, someone defines 'human' as 'an animal of upright stature, walking on two, having no feathers.' The opponent objects that a rooster with its feathers removed fits this description, so the definition is not exclusive. The text cuts off before the definer's response, but the implied answer would be to reinterpret one of the conditions, perhaps 'no feathers' as meaning by nature, or 'upright stature' in a way that excludes birds. The passage illustrates how tahrir al-murad from parts of the definition can salvage a definition from both types of objections.

Key Terms
تحرير المراد من بعض أجزاء التعريف
المفكر
الجامع
المانع
المستدل
صاحب التعريف

Checkpoint

A) He denies that horses and mules are animals.
B) He removes the condition 'thinking' from the definition.
C) He reinterprets 'thinking' as 'moving by will', which includes all animals.
D) He argues that the definition is verbal and not subject to conditions.

Key Takeaway
Tahrir al-murad from parts of a definition allows the definer to reinterpret a term in a way that makes the definition equivalent to the definiendum, answering objections of non-comprehensiveness or non-exclusivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'human' as 'a rational animal'. An opponent objects that this definition is not exclusive because angels are also rational. Propose a tahrir al-murad response by reinterpreting 'rational' (or another term) to exclude angels.
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, the definition of 'qiyas' is 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared 'illah'. An opponent might object that this definition includes istihsan. Use tahrir al-murad to defend the definition.

Defending Definitions Against Dialectical Objections
Objections to definitions and their dialectical responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three common objections to definitions (ghalat, majaz, mushtarak) and produce appropriate responses as per the jadal tradition.

Core Idea
Violations of definition conditions can be defended with pre-arranged dialectical answers.

In dialectical debates about definitions, objectors often attack the wording. This passage equips you with standard responses to three common charges: grammatical error, metaphor without clarification, and ambiguity. These responses are not ad hoc but follow a structured method of denial, contextualization, or concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Determine whether the objector is accusing the definition of ghalat (grammatical error), majaz (metaphor without qarina), or ishtirak (ambiguity without qarina).
Each type has a fixed number of responses: two for ghalat, two for majaz, three for ishtirak.
2
Recall the standard answers
For ghalat: deny the error by citing a grammatical school, or concede but argue it does not affect definitional correctness. For majaz: claim the term has become a customary literal meaning (haqiqa 'urfiyya) or assert a qarina exists. For ishtirak: claim customary literal meaning, or concede that ambiguity is only a problem if all meanings cannot be intended (or similar).
The passage gives the first two answers explicitly; the third for ishtirak is cut off but typically involves context or purpose.
3
Apply the defense strategy
Choose the appropriate answer based on the objection. If you deny the flaw, provide a justification (e.g., a grammatical school). If you concede, explain why the flaw is harmless.
The goal is to block the objection without undermining the definition.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Take a real definition from fiqh or usul (e.g., 'qiyas') and consider how an objector might use one of these charges and how you would respond.
This prepares you for actual munazara scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الاعتراض على التعريف إن كان من جهة اختلال بعض شروط حسنه فله أجوبة أخرى غير ما ذكرنا : فإن قال المعترض : هذا التعريف فيه لفظة كذا وهي غلط، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن فلصاحب التعريف عن ذلك جوابان : الأول : منع كون ذلك اللفظ غلطا ؛ لجريانه على بعض المذاهب النحوية . والثاني : القول بموجبه ؛ بأن يقول : سلمنا أن ذلك اللفظ غلط، ولكنه لا تتوقف عليه صحة التعريف، وأنا لا يلزمني إلا صحة التعريف، فلم أعتد إلا بالذي لابد منه، وهو صحة تعريفي. وإن اعترض على التعريف بأن فيه لفظة كذا يراد بها كذا وهو معنى مجازي وليس ثَمّ قرينةٌ تعيّن المراد وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن فلصاحب التعريف عن ذلك جوابان أيضًا : الأول : أن يدعي أن ذلك اللفظ المجازي صار حقيقة عرفية في المعنى المقصود. والثاني: أن يدعي أن ثَم قرينةً تبيّن المراد، ولكن المعترض غفل عنها . وإن كان قال : هذا التعريف مشتمل على لفظ كذا، وهو مشترك ؛ لوضعه لمعاني متعددة وليس هناك قرينة تعين المراد، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن فلصاحب التعريف عن ذلك ثلاثة أجوبة : الأول : أن يجيب بأنّ اللفظ المدعى أنه مشترك قد صار حقيقة عرفية في المعنى الذي يقصده دون غيره من المعاني . والثاني : أن يقول بموجبه ، فيقول : سلّمنا أنه مشترك، ولكن عدم حسن استعمال المشترك في التعريف محله فيما لم تصح فيه إرادة كل
And know that if the objection to the definition is due to the violation of some conditions of its goodness, then it has other answers besides what we mentioned. For if the objector says: This definition contains such-and-such a word, and it is a mistake (ghalat), and every definition that is like that is not good, then the author of the definition has two answers regarding that: The first: Denying that the word is a mistake, because it is in accordance with some grammatical schools. The second: Accepting that it is a mistake, by saying: We grant that the word is a mistake, but the correctness of the definition does not depend on it, and I am only obliged to produce a correct definition. So I only care about what is necessary, which is the correctness of my definition. And if the objection is that the definition contains such-and-such a word intended to mean such-and-such, and it is a metaphorical meaning, and there is no qarina that specifies the intended meaning, and every definition like that is not good, then the author of the definition has two answers as well: The first: That he claims that the metaphorical word has become a customary literal meaning (haqiqa 'urfiyya) in the intended sense. The second: That he claims there is a qarina that shows the intended meaning, but the objector was heedless of it. And if he said: This definition contains such-and-such a word, and it is ambiguous (mushtarak) because it is placed for multiple meanings, and there is no qarina that specifies the intended meaning, and every definition like that is not good, then the author of the definition has three answers: The first: That he answers that the word claimed to be ambiguous has become a customary literal meaning in the meaning he intends to the exclusion of other meanings. The second: That he accepts it, saying: We grant that it is ambiguous, but the defect of using an ambiguous word in a definition is only when it is not valid to intend every [meaning]
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage classifies objections to definitions that arise from violating conditions of a good definition (shurut husn al-ta'rif) and provides standard responses. The author mentions three types of objections: 1) a word in the definition is grammatically incorrect (ghalat), 2) a word is used metaphorically (majaz) without a qarina specifying intent, and 3) a word is ambiguous (mushtarak) without a qarina. For each, the author gives fixed answers. For the first (ghalat), two answers: either deny it is a mistake by citing a grammatical school that allows it, or concede the mistake but argue it doesn't affect the definition's correctness. For the second (majaz), two answers: claim the word has become a customary literal meaning (haqiqa 'urfiyya) in that context, or assert that there is a qarina but the objector missed it. For the third (ishtirak), three answers: first, claim the word has become a customary literal meaning in the intended sense; second, accept the ambiguity but argue the defect is only when it is not possible to intend all meanings? The text cuts off, but the standard third answer is often that the ambiguity is resolved by the context or that the definition's purpose is not harmed. This passage is a useful catalogue for dialectical practice.

A good definition can be defended against linguistic objections by denying the flaw, contextualizing it, or admitting it without harm.

Two Responses to a Grammatical Objection
Author presents two methods for rebutting a grammatical objection to a definition word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two responses to a grammatical objection in a dialectical exchange: man' (denial) and al-qawl bi-mūjabihi (conceding the premise but denying relevance).

Core Idea
Two ways to parry an objection: deny the fact or concede it but deny its force.

In dialectical debates, an opponent may attack the wording of a definition, claiming it is grammatically incorrect. This passage from the tradition of adab al-bahth provides two standard rebuttals. Understanding these moves will sharpen your ability to defend definitions in Islamic scholarly discussions, whether in fiqh, usul, or kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that a word in the definition is grammatically wrong (lafẓ ghalat). Recognize this as an attack on the form, not the substance, of the definition.
2
First response: man' (denial)
The defender denies that the word is a mistake, arguing that it is accepted in at least one grammatical school. This rebuttal challenges the accuracy of the objection itself.
3
Second response: al-qawl bi-mūjabihi (concession)
The defender accepts the objection as true but argues that the grammatical mistake does not affect the correctness of the definition. The definition's validity does not depend on that single word.
This move separates necessary from unnecessary elements in a definition.
4
Compare the two strategies
One strategy attacks the premise (the word is not really wrong), the other attacks the relevance (even if wrong, it doesn't matter). Both are valid dialectical maneuvers.
In practice, choose based on the strength of the objection and your evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : منع كون ذلك اللفظ غلطا ؛ لجريانه على بعض المذاهب النحوية .
والثاني : القول بموجبه ؛ بأن يقول : سلمنا أن ذلك اللفظ غلط، ولكنه لا تتوقف عليه صحة التعريف، وأنا لا يلزمني إلا صحة التعريف، فلم أعتد إلا بالذي لابد منه، وهو صحة تعريفي.
The first: denying that the word is a mistake, because it runs according to some grammatical schools. The second: accepting its premise, as he says: we grant that the word is a mistake, but the correctness of the definition does not depend on it, and I am only obligated to the correctness of the definition; so I only considered what is necessary, which is the correctness of my definition.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents two standard responses when an objector claims that a word in the definition is grammatically wrong. The first response is denial: the defender claims the word is not actually wrong because it is accepted in at least some grammatical schools. This shows that the definition is not necessarily flawed by a disputed usage. The second response is acceptance of the premise: the defender concedes that the word is a mistake but argues that the definition's correctness does not hinge on that word. Since the definition can be valid even with a minor error in wording, the objection is overridden. This illustrates the principle that in dialectical discussion, one may either contest the premise or grant it and deny its relevance.

Key Terms
منع
القول بموجبه
لفظ غلط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A grammatical objection to a definition can be rebutted by denying the mistake or conceding it while denying its relevance to the definition's correctness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Defenses Against a Metaphor Objection
Author explains two ways to defend a definition that uses a metaphorical word without context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply two dialectical responses—ḥaqīqa ‘urfiyya and qarīna—to defend a definition against the charge of using a metaphorical term without context.

Core Idea
A metaphorical term in a definition can be saved by either convention or context.

In a dialectical exchange, the objector may claim that a definition fails because it uses a metaphorical word (majāz) without a contextual indicator (qarīna) to specify the intended meaning. The author teaches you two ready-made countermoves. Mastering these allows you to protect definitions from being dismissed as ambiguous, a crucial skill in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector points out a word in the definition that is metaphorical and lacks a qarīna. Understand this is a challenge to the clarity of the definition.
The objection assumes that every definition must use terms in their literal meaning unless context is provided.
2
Apply the first response: claim ʿurfiyya
Assert that the word, though once metaphorical, has become conventional reality (ḥaqīqa ‘urfiyya) for the intended meaning due to common usage. This neutralizes the objection because the term is now effectively literal.
This move is common in uṣūl al-fiqh, e.g., 'ṣalāh' originally meant 'supplication' but came to mean the ritual prayer.
3
Apply the second response: point to a qarīna
Insist that there indeed is a contextual indicator that clarifies the intended meaning, but the objector overlooked it. The definer must be ready to identify the qarīna if pressed.
The qarīna can be linguistic (e.g., additional words) or situational (e.g., the context of discussion).
4
Distinguish between the two responses
The first response (ʿurfiyya) changes the status of the term itself; the second (qarīna) appeals to external evidence. They are not mutually exclusive but can be used separately.
Choosing the right response depends on the term and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اعترض على التعريف بأن فيه لفظة كذا يراد بها كذا وهو معنى مجازي وليس ثَمّ قرينةٌ تعيّن المراد وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن فلصاحب التعريف عن ذلك جوابان أيضًا :
الأول : أن يدعي أن ذلك اللفظ المجازي صار حقيقة عرفية في المعنى المقصود.
والثاني: أن يدعي أن ثَم قرينةً تبيّن المراد، ولكن المعترض غفل عنها.
And if one objects to the definition that it contains such-and-such a word intended to mean something, and it is a metaphorical meaning, and there is no context indicator that specifies the intended meaning, and every definition that is like that is not good, then the definer has two answers to that as well: The first: that he claims that the metaphorical word has become a conventional reality in the intended meaning. The second: that he claims that there is a context indicator that clarifies the intended meaning, but the objector was inattentive to it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage continues the theme of defending definitions. Here the objection is that a definition uses a metaphorical term (majāz) without a qarīna (context indicator) to specify the intended meaning; such a definition is considered deficient. The author offers two replies. The first is to assert that the originally metaphorical expression has become a conventional reality (ḥaqīqa ‘urfiyya) in that meaning. In other words, it is so commonly used for that meaning that it is no longer metaphorical but has become a real term. The second reply is to insist that there is indeed a qarīna that clarifies the intent, but the objector overlooked it. These are standard moves in dialectic to protect a definition from the charge of ambiguity.

Key Terms

حقيقة عرفية

Key Takeaway

Three Responses to the Homonym Objection in Definitions
Author presents three methods to defend a definition that uses a homonym without context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three responses to the objection that a definition uses a homonym without a context indicator.

Core Idea
A homonym in a definition is not always fatal—it can be salvaged by convention, universal applicability, or an overlooked context.

When defining a term, using a homonym (mushtarak) without a context indicator (qarīna) can be criticised as ambiguous and invalid. Logicians (manṭiqiyyūn) strictly forbid this, but practitioners of dialectic (ahl al-fann) are more lenient. In this passage, the author outlines three powerful responses a definer can use to defend such a definition, each grounded in different logical or linguistic principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Identify the objection: a definition contains a homonym with multiple meanings and no context indicator to specify which is intended. Why is this considered problematic? It can lead to ambiguity and failure to convey a single meaning.
The objection is that the definition is 'not good' (ghayr ḥasan).
2
Explore the First Response: Conventional Reality
The definer can claim the word has become a ḥaqīqa ‘urfiyya (conventional reality) in the intended meaning. That is, through common usage it no longer carries ambiguity in that context. This transforms a homonym into a univocal term within the relevant field.
This response neutralises the objection by denying that the word is genuinely ambiguous in practice.
3
Analyse the Second Response: Universal Applicability
The definer concedes the word is homonymous but argues that the defect only applies when not all meanings can be truthfully intended. If all meanings of the homonym apply to the defined thing, using the word without context is acceptable. Study the example of 'ʿayn Zayd' where all three meanings (eye, spring, gem) are true of the attacked object.
This is a clever move: it shifts the condition from absence of context to impossibility of simultaneous truth.
4
Recognise the Third Response: Overlooked Context
The definer can simply assert that a context indicator exists but the objector failed to notice it. This is the simplest reply and often used when the context is obvious to specialists.
This response shifts the burden to the objector to prove no context exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان قال : هذا التعريف مشتمل على لفظ كذا، وهو مشترك ؛ لوضعه لمعاني متعددة وليس هناك قرينة تعين المراد، وكل تعريف كان كذلك فهو غير حسن فلصاحب التعريف عن ذلك ثلاثة أجوبة : الأول : أن يجيب بأنّ اللفظ المدعى أنه مشترك قد صار حقيقة عرفية في المعنى الذي يقصده دون غيره من المعاني .
والثاني : أن يقول بموجبه ، فيقول : سلّمنا أنه مشترك، ولكن عدم حسن استعمال المشترك في التعريف محله فيما لم تصح فيه إرادة كل معنى من معانيه وهنا تصح إرادة كل معنى من معانيه، فلو تعدى اللصوص على رجل اسمه (زيد) في محل معين، فعوّروا عينه الباصرة، وغوّروا عينه الجارية، واستلبوا عينه التي هي فضته وذهبه، فقال قائل : عرّف لنا الشيء الذي تعدى عليه اللصوص في ذلك المحل المعين، فقال: المعرّف : هو عين زيد فاعترض عليه المعترض بأنَّ العين لفظ مشترك، فإنه يجيب بأن إطلاق العين في التعريف على كل صحيح؛ لأنه كله واحد من الثلاثة حق، واللفظي لا يُشترط فيه الجمع عند أكثر أهل هذا الفن والمشترك إن كان كذلك فلا مانع منه في التعريف .
وقد قدمنا أن إطلاق المجاز والمشترك دون قرينة تعين المراد في التعريفات يبطل التعريف عند المنطقيين، فالسلامة من ذلك شرط عندهم لا شرط في الحسن، خلافًا لأهل هذا الفن، وقد قدمنا أن قول المنطقيين فيه أظهر .
الثالث : إثبات قرينة تعين المراد من المشترك ولكن المعترض لم ينتبه لها
And if he said: This definition contains such-and-such a word, and it is a homonym, because it is placed for multiple meanings, and there is no context indicator to specify the intended meaning, and every definition that is like that is not good, then the definer has three answers for that: The first: that he replies that the word claimed to be homonymous has become a conventional reality in the meaning he intends exclusively. The second: that he accepts its premise, saying: we grant that it is a homonym, but the absence of goodness in using a homonym in a definition applies where it is not valid to intend every one of its meanings, and here it is valid to intend every one of its meanings. For example, if thieves attacked a man named Zayd in a specific place, and they gouged his seeing eye (ʿayn), and damaged his running eye (spring), and stole his eye which is his silver and gold (precious gem), and someone said: define for us the thing that the thieves attacked in that specific place, and he said: the defined is: the ʿayn of Zayd; then the objector objected that ʿayn is a homonym, he replies that using ʿayn in the definition for everything is correct, because each one of the three is real, and in the lafẓī (verbal) it is not required to have all meanings intended according to most practitioners of this art, and if the homonym is such, there is no prevention in the definition. And we have previously stated that using metaphor and homonym without a context indicator to specify the intended meaning invalidates the definition according to the logicians; so freedom from that is a condition for them, not a condition for goodness, contrary to the practitioners of this art, and we have previously stated that the position of the logicians is more evident. Third: establishing a context indicator that specifies the intended meaning from the homonym, but the objector did not pay attention to it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection that a definition contains a homonym (mushtarak) without a context indicator; such a definition is considered defective. The author provides three responses. The first is the same as for metaphor: the word has become a conventional reality (ḥaqīqa ‘urfiyya) in the intended sense, so it is no longer ambiguous. The second response is more complex: the defender concedes the word is homonymous but argues that the defect only applies when it is not possible to intend all meanings simultaneously. In this example, with the word ʿayn (eye, spring, precious gem), in the scenario given, all three meanings actually apply to the object attacked (the man's physical eye, his spring, and his gem), so each meaning is true of the defined thing. Since the object attacked encompasses all three, using the homonym is valid without a context indicator because each intended meaning is correct. The author also notes that the logicians (manṭiqiyyūn) consider using metaphor or homonym without a context indicator as invalidating the definition (bāṭil), but the practitioners of this art (ahl hādhā al-fann) treat it only as a defect in perfection (laysa bi-ḥasan). The author sides with the logicians. The third response is again the claim that a context indicator exists but was missed.

Key Terms
مشترك
حقيقة عرفية
منطقيون
أهل هذا الفن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of 'ʿayn Zayd', why does the defender argue that using the homonym is valid?
A) Because 'ʿayn' has become a conventional reality for each meaning.
B) Because each meaning of 'ʿayn' is true of the thing being defined.
C) Because the objector is ignorant of grammar.
D) Because logicians allow any homonym in definitions.

Key Takeaway
A homonym in a definition can be defended if it has become a conventional reality, all meanings apply to the defined thing, or a context indicator exists but was overlooked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

All Objections Target Implicit Claims in Definitions
Author notes that every objection to a definition ultimately challenges an implicit claim of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit claims entailed by a given definition in a dialectical context.

Core Idea
Every definition carries hidden claims — an objection attacks those, not the surface wording.

The author teaches that definitions are not simple statements: they carry implicit claims about their type and structure. Understanding these hidden premises is crucial for proper dialectical engagement. When a definer says 'This is a real and complete definition,' he is implicitly claiming, among other things, that it uses the proximate genus and differentia. Any objection to the definition actually targets one or more of these hidden claims.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the definer's implicit assertion
When someone presents a definition, note what they are implicitly claiming beyond the surface words. For example, saying 'This definition is haqiqi' implies it captures the essence, not just accidental properties.
The surface wording may be neutral, but the claim carries logical commitments.
2
Extract the type and completeness claim
Identify whether the definer claims the definition is real (haqiqi) or descriptive (rasmi), and whether it is complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis). These are the first layer of implicit claims.
A complete hadd must have proximate genus and differentia.
3
Identify the structural entailments
A claim of 'hadd tamm' entails that the definition uses the proximate genus and differentia. Trace what components are necessarily implied by the type of definition.
The author specifically mentions the first implied claim: that it is by the proximate genus and differentia.
4
Trace objections back to implicit claims
Any objection to a definition can be reformulated as an attack on one of its implicit claims. For instance, objecting that the definition is not complete challenges the claim of being a hadd tamm.
This meta-level understanding allows for more precise dialectical responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التعريف تتوجه إليه اعتراضات غير ماذكرنا، وكلها راجع في الحقيقة إلى الدعاوى الضمنية التي تضمنها التعريف كما قدمنا إيضاحه كأن يقول صاحب التعريف مثلاً : هذا التعريف حقيقي، وهو حد تام، فإنّ كلامه هذا يستلزم الدعاوى الآتية : الأولى : أنه بالجنس والفصل القريبين .
And know that there are objections directed at definitions other than what we have mentioned, and all of them in reality go back to the implicit claims that the definition contains, as we previously clarified. For example, if the definer says: 'This definition is haqiqi and it is a complete hadd,' then this speech of his entails the following claims: The first: that it is by the proximate genus and differentia.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage introduces a meta-level insight: every definition carries within it several implied claims (da‘āwā ḍimniyya). When someone defines something, they are implicitly asserting, for example, that the definition is real (ḥaqīqī) and that it is a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm). Such a claim entails, among other things, that the definition uses the proximate genus (jins qarīb) and differentia (faṣl qarīb). Thus, any objection to the definition actually targets one or more of these implicit claims. The author mentions this to show that the specific objections discussed earlier are not exhaustive; all objections reduce to attacking the hidden claims inherent in the act of defining. This deepens the student's understanding of the dialectical nature of definition criticism.

Key Takeaway
All objections to definitions ultimately attack the hidden claims they entail, not the surface wording.

Objections to Definitions and Responses in Dialectic
Author explains types of objections to definitions and the requirement of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of objections to definitions, explain why objections require proof, and describe how a definer may respond with refusal (mana').

Core Idea
Objections to definitions need proof; the definer can simply refuse without counter-proof.

In dialectical debate (jadal), definitions are often challenged. The author outlines three standard objections: parts are accidental, parts are not the proximate genus and differentia, or another complete definition exists. He then establishes a key procedural rule: any objection must be supported by a proof (dalīl). The definer may respond with a simple refusal (mana') or provide counter-evidence. Understanding this rule is essential for engaging in rigorous theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definer's claim
What definition is being proposed? In the passage, the example is 'human is a rational animal' (al-insān hayawān nāṭiq), claimed as a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm).
2
Recognize the objector's challenge
Which of the three objection types does the objector use? Here, the objector claims that 'rational' is not essential (dhātī) but accidental (ʿaraḍī), equating it to 'laughing' (ḍāḥik). This falls under the first type: parts are not essential.
The other two types: parts are not proximate genus and differentia, or there exists another complete definition.
3
Check for proof
Does the objector provide a proof (dalīl) for his objection? The objector says 'rational' and 'laughing' are no different, but the author states that such a claim is not accepted without proof. The objector must bring evidence, not mere assertion.
The author emphasizes: 'these mentioned claims are not accepted as objections unless with a proof that produces them.'
4
Analyze the definer's response
How does the definer respond? He may simply refuse (mana') the objection without proof, or he may provide proof. In the example, the definer refuses the claim that 'rational' is not essential and that there is no difference between rational and laughing. He can optionally cite the proof that one can ask 'Why are you laughing?' but not 'Why are you rational?'.
Refusal (mana') is a valid dialectical move; the definer is not obliged to give counter-proof, though doing so strengthens his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : أن أجزاءه كلها ذاتية لا عرضية . فيُعترض عليه بغير ما تقدم : كأن يقول المعترض: أجزاء تعريفك هذا ليست ذاتية بل هي عرضية، أو بعضها عرضي. وكأن يقول : هذان الجزآن ليسا هما الجنس والفصل القريبين . وكأن يدعي أن لتلك الحقيقة حدًا تامًا غير ماذكره ؛ لاستحالة إمكان حدين لحقيقة واحدة؛ لأن الحد لا يكون إلا بالفصل الذي هو المميز الذاتي، وتعدده مستحيل ؛ لأن الفصل القريب لا يمكن تعدده لحقيقة واحدة كما هو معلوم في محله؛ لأن تعدده يفضي إلى المستحيل، وهو كون الحقيقة الواحدة في ذاتها حقيقتين متباينتين؛ لأن كل جنس قريب إذا ذكر معه الفصل حصلت حقيقة الماهية؛ لأنها مركبة عندهم من جنس وفصل، كما تقدم إيضاحه(۱)، فلو فرضنا تعدد الفصل القريب للماهية الواحدة لكان كل واحد من الفصلين مع الجنس حقيقة مستقلة غير الحقيقة الأخرى، والمفروض أنها حقيقة واحدة، فلا يمكن كونها حقيقتين مختلفتين، وتعدد الفصل يستلزم ذلك، وما استلزم المحال فهو محال ، هكذا يقولون، والعلم عند الله تعالى . واعلم أن هذه الدعاوى المذكورة لا يُقبل الاعتراض بها إلا بدليل ينتجها ، فلا تُقبل من المعترض بدعواه المجردة عن الدليل، فإن أقام الدليل على أحد أوجه الاعتراض المذكورة كان لصاحب التعريف أن يجيب عن دليله بالمنع، سواء ذكر دليل المنع أو لم يذكره، وسيأتي في الكلام على المنع إن شاء الله ما يزيد هذا إيضاحا . فلو قال المعرّف : تعريفي الإنسان بأنه (الحيوان الناطق) حد تام ؛ فأجزاؤه ذاتية ؛ لأنها حد وفصل قريبان . فقال المعترض : لا أسلم أن الناطق مثلاً ذاتي، بدليل أن الضاحك غير ذاتي، والناطقُ والضاحكُ لا فرق بينهما . فيقول صاحب التعريف : أمنع قولك : إن الناطق غير ذاتي، وأنه لا فرق بينه وبين الضاحك. وإن شاء اقتصر على هذا المنع مجردًا عن الدليل، وإن شاء أقام عليه الدليل وأدلة الفرق بينهما قد قدمناها موضحة في المقدمة المنطقية (١)، ومثال إقامة الدليل على الفرق بينهما ببعض الفوارق التي قدمناها أن يقول : لو رأيت إنسانًا يضحك لكان لك أن تسأله ضحكه عن سبب فتقول :له ما أضحكك ؟ بخلاف كون الإنسان ناطقا ـ أي ذا قوة عاقلة مفكرة يقدر بها على إدراك العلوم والآراء ، فليس لك أن تقول له : ما سبب كونك ناطقا ؟ كما تقدم إيضاحه .
Second: that all its parts are essential, not accidental. So it is objected to with other than what preceded: such as the objector saying: The parts of this definition of yours are not essential but accidental, or some of them are accidental. And such as saying: These two parts are not the proximate genus and differentia. And such as claiming that that essence has a complete other definition besides what he mentioned, because it is impossible for there to be two definitions for a single essence; because the definition is only by the differentia which is the essential distinguisher, and its multiplicity is impossible, because the proximate differentia cannot be multiple for a single essence as is known in its place; because its multiplicity leads to the impossible, which is that the single essence in itself becomes two distinct essences; because every proximate genus when mentioned with its differentia yields the essence of the quiddity; because it is composed, according to them, of genus and differentia, as was clarified previously(1), so if we suppose multiple proximate differentiae for one quiddity, then each one of the two differentiae with the genus would be an independent essence different from the other essence, and it is assumed to be a single essence, so it cannot be two different essences, and multiplicity of the differentia necessitates that, and whatever necessitates the impossible is itself impossible, thus they say, and knowledge is with Allah Almighty. And know that these mentioned claims are not accepted as objections unless with a proof that produces them, so they are not accepted from the objector by his mere claim without proof. If he brings a proof for one of the mentioned forms of objection, then the author of the definition can answer his proof with refusal (mana'), whether he mentions the proof of refusal or not, and there will come in the discussion on refusal, Allah willing, what will further clarify this. So if the definer says: My definition of human as 'rational animal' is a complete definition; its parts are essential because they are genus and proximate differentia. So the objector says: I do not grant that 'rational', for example, is essential, based on the proof that 'laughing' is not essential, and 'rational' and 'laughing' are no different between them. So the author of the definition says: I refuse your statement that 'rational' is not essential and that there is no difference between it and 'laughing'. If he wishes, he may confine himself to this refusal without proof, and if he wishes, he may bring proof upon it. And the proofs of the difference between them have been presented earlier clarified in the logical introduction (1). An example of bringing proof of the difference between them using some of the distinctions we presented is that he says: If you see a human laughing, you can ask him about the reason for his laughter, saying: Why are you laughing? Unlike the fact that the human is rational—i.e., having a rational thinking faculty by which he is capable of grasping sciences and opinions—then you cannot say to him: What is the reason for your being rational? As was clarified previously.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage deals with dialectical objections to definitions (hadd) and the proper way to respond. The author presents three types of objections: 1) claiming that some parts of the definition are not essential but accidental, 2) claiming that the parts are not the proximate genus and differentia, and 3) claiming that the essence has another complete definition, arguing that two definitions for one essence are impossible because each differentia gives a distinct species. The author then stresses that such objections are not accepted unless supported by proof (dalīl). The definer may simply refuse (mana') the objection without proof or may provide counter-evidence. The example given is the definition of human as 'rational animal'. The objector argues that 'rational' is not essential because 'laughing' is not essential and there is no real difference. The definer can either deny this or point out that you can ask why someone laughs but not why they are rational, showing a clear distinction. This highlights the dialectical principle that mere claims are insufficient; proof is required for an objection to be sustained.

عرضي (ʿaraḍī)

الحد التام (al-ḥadd al-tāmm)
المنع (al-manaʿ)

The Dialectical Move of Denial (Man') in Definition Debates
Author illustrates how a definer can respond by denying the objector's premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the denial (man') response in a dialectical exchange and explain when a definer may use it without evidence.

Core Idea
In jadal, the definer may simply deny the objector's premise—evidence is optional.

In this passage, the author shows how a definer can counter an objection by denying the objector's claim. This response, called man', is a fundamental move in the jadal tradition. Understanding when and how to use denial is key to mastering dialectical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definer's claim
Locate the definer's assertion that his definition is perfect (hadd tamm) and its parts are essential. In the example, the definition of man as 'rational animal' is claimed to have essential parts.
The definer provides both genus and differentia.
2
Spot the objector's objection
The objector argues that 'rational' is not essential by comparing it to 'laughing', which is accidental, and claiming no difference between them. This is the premise the definer will deny.
The objector uses an analogy between essential and accidental.
3
Locate the denial (man')
The definer responds by saying 'I deny your statement that rational is not essential and that there is no difference between it and laughing.' This is the denial of the objector's premise.
The denial is a direct rejection of the objector's claim.
4
Assess the optional evidence
The author notes that the definer may stop at denial or provide evidence. An example given: you can ask about the cause of laughter but not of rationality, proving they are different.
Evidence strengthens the denial but is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجيب عن دليله بالمنع، سواء ذكر دليل المنع أو لم يذكره، وسيأتي في الكلام على المنع إن شاء الله ما يزيد هذا إيضاحا . فلو قال المعرّف : تعريفي الإنسان بأنه (الحيوان الناطق) حد تام ؛ فأجزاؤه ذاتية ؛ لأنها حد وفصل قريبان . فقال المعترض : لا أسلم أن الناطق مثلاً ذاتي، بدليل أن الضاحك غير ذاتي، والناطقُ والضاحكُ لا فرق بينهما . فيقول صاحب التعريف : أمنع قولك : إن الناطق غير ذاتي، وأنه لا فرق بينه وبين الضاحك. وإن شاء اقتصر على هذا المنع مجردًا عن الدليل، وإن شاء أقام عليه الدليل وأدلة الفرق بينهما قد قدمناها موضحة في المقدمة المنطقية، ومثال إقامة الدليل على الفرق بينهما ببعض الفوارق التي قدمناها أن يقول : لو رأيت إنسانًا يضحك لكان لك أن تسأله ضحكه عن سبب فتقول :له ما أضحكك ؟ بخلاف كون الإنسان ناطقا ـ أي ذا قوة عاقلة مفكرة يقدر بها على إدراك العلوم والآراء ، فليس لك أن تقول له : ما سبب كونك ناطقا ؟ كما تقدم إيضاحه .
He answers his proof by denial, whether he mentions the evidence for the denial or not. And there will come in the discussion on denial, God willing, what increases this clarification. So if the definer says: 'My definition of man as 'the rational animal' is a perfect definition; its parts are essential because they are a proximate genus and differentia.' Then the objector says: 'I do not accept for example that 'rational' is essential, based on the evidence that 'laughing' is not essential, and there is no difference between 'rational' and 'laughing.' Then the definer says: 'I deny your statement that 'rational' is not essential and that there is no difference between it and 'laughing.' And if he wishes, he restricts himself to this denial without evidence, and if he wishes, he adduces evidence for it. And the proofs of the difference between them have been presented clarified in the Logical Introduction. An example of adducing evidence for the difference between them using some of the distinctions we presented is that he says: 'If you saw a man laughing, you could ask him about the cause of his laughter and say: 'What made you laugh?' Unlike man being rational – i.e., possessing a thinking, rational faculty by which he can grasp knowledge and opinions – you cannot say to him: 'What is the cause of your rationality?' as was clarified previously.'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage illustrates the dialectical method of responding to an objection by denial (man') in a debate about definition. The author first states the general principle: the definer who is attacked may answer by denying a premise of the objector's proof, whether or not he provides evidence for that denial. Then he gives a concrete example. The definer claims his definition of man as 'rational animal' is a perfect definition (ḥadd tāmm). The objector objects that 'rational' (nāṭiq) is not essential, citing 'laughing' (ḍāḥik) as a comparison: since laughing is not essential, and there is no difference between rational and laughing, rational is also not essential. The definer responds by simply denying the objector's claim: 'I deny your statement that rational is not essential and that there is no difference between it and laughing.' The definer has the option to either stop at this denial or to provide evidence for the difference. The author notes that the evidence has been given earlier in the book. He then provides a sample evidence: if you see a man laughing, you can ask him 'What made you laugh?', because laughter has a cause. But you cannot ask a man 'What is the cause of your rationality?' because rationality is an essential property, not an accidental one. This demonstrates that rational and laughing are different: rationality is a necessary, intrinsic characteristic, while laughter is a contingent, observable action. This example is rooted in the Islamic philosophical tradition, using the standard definition of man as rational animal.

Key Terms
المنع
الناطق
الضاحك
الحد التام
الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Rational and laughing have no difference, and laughing is not essential.
D) Rational is a genus, not a differentia.

Key Takeaway
Denial (man') is a legitimate dialectical response that does not obligate evidence; it shifts the burden to the objector.

Terms for Objector and Defender in Definition Debates
Author discusses the different names given to the objector and defender in munazara on definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two terminological conventions for the objector and defender in a definition debate and explain the logical reasoning behind the majority's terms.

Core Idea
The objector must give evidence; the defender may simply deny. Hence the majority calls them mustadill and māni'.

In Islamic dialectic (munāzara), the roles of the disputants are not arbitrary but reflect the burden of proof. This lesson clarifies the prevalent terminology used for the objector (nāqiḍ) and defender (mujīb) in debates about definitions (taʻrīf), and the reasoning behind the majority's terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two naming conventions
The author presents two sets of terms. Some scholars call the objector 'sā'il' (questioner) and the defender 'mu'allil' (reason-giver). The majority calls the objector 'mustadill' (evidence-adducer) and the defender 'māni'' (denier).
2
Understand the rationale for 'mustadill'
The objector cannot merely claim the definition is defective; he must provide evidence (istidlāl) showing that a condition of correct definition is violated. Hence he is appropriately called 'mustadill'.
This aligns with the principle that objections require justification.
3
Understand the rationale for 'māni''
The defender's response suffices by simply denying one of the premises of the objector's proof, without necessarily providing counter-evidence. Thus he is called 'māni'' (denier).
This was referenced earlier (page 191) regarding the permissibility of denial with or without support.
4
Note the parallel with division debates
The author mentions that similar naming applies to debates about division (taqsīm), indicating this terminology is part of a broader dialectical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تسمية طرفي المناظرة في التعريف. اعلم أن من علماء هذا الفن من يسمي ناقض التعريف المعترض عليه (سائلاً) وموجهه المدافع عنه : (معللا) . وأكثرهم على أن ناقضه يسمى: (مستدلا) وموجهه يسمى: (مانعا)، ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح. وهم يريدون بذلك أن الاعتراض على التعريف لا يتم بالدعوى مجردة بل لابد لمدعي فساد التعريف من إقامة الدليل على دعواه اختلال شرط من شروط صحته مثلاً، وبذلك يتجه كونه مستدلاً . ويقصدون بتسمية الآخرِ مانعا أنّ جوابه عن الاعتراض على تعريفه يكفي أن يكون بمنع مقدمة من مقدمات دليل البطلان، سواء ذكر سندا لمنعه أو لم يذكره، كما أشرنا إليه قريبًا، وبذلك يتجه كونه مانعا . ونحو هذا يجري في توجيه تسمية طرفي المناظرة في التقسيم، التي أشرنا لها سابقا والعلم عند الله - تعالى -.
Chapter on naming the two parties of the debate in definition. Know that among the scholars of this art, some call the one who objects to the definition (the one objected to) a 'questioner' (sā'il), and the one who directs it and defends it a 'reason-giver' (mu'allil). But the majority are that the objector is called a 'one who adduces evidence' (mustadill), and the one who directs it is called a 'denier' (māni'), and there is no quarreling over terminology. By this they mean that objection to a definition is not accomplished by mere claim; rather, the one who claims the definition is defective must adduce evidence for his claim, for example by alleging the violation of one of its conditions of validity, and thereby it is appropriate that he be a 'mustadill'. And they intend by naming the other a 'māni'' that his response to the objection to his definition suffices by denying a premise from the premises of the proof of invalidity, whether he mentions a basis for his denial or not, as we recently indicated. And thereby it is appropriate that he be a 'māni''. And something similar applies to directing the naming of the two parties in the debate on division (taqsīm), which we indicated previously. And knowledge belongs to God, Most High.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the terminology used for the two opposing parties in a munāẓara (debate) focused on definition (taʻrīf). The author presents two terminological conventions. First, some scholars call the objector (nāqiḍ or muʻtarid) a 'sā'il' (questioner) and the defender (muwajjih) a 'mu'allil' (reason-giver). Second, the majority prefer to call the objector a 'mustadill' (one who brings evidence) and the defender a 'māni'' (denier). The author asserts there is no conflict (lā mushāḥata) over terminology. He then explains the logic behind the majority's terminology: the objector cannot merely claim the definition is defective; he must provide evidence (istidlāl) showing that some condition of correct definition is violated, hence he is a 'mustadill'. The defender, on the other hand, can respond by simply denying (manʻ) one of the premises of the objector's proof, without necessarily providing counter-evidence, so he is a 'māni''. This aligns with the earlier discussion on page 191 about the permissibility of denial with or without evidence. The author notes that similar naming applies to debates about division (taqsīm), which were discussed previously. This classification is crucial for understanding the structure of dialectical exchanges in Islamic logic and debate.

Key Takeaway
The majority terminology (mustadill/māni') reflects the logical structure of definition debates: the objector must provide evidence, while the defender may simply deny a premise.

?
1. In a debate about the definition of 'salah', you are the objector. According to the majority terminology, what must you do before your objection is considered valid? Write a brief explanation.
?

The Prover and the Preventer: Key Roles in Definition Debates
Author explains the conventional names for objector and defender in definition debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the roles of the mustadill (prover) and the mani' (preventer) in a definition debate and explain why the objector must provide a proof while the defender need only block a premise.

Core Idea
In definition debates, the objector proves a flaw; the defender only needs to block that proof.

This lesson introduces the conventional terminology used in Islamic dialectic (jadal) for the two sides of a definition debate. Understanding these roles is crucial because they dictate the burden of proof: the objector cannot simply claim a definition is wrong; he must produce evidence. The defender, in turn, can respond minimally by preventing one premise of that evidence, without having to rebuild the definition. This principle structures countless juristic and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
Recognize that the objector is called mustadill (prover) and the defender is called mani' (preventer) in the majority usage. Note that some scholars use alternative terms (sa'il and mu'allil), but the dominant convention is mustadill and mani'.
The passage explicitly mentions both terminologies but states that most scholars adopt mustadill and mani'.
2
Understand the objector's burden
The objector cannot merely assert the definition is invalid; he must present a proof (dalil) showing that a specific condition of validity is violated. For example, he might argue that the definition is too broad or too narrow.
The text says: 'the objection to the definition is not accomplished by a mere claim; rather, the one who claims that the definition is corrupt must provide proof for his claim'.
3
Understand the defender's minimal response
The defender's response need only block (man') one premise of the objector's proof. He does not need to provide counter-evidence or a new definition; simply preventing a premise suffices to defeat the objection.
The text states: 'his response to the objection... need only be to prevent one premise of the premises of the proof of falsity, whether he mentions a support for his prevention or not'.
4
Connect to real debates
Apply this logic to a real example: In a debate about the definition of 'worship' ('ibada) in usul al-fiqh, the objector (mustadill) must prove that a proposed definition fails to include all acts of worship or includes non-worship acts. The defender (mani') can respond by blocking one premise of that proof, e.g., by denying that a particular act is excluded.
This illustrates how the rule operates in practice, ensuring that debates remain focused on specific logical flaws rather than descending into general assertions.

وهم يريدون بذلك أن الاعتراض على التعريف لا يتم بالدعوى مجردة بل لابد لمدعي فساد التعريف من إقامة الدليل على دعواه اختلال شرط من شروط صحته مثلاً، وبذلك يتجه كونه مستدلاً . ويقصدون بتسمية الآخرِ مانعا أنّ جوابه عن الاعتراض على تعريفه يكفي أن يكون بمنع مقدمة من مقدمات دليل البطلان، سواء ذكر سندا لمنعه أو لم يذكره، كما أشرنا إليه قريبًا، وبذلك يتجه كونه مانعا . ونحو هذا يجري في توجيه تسمية طرفي المناظرة في التقسيم، التي أشرنا لها سابقا والعلم عند الله - تعالى -.
Chapter on naming the two sides of the debate in definition. Know that some scholars of this art call the one who refutes the definition, i.e., the objector, a 'questioner' (sa'il), and the one who defends it, i.e., the defender, a 'rationalizer' (mu'allil). Most of them, however, call the refuter a 'prover' (mustadill) and the defender a 'preventer' (mani'). There is no contention over terminology. By that they intend that the objection to the definition is not accomplished by a mere claim; rather, the one who claims that the definition is corrupt must provide proof for his claim, e.g., of the violation of one of its conditions of validity. Thus, he is a 'prover'. And they intend by naming the other a 'preventer' that his response to the objection to his definition need only be to prevent one premise of the premises of the proof of falsity, whether he mentions a support for his prevention or not, as we indicated recently. Thus, he is a 'preventer'. And something similar applies in the terminology for the two sides of the debate in division, which we indicated previously. And knowledge is with Allah, Most High.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage clarifies the technical terminology used for the two participants in a debate (munazara) concerning a definition. The author first notes that some scholars call the one who objects to a definition a 'sa'il' (questioner) and the defender a 'mu'allil' (rationalizer). However, the majority use 'mustadill' (prover) for the objector and 'mani'' (preventer) for the defender. The author emphasizes that this is just terminology with no contention. The rationale is that the objector cannot merely claim the definition is invalid; he must present evidence for a specific flaw. Thus, he is a 'mustadill' (one who brings a proof). Conversely, the defender can respond by simply blocking (man') one premise of that proof, without needing to provide counter-evidence. This is a key principle in dialectical method (jadal). The author notes the same pattern applies in debates about divisions. This passage is a meta-discussion on the rules of debate, not a logical argument itself.

Key Takeaway
The mustadill must prove a flaw; the mani' need only block a premise of that proof.

The Twofold Classification of Tasdiq in Munazara
Author classifies tasdiq into nazari and badihi khafi with four cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given tasdiq in a munazara as nazari or badihi khafi, and identify whether it is presented with or without its required support.

Core Idea
A tasdiq's need for proof or reminder determines its presentation and the opponent's response.

In Islamic dialectical debates, not all assents are treated equally. The author distinguishes theoretical assents that require proof from hidden self-evident assents that need only a reminder. This lesson teaches you to identify these types and their two presentation states, forming four distinct cases that govern debate strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core types of tasdiq
The passage divides tasdiq into nazari (theoretical) and badihi khafi (hidden self-evident). Nazari needs a proof (daleel), while badihi khafi needs a reminder (tanbih).
Badihi khafi is self-evident in itself but not immediately obvious to the mind.
2
Recognize the two presentation states for each type
Each type can be presented either alone (without its required element) or accompanied by its element (proof for nazari, reminder for badihi khafi). This gives a 2x2 matrix.
3
Map the four cases
The four cases are: (1) nazari without proof, (2) nazari with proof, (3) badihi khafi without reminder, (4) badihi khafi with reminder. The passage lists them explicitly.
4
Practice with the examples from the passage
The mutawatir example about Baghdad (with reminder) is case 4; the creation example (with proof) is case 2. Classify them to confirm understanding.
The syllogisms themselves are secondary; focus on the type of tasdiq and presentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال التنبيه في المتواتر أن تقول: وجود بغداد أخبر به عدد يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب عادة وكل شيء أخبر به عدد كذلك فهو حق ينتج من الشكل الأول : وجود بغداد حق. وهذا القياس للتنبيه لا الاستدلال ؛ لأن من المعلوم أن ما أخبر به العدد المذكور فهو حق؛ ولأن المتواترات من البديهيات. ومثال الاستدلال على التصديق النظري قولك : أنا مخلوق بعد أن كنتُ معدومًا، وكل مخلوق بعد أن كان معدوما فلابد له من خالق، ينتج من الشكل الأول : أنا لابد لي من خالق . وقد علمت مما مر أن المناظرة في التصديق لا تكون إلا في نوعين لا ثالث لهما : الأول التصديق النظري، وهو المحتاج إلى الدليل . والثاني التصديق البديهي الخفي، وهو المحتاج إلى التنبيه . وهذه تفاصيل طرق المناظرة فيهما : اعلم أولاً أنك إذا ألقى إليك صاحب التصديق تصديقا نظريًا أو تصديقا بديهيا خفيًا فلكل واحد منهما حالتان : أما حالتا التصديق النظري فالأولى منهما أن يكون ألقاه إليك مجردًا عن الدليل؛ لأجل إيهام أنه من البديهيات التي لا تحتاج لدليل أو لغرض آخر ، والثانية أن يكون ألقاه إليك مقرونا بالدليل . وهاتان هما حالتا البديهي الخفي؛ لأنه تارة يلقيه إليك مجردًا عن التنبيه لأجل إيهام أنه من الجليات أو لغرض آخر، وتارة يلقيه إليك مقرونا بالتنبيه، فالأقسام أربعة : الأول : تصديق نظري مجرد عن الدليل . الثاني : تصديق نظري مقرون بالدليل . الثالث : بديهي خفي مجرد عن التنبيه . الرابع : بديهي خفي مقرون بالتنبيه . فإن كان ألقى إليك تصديقا نظريًا مجردًا عن الدليل، أو بديهيا خفيًا مجردًا عن التنبيه فليس لك في الاعتراض عليه إلا شيء واحد وهو المنع ، كأن تقول : أمنع هذه الدعوى، أو : لا أسلم لك هذه الدعوى، أو : هذه الدعوى ممنوعة، أو غير مسلمة، ونحو ذلك ويكفيك الاقتصار على واحدة من تلك العبارات، فيكون منعك هذا منعا مجردًا عن السند، وهو الأظهر عندي. وقد قال غير واحد من أهل هذا الفن: إنه لا مانع من أن تذكر مع المنع المذكور السند الذي استندت إليه في المنع، فيكون منعك
And an example of reminder in the mutawatir: you say: The existence of Baghdad was reported by a number whose collusion upon lying is habitually impossible. And everything reported by such a number is true. It yields from the first figure: The existence of Baghdad is true. This syllogism is for reminder, not inference, because it is known that what is reported by the said number is true, and because mutawatirat are self-evident. An example of inference upon theoretical assent: your saying: I was created after I was non-existent, and everything created after being non-existent must have a creator. It yields from the first figure: I must have a creator. And you have known from what preceded that disputation concerning assent is only of two types, no third: first: theoretical assent, which needs a proof. Second: hidden self-evident assent, which needs a reminder. And these are the details of the methods of disputation concerning them: Know first: if the proponent of assent presents to you a theoretical assent or a hidden self-evident assent, each one has two states. As for the two states of theoretical assent: the first is that he presents it to you without proof, in order to mislead that it is among the self-evidents that do not need a proof, or for another purpose. The second is that he presents it to you accompanied by proof. And these two are also the states of the hidden self-evident: because sometimes he presents it to you without a reminder to mislead that it is among the clear things or for another purpose, and sometimes he presents it to you accompanied by a reminder. So the divisions are four: First: theoretical assent without proof. Second: theoretical assent with proof. Third: hidden self-evident without reminder. Fourth: hidden self-evident with reminder. If he presents to you a theoretical assent without proof, or a hidden self-evident without reminder, then you have nothing in objecting to it but one thing: denial. Such as you saying: I deny this claim, or I do not concede this claim, or this claim is denied, or not granted, and the like. And it suffices you to limit yourself to one of those expressions. So your denial will be a denial without support, and that is the more apparent to me. And more than one of the people of this art have said: there is no obstacle to mentioning along with the denial the support upon which you relied in the denial, so your denial...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of types of assent (tasdiq) and the corresponding permissible objections in disputation (munazara). It begins by giving two examples: the first illustrates tanbih (reminder) for a mutawatir (widespread report) claim, using a syllogism in the first figure to remind that the existence of Baghdad is true. The second illustrates istidlal (inference) for a theoretical claim, using a first-figure syllogism to prove that the speaker must have a creator. The author then states that disputation concerning assent is only applicable to two types: theoretical (nazari) which requires proof, and hidden self-evident (badihi khafi) which requires reminder (tanbih). He then explains that each type can be presented in two states: either without any proof/reminder (to mislead as to its status) or with its accompanying proof/reminder. This gives four cases. The rule: when a theoretical assent is presented without proof, or a hidden self-evident assent is presented without reminder, the only valid objection is outright denial (man'). The author lists permissible expressions of denial and states that the denial may be given without supporting evidence (man' mujarrad 'an al-sanad), which he considers the more apparent view. He then notes that some scholars permit mentioning the support with the denial, but the text cuts off before completing the discussion.

التصديق النظري
التصديق البديهي الخفي
التنبيه
الاستدلال
المنع

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of inference (istidlal) for a theoretical assent (tasdiq nazari)?
A) The mutawatir example about Baghdad
B) The creation example: 'I was created... I must have a creator'
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq in munazara is only of two types, each with two presentation states, forming four cases that dictate the opponent's response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, an opponent asserts 'The Quran is the word of God' without providing any evidence. Classify this tasdiq (type and presentation state) and state what objection is permissible according to the passage.

The Rule of Denial (Man') for Unsupported Tasdiq
Author states that man' is the only valid objection for unsupported claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the only permissible objection to a tasdiq presented without its required support is denial (man'), and differentiate between man' mujarrad and man' with sanad.

Core Idea
When a claim is presented without proof or reminder, the opponent may simply deny it without justification.

In the previous lesson, we learned the four cases of tasdiq presentation. Now we focus on the two cases where the claim is presented without its necessary support. For these, the author rules that the opponent has only one objection: denial (man'). This lesson explores the nature of that denial and the scholarly disagreement over whether it may be accompanied by supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases requiring man' only
From the four cases, focus on case 1 (nazari without proof) and case 3 (badihi khafi without reminder). These trigger the rule that the opponent may only deny.
The other two cases (with proof/reminder) require a different response, not discussed here.
2
Learn the expressions of denial
The passage lists phrases like 'amna'u hadhihi al-da'wa', 'la usallimu laka', 'hadhihi al-da'wa mamnu'a'. The opponent may choose any single expression.
3
Understand the concept of man' mujarrad
The author states that it suffices to simply deny without providing any support (sanad). This is his preferred view: 'al-azhar 'indi'.
This is a point of difference; some scholars require a reason.
4
Consider the opposing view on sanad
The passage mentions that some scholars allow mentioning the support (sanad) for the denial. The text cuts off, but note that this is an internal debate in the discipline.

And an example of reminder in the mutawatir: you say: The existence of Baghdad was reported by a number whose collusion upon lying is habitually impossible. And everything reported by such a number is true. It yields from the first figure: The existence of Baghdad is true. This syllogism is for reminder, not inference, because it is known that what is reported by the said number is true, and because mutawatirat are self-evident. An example of inference upon theoretical assent: your saying: I was created after I was non-existent, and everything created after being non-existent must have a creator. It yields from the first figure: I must have a creator. And you have known from what preceded that disputation concerning assent is only of two types, no third: first: theoretical assent, which needs a proof. Second: hidden self-evident assent, which needs a reminder. And these are the details of the methods of disputation concerning them: Know first: if the proponent of assent presents to you a theoretical assent or a hidden self-evident assent, each one has two states. As for the two states of theoretical assent: the first is that he presents it to you without proof, in order to mislead that it is among the self-evidents that do not need a proof, or for another purpose. The second is that he presents it to you accompanied by proof. And these two are also the states of the hidden self-evident: because sometimes he presents it to you without a reminder to mislead that it is among the clear things or for another purpose, and sometimes he presents it to you accompanied by a reminder. So the divisions are four: First: theoretical assent without proof. Second: theoretical assent with proof. Third: hidden self-evident without reminder. Fourth: hidden self-evident with reminder. If he presents to you a theoretical assent without proof, or a hidden self-evident without reminder, then you have nothing in objecting to it but one thing: denial. Such as you saying: I deny this claim, or I do not concede this claim, or this claim is denied, or not granted, and the like. And it suffices you to limit yourself to one of those expressions. So your denial will be a denial without support, and that is the more apparent to me. And more than one of the people of this art have said: there is no obstacle to mentioning along with the denial the support upon which you relied in the denial, so your denial...
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of types of assent (tasdiq) and the corresponding permissible objections in disputation (munazara). It begins by giving two examples: the first illustrates tanbih (reminder) for a mutawatir (widespread report) claim, using a syllogism in the first figure to remind that the existence of Baghdad is true. The second illustrates istidlal (inference) for a theoretical claim, using a first-figure syllogism to prove that the speaker must have a creator. The author then states that disputation concerning assent is only applicable to two types: theoretical (nazari) which requires proof, and hidden self-evident (badihi khafi) which requires reminder (tanbih). He then explains that each type can be presented in two states: either without any proof/reminder (to mislead as to its status) or with its accompanying proof/reminder. This gives four cases. The rule: when a theoretical assent is presented without proof, or a hidden self-evident assent is presented without reminder, the only valid objection is outright denial (man'). The author lists permissible expressions of denial and states that the denial may be given without supporting evidence (man' mujarrad 'an al-sanad), which he considers the more apparent view. He then notes that some scholars permit mentioning the support with the denial, but the text cuts off before completing the discussion.

Key Terms
المنع
المنع المجرد عن السند
السند
التصديق النظري
التصديق البديهي الخفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a theoretical assent is presented without proof, what is the opponent's only valid objection?
A) al-man' (denial)
B) al-naqd (refutation)
C) al-mu'arada (counter-argument)
D) al-su'al (asking a question)

Key Takeaway
For unsupported claims, denial (man') is the sole objection, and it may be given without any supporting evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
