Denial with Evidence as Usurpation
Defines when denial with evidence becomes usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a questioner's denial accompanied by evidence constitutes usurpation (ghaṣb) in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Providing evidence for a denial before the opponent proves his claim is stealing his role.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), each participant has a defined role. The respondent proves his claim; the questioner objects. If the questioner brings evidence in his objection before the respondent has provided proof, he oversteps his role. This is called ghaṣb (usurpation). This lesson clarifies the rule with examples from theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the respondent's claim
Determine whether the claim is theoretical (requires proof) or hidden self-evident (requires a reminder). Check if the respondent has provided any proof or reminder.
2
Examine the questioner's response
Is it a bare denial (manʿ mujarrad) or a denial with evidence (manʿ maʿa al-sanad)?
3
Apply the rule
If the claim is unsupported, only bare denial is allowed. If the questioner brings evidence, it is usurpation because argumentation belongs to the respondent.
Some scholars permit it, but the default ruling is that it is ghaṣb.
4
Test with examples
Use the examples from the passage: 'The world is temporally originated' (theoretical) and 'Rise of river water causes rise of well water' (hidden self-evident).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذكورُ مقرونا بالسند . والذي يظهر أن إتيانه بالسند في منع دعوى لم يقم صاحبها عليها دليلاً أنه من أنواع الغصب، والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند أهل هذا الفن، وسيأتي في الكلام على الغصب أن السائل إذا عمد إلى دعوى غير مستدل عليها أو إلى مقدمة دليل لم يُقم المعلل عليها دليلاً فأقام دليلاً على بطلان إحداهما أنه يكون غاصبا والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة في هذا الفن، ووجه كونه غاصبا ظاهر؛ لأن الاستدلال لصاحب التصديق المحتاج إلى دليل أو تنبيه، فإذا منعه السائل واستدل على منعه فقد غصب وظيفة المعلل التي هي الاستدلال، واقتران المنع بسند استدلال بذلك السند على بطلان دعوى المعلل قبل أن يستدل عليها فكونه من قبيل الغصب ظاهر . والظاهر أن من أجاز اقتران المنع المذكور بالسند من أهل هذا الفن يرى هذا النوع من الغصب جائزا كما لا يخفى . ومثاله في التصديق النظري أن تقول: العالم كله حادث، فيقول خصمك القائلُ بقدمه من الفلاسفة الضالين : أمنع دعواك هذه . ومثاله في البديهي الخفي أن تقول : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سبب في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، فيقول خصمك : أمنع هذه الدعوى، أو : لا أسلمها، ونحو ذلك من العبارات المتقدمة . وسيأتي إن شاء الله (۱) أوجه الجواب عن المنع المذكور، مع أمثلة (۱) ص ۲۲۳ . استدلال السائل على المنع الذي ذكرنا أنه من الغصب . وإذا عرفت أن التصديق الذي لم يكن مقرونا بدليل إن كان نظريًا، أو بتنبيه إن كان بديهيا خفيًا، لا يصح الاعتراض على واحد منهما إلا بالمنع مجردًا عن السند، أو أو مع السند عند من يجيز ذلك، فاعلم أن صاحب التصديق إذا ألقى إليك تصديقا نظريًا مصحوبًا بالدليل على صحته، أو تصديقا بديهيا خفيًا مصحوبًا بتنبيه يزيل عنه الخفاء، فلك في الاعتراض على صاحب التصديق حينئذ ثلاث طرق(۱)، كل واحدة منها يجوز للسائل المناظرة بها والاعتراض على صاحب التصديق بها: الطريق الأولى : هي أن تمنع مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل تمنع صغراه فقط، أو كبراه فقط، وإن منعت الصغرى ومنعت الكبرى أيضًا فهما منعان . وهذا النوع الذي هو منع مقدمة معينة من الدليل هو أسلم وظائف السائل، وأبعدها عن شائبة الغصب، وهو المعروف بالمناقضة، وإنما سمي أيضًا مناقضة لاستلزامه الإبطال في بعض الموارد، ويسمى بالنقض التفصيلي ؛ لتفصيل السائل في المنع بتبيين المقدمة الممنوعة أيضًا بالمنع الحقيقي ،والممانعة، فهذه كلها أسماء له عند أهل هذا الفن .
The mentioned [claim] accompanied by evidence. And it appears that his bringing evidence in denying a claim whose proponent did not establish evidence for it is a type of usurpation, and usurpation is an unacceptable function among the practitioners of this art. It will come in the discussion on usurpation that if the questioner intentionally [targets] a claim not supported by evidence or a premise of a proof for which the arguer did not establish evidence, and he brings evidence for the invalidity of one of them, then he is a usurper, and usurpation is an unacceptable function in this art. The reason for his being a usurper is clear: because argumentation belongs to the proponent of the proposition who needs a proof or a reminder. So when the questioner denies him and argues for his denial, he has usurped the function of the arguer, which is argumentation. And coupling the denial with evidence—arguing by that evidence for the invalidity of the arguer's claim before he argues for it—so its being a type of usurpation is clear. And it appears that whoever permits the aforementioned denial coupled with evidence among the practitioners of this art considers this type of usurpation permissible, as is not hidden. An example of this in theoretical assent is when you say: 'The entire world is temporally originated', and your opponent, among the straying philosophers who assert its eternity, says: 'I deny this claim of yours.' An example of it in hidden self-evident [propositions] is when you say: 'The rise of river water is a cause for the rise of well water', and your opponent says: 'I deny this claim' or 'I do not concede it', and similar expressions mentioned earlier. And there will come, God willing, the aspects of the answer to the mentioned denial, with examples (1) p.223. [This is] the questioner's argumentation on the denial which we mentioned that it is part of usurpation. And if you know that a proposition that is not accompanied by a proof—if it is theoretical, or by a reminder if it is a hidden self-evident—it is not valid to object to either of them except with a denial bare of evidence, or with evidence according to those who permit that. Then know that when the proponent of the proposition presents to you a theoretical proposition accompanied by the proof of its correctness, or a hidden self-evident proposition accompanied by a reminder that removes its hiddenness, then you have three ways to object to the proponent of the proposition at that point, each of which is permissible for the questioner to debate and object with: The first way is that you deny a specific premise from the premises of the proof—you deny its minor premise only, or its major premise only, and if you deny the minor and also deny the major, then they are two denials. And this type, which is denying a specific premise of the proof, is the safest of the questioner's functions, and the furthest from any taint of usurpation. It is known as contradiction (al-munaqaḍah), and it is also called contradiction because it entails invalidation in some cases, and it is called detailed refutation (al-naqḍ al-tafṣīlī) because of the questioner's detail in denial by specifying the denied premise. Also by [the terms] actual denial and opposition—these are all names for it among the practitioners of this art.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from Adab Bahth deals with the rules of objection in debate. It first clarifies when a denial accompanied by evidence constitutes usurpation (ghasb) of the respondent's role. The author explains that if the questioner responds to an unsupported claim by denying it with evidence, he is usurping the respondent's function, which is to provide proof. This is unacceptable unless the respondent has already provided evidence. The text gives two examples: a theoretical claim (the world is originated) and a hidden self-evident claim (river water rise causes well water rise). The author then transitions to the proper ways to object when a claim is accompanied by evidence. Three methods are introduced; the first is detailed here: denying a specific premise of the proof (minor or major). This is called munaqaḍah (contradiction) or naqḍ tafsīlī (detailed refutation) and is considered the safest function for the questioner because it avoids usurpation.

Key Takeaway
A questioner must not provide evidence for a denial when the respondent has not yet proven his claim; doing so is usurpation.

The First Objection Method: Denying a Specific Premise
Introduces the first of three objection methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first objection method (munaqaḍah/naqḍ tafṣīlī) by identifying which premise of a proof to deny.

Core Idea
Denying a specific premise is the safest objection because it respects the respondent's role.

Once the respondent has presented a proof with premises, the questioner may object. The safest method is to deny one specific premise (e.g., major or minor). This is called munaqaḍah (contradiction) or naqḍ tafṣīlī (detailed refutation). This lesson explains how it works and why it is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof structure
The respondent's proof consists of premises: usually a minor premise (ṣughrā) and a major premise (kubrā). Identify them.
2
Choose which premise to deny
You may deny the minor premise only, the major premise only, or both (each is a separate denial). Do not provide evidence for the denial—keep it bare.
3
Verify it is not usurpation
Since the respondent has already provided proof, a bare denial of a specific premise is not usurpation. It is a legitimate objection.
4
Recognize its names
This method is called munaqaḍah (because it can invalidate the proof) or naqḍ tafṣīlī (because it specifies the denied premise).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Tanbih (Alerting) is of Limited Benefit in Debate
Author explains tanbih's limited benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tanbih from istidlal and explain why objections to tanbih are generally invalid in Islamic dialectics.

Core Idea
Tanbih alerts the self-evident; it does not prove, so objecting to it misses the point.

In this passage, the author explains why tanbih (alerting) is of limited benefit in debate. Unlike istidlal, which aims to establish a claim from first principles, tanbih merely draws attention to something already self-evident. You will learn to recognize tanbih and understand why objections against it are typically misdirected, citing the authority of al-Jurjani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being asserted
In the example, the proponent claims: 'The prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain.' This is the conclusion he wants to defend.
The claim is presented as self-evident to anyone familiar with mutawatir hadith.
2
Recognize the form of tanbih
The proponent provides a syllogism: major premise: 'Every prohibition reported by mutawatir is certain'; minor: 'This prohibition is reported by mutawatir'; conclusion: 'Therefore it is certain.' This is tanbih because the minor premise is the very thing that makes it self-evident.
The syllogism does not prove the claim but clarifies why it is already known.
3
Note the opponent's objection
The opponent says: 'I prevent the minor premise' – i.e., he denies that the prohibition is mutawatir. This is a man' (prevention) against a specific premise.
In tanbih, such an objection is misplaced because the truth is independent of the syllogism.
4
Understand the author's response
The author explains that tanbih is not meant to establish the claim; it only removes obscurity. A genuine objection (man' haqiqi) would only apply if the tanbih was used incorrectly, as in the flawed example given.
The author cites al-Jurjani to reinforce that tanbih's benefit is small.
5
Contrast tanbih with istidlal
Unlike tanbih, istidlal (inferential proof) aims to prove a claim from first principles. Therefore, objections against istidlal are meaningful and can be genuine. The author will henceforth focus on istidlal.
This contrast helps in evaluating the strength of objections in debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And its example in alerting (tanbih) is that the proponent of a belief says: The prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain. Then he establishes proof for that by saying: Its prohibition was reported from the Prophet by a number whose collusion in falsehood is impossible, and every prohibition reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him) by a number whose collusion in falsehood is impossible is certain. From the first figure it results: The prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain. So his opponent says: I prevent the minor premise of your proof, which is your statement: 'Its prohibition was reported by a number whose collusion in falsehood is impossible.' And know that we do not prolong speech about tanbih due to the paucity of benefit in it; because the self-evident does not need a proof for its original establishment, rather it needs an alerting that removes the obscurity from it, and a genuine objection that is true in reality does not apply to it unless its author brought it in a way other than its proper way, like the example we mentioned. And we only mentioned it in a way other than its proper way to clarify an example of objection (man') being applied to it, and the example is not objected to as previously mentioned. And we will come, God willing, in what follows with correct examples of tanbih and istidlal, where the objection applied will be false, to clarify the aspect of its falsity. And al-Jurjani said in his treatise on research and debate, verbatim: 'As for tanbih, that (objection) applies to it, but its benefit is not great because it is not intended for establishing the claim, so it does not harm its establishment which is independent of proof, unlike istidlal.' End quote. And it is clear. And based on that, we will reduce speech on tanbih and expand on istidlal. And these examples we have mentioned are all examples of preventing a specific premise from the evidence of the proponent for his belief, while the said prevention is bare of evidence. And it is known that the said prevention of the specific premise is sometimes bare of evidence, and sometimes accompanied by evidence, and it should not be that the said prevention is accompanied by evidence except after the proponent has established proof against it; otherwise the questioner's prevention
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage first gives an example of tanbih: a proponent wants to assert that the prohibition of domestic donkeys is certain (mutawātir transmitted). He provides a syllogism that is essentially tautological: the prohibition is reported by a mass-transmission, and any mass-transmitted prohibition is certain, so the prohibition is certain. The opponent objects by preventing the minor premise, i.e., denying that it is mass-transmitted. The author then explains why such objections to tanbih are not serious: tanbih is only meant to remove obscurity from a self-evident truth (badīhī), not to prove it in the first place. A genuine objection would only apply if the proponent used tanbih wrongly (like the flawed example given). The author cites al-Jurjānī who states that tanbih has little benefit because it is not intended for proof, so denying it does not harm the claim which is self-evident. Consequently, the author will focus on istidlal (deductive reasoning) rather than tanbih in the rest of the book. Finally, he notes that the examples given earlier all illustrate a man' that is bare of any supporting evidence (sanad), but man' could be accompanied by evidence, though only after the proponent has provided his own proof.

Key Takeaway

Types of Objection in Debate: With and Without Evidence
Author explains types of objection and the ghasb controversy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between objection with evidence and objection without evidence, and identify when each is permissible in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Objection without evidence is always allowed; objection with evidence must wait until the opponent proves his premise.

In any structured debate, the questioner must know how to object properly. This passage from al-'Amidi distinguishes two types of objection: one that merely denies a premise (mujarrad) and one that brings evidence (maqrun). Understanding the difference is crucial to maintain the correct roles of questioner and opponent. Let's break down the rules step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of the objection
In the Arabic passage, the author says the objection is to 'a specific premise of an argument' (مقدمة معينة من دليل). Find which premise is being objected to in a given debate scenario.
Focus on the premise, not the entire argument.
2
Determine if the objection is accompanied by evidence
Check whether the questioner simply says 'I reject that premise' (man' mujarrad) or provides a reason or support (sanad). The passage states that both types are possible, but the timing differs.
Evidence here could be a short argument or a reference to authority.
3
Apply the timing rule for evidence-based objections
If the objection includes evidence, it is only permissible after the opponent (mu'allil) has presented his proof for that premise. Objecting with evidence before that is called 'ghasb' (usurpation), which is generally rejected.
This rule preserves the procedural order: the opponent speaks first, then the questioner may respond with evidence.
4
Recognize the exception: al-'Amidi's view on ghasb
The author notes that a small minority, including Rukn al-Din al-'Amidi, allows ghasb. However, the majority holds it invalid because it reverses the roles. Be able to state both positions.
This is the root of a known controversy in Adab al-Bahth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأمثلة التي ذكرنا كلُّها أمثلة لمنع مقدمة معينة من دليل ومن المعلل على تصديقه مع كون المنع المذكور مجردًا عن السند المعلوم أن المنع المذكور للمقدمة المعينة تارة يكون مجردا عن السند، وتارة يكون مقرونا بالسند ولا ينبغي أن يكون المنع المذكور مقرونا بالسند إلا بعد إقامة المعلل عليه الدليل ; فإن منعه السائل بالسند قبل استدلال المعلل فهو غصب، وقد قدمنا أن الغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند أهل هذا الفن خلافًا لمن أجازه من أهل هذا الفن، كركن الدين أبي حامد محمد بن محمد العميدي السمرقندي الحنفي ، وهو أول من ميز هذا الفن عن غيره من العلوم، وجعله فنا مستقلاً على الكيفية التي يتناقلها أهله (2). وهو يجيز الغصب المذكور، هو ومن تبعه، وأكثر أهل هذا الفن على أنه وظيفة غير مقبولة، وهو الصواب ; لأن ارتكاب الغصب يصير السائل في منزلة المعلل، كعكسه، وهذا لا ينبغي في المناظرة . وقد يقول صاحب السند إنه ليس من الغصب؛ لأنه ليس بدليل مركب تام ويجاب بأنه دليل على المنع ، وإن حذف بعض مقدماته، فلو قلتَ مثلاً : لا يجوز أن يكون كذا، فكأنك قلت : العقل يجوز خلاف الدعوى، وكل دعوى يجوز العقل خلافها فليست بصحيحة إلا احتمالاً، واحتمال الصحة لا يستلزم الصحة، فمنعها بطلب الدليل عليها واضح . أما إن أقام المعلل الدليل على إحدى مقدمتي دليله، أو عليهما، فمنعهما منعًا مقرونا بالسند الذي هو دليل المنع ، فهو مقبول جائز . وإذا عرفت أن منع مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل قد يكون مجردا عن السند كما مثلنا، وقد يكون مقرونا بالسند جوازا اتفاقاً
And these examples we mentioned are all examples of objecting to a specific premise of an argument, and of the opponent regarding his assent, while the mentioned objection is devoid of evidence. It is known that the mentioned objection to a specific premise is sometimes devoid of evidence, and sometimes accompanied by evidence. It is not appropriate for the mentioned objection to be accompanied by evidence except after the opponent has established the proof for it. For if the questioner objects with evidence before the opponent's inference, it is ghasb (usurpation). We have stated previously that ghasb is an unacceptable device among the practitioners of this art, contrary to those who permit it among them, such as Rukn al-Din Abu Hamid Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-‘Amidi al-Samarqandi al-Hanafi, who was the first to distinguish this art from other sciences and made it an independent discipline in the manner transmitted by its people. He permits the aforementioned ghasb, he and those who follow him. The majority of practitioners of this art hold that it is an unacceptable device, and that is correct, because committing ghasb puts the questioner in the position of the opponent, and vice versa, and this is not appropriate in debate. The proponent of evidence may say that it is not ghasb because it is not a complete compound argument. The response is: it is evidence for the objection, even if some of its premises are omitted. For example, if you say: 'It is not permissible to be such,' it is as if you said: 'The intellect allows something contrary to the claim, and every claim that the intellect allows its contrary is not correct except as a possibility, and possibility of correctness does not entail correctness.' So objecting to it by requesting evidence is clear. As for when the opponent has established the proof for one of his two premises, or for both, then objecting to them with an objection accompanied by evidence that is the evidence for the objection, it is acceptable and permissible. And if you know that objecting to a specific premise of the argument may be devoid of evidence as we exemplified, and may be accompanied by evidence permissibility according to agreement.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage details the rules of objection (man') in debate. The author distinguishes two types: objection without evidence (mujarrad 'an al-sanad) and objection with evidence (maqrun bi al-sanad). The latter is only permissible after the opponent (mu'allil) has presented his proof. If the questioner (sa'il) objects with evidence before the opponent's inference, it is called ghasb (usurpation), which most scholars reject. The author mentions that Rukn al-Din al-'Amidi allowed it, but the majority consider it invalid because it reverses the roles of questioner and opponent. The author then addresses a potential counterargument: someone might claim it is not ghasb because it is not a complete argument. The response is that even if some premises are omitted, it is still an argument for the objection. An example is given: saying 'It is not permissible to be such' implicitly involves a syllogism about intellectual possibility. Finally, if the opponent has already proven his premises, then objecting with evidence is acceptable. The passage underscores the procedural etiquette of debate.

The Prohibition of Ghasb in Dialectical Argumentation
Author explains the rule against ghasb and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a questioner commits ghasb in a disputation and explain why it is impermissible according to the majority of jadal scholars.

Core Idea
Ghasb is using your opponent's evidence before his argument is complete, which disrupts the proper roles in disputation.

In this passage, the author discusses a key rule of adab al-bahth: the questioner may not 'usurp' the evidence (sanad) from the respondent before the respondent has finished presenting his argument. This rule ensures that each party maintains their proper role. We will explore what ghasb is, why the majority forbid it, and when it is actually permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ghasb
Find how the passage defines ghasb. What action is considered usurpation?
The passage begins: 'Using the sanad before the mu‘allil’s argument is usurpation.' So ghasb occurs when the questioner uses evidence to reject a claim before the mu‘allil has completed his argument.
2
Understand the majority position
The author says most scholars reject ghasb. Why? What is the underlying reasoning about role inversion?
The author explains: 'because committing usurpation makes the questioner assume the position of the mu‘allil, and vice versa, and this is not appropriate in disputation.'
3
Examine the objection
Some claim that using a sanad is not ghasb if it is not a complete argument. How does the author respond?
The author responds that even if some premises are omitted, it still functions as an argument for rejection. He gives an example: saying 'It is not permissible that it be such' implies a whole argument.
4
Learn the permissible scenario
When is it allowed to reject a premise with a sanad? Look at the condition mentioned at the end.
If the mu‘allil himself has presented evidence for a premise (or both premises), then rejecting it with a sanad is acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالسند قبل استدلال المعلل فهو غصب، وقد قدمنا أن الغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند أهل هذا الفن خلافًا لمن أجازه من أهل هذا الفن، كركن الدين أبي حامد محمد بن محمد العميدي السمرقندي الحنفي، وهو أول من ميز هذا الفن عن غيره من العلوم، وجعله فنا مستقلاً على الكيفية التي يتناقلها أهله، وهو يجيز الغصب المذكور، هو ومن تبعه، وأكثر أهل هذا الفن على أنه وظيفة غير مقبولة، وهو الصواب؛ لأن ارتكاب الغصب يصير السائل في منزلة المعلل، كعكسه، وهذا لا ينبغي في المناظرة. وقد يقول صاحب السند إنه ليس من الغصب؛ لأنه ليس بدليل مركب تام ويجاب بأنه دليل على المنع، وإن حذف بعض مقدماته، فلو قلتَ مثلاً: لا يجوز أن يكون كذا، فكأنك قلت: العقل يجوز خلاف الدعوى، وكل دعوى يجوز العقل خلافها فليست بصحيحة إلا احتمالاً، واحتمال الصحة لا يستلزم الصحة، فمنعها بطلب الدليل عليها واضح. أما إن أقام المعلل الدليل على إحدى مقدمتي دليله، أو عليهما، فمنعهما منعًا مقرونا بالسند الذي هو دليل المنع، فهو مقبول جائز.
Using the sanad before the mu‘allil’s argument is usurpation. We have already mentioned that usurpation is an unacceptable practice among the people of this art, contrary to those who permit it, such as Rukn al-Din Abu Hamid Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-‘Amidi al-Samarqandi al-Hanafi, who was the first to distinguish this art from other sciences and make it an independent discipline in the manner transmitted by its practitioners. He and his followers permit the aforementioned usurpation, but the majority of the people of this art hold that it is unacceptable, and this is correct, because committing usurpation makes the questioner assume the position of the mu‘allil, and vice versa, and this is not appropriate in disputation. The possessor of the sanad may say that it is not usurpation because it is not a complete composite argument. The answer is that it is an argument for rejection, even if some of its premises are omitted. For example, if you say: 'It is not permissible that it be such', it is as if you said: 'Reason permits the opposite of the claim, and every claim whose opposite reason permits is not true except as a possibility, and the possibility of truth does not entail truth', so rejecting it by requesting evidence for it is clear. As for when the mu‘allil presents evidence for one of the two premises of his argument, or for both, then rejecting them with a rejection accompanied by a sanad that is the evidence for the rejection, it is acceptable and permissible.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage addresses a technical point in dialectical argumentation: the prohibition of 'ghasb' (usurpation). The author begins by stating that using the sanad (the evidence) before the mu‘allil (the one who presents the proof) has established his argument is considered ghasb. He notes that while some, like al-‘Amidi, permit this, the majority of scholars of this art reject it because it inverts the roles of questioner and respondent, which is inappropriate. The author then examines an objection: the possessor of the sanad might claim it is not usurpation because his statement is not a fully composed argument. The response clarifies that even if some premises are omitted, it still functions as an argument for rejection. A concrete example is given: if one says 'It is not permissible that it be such', that implies that reason allows the opposite; any claim that reason allows the opposite is not certain but merely possible; thus requesting evidence for it is legitimate. Finally, the author distinguishes a permissible scenario: if the mu‘allil himself brings evidence for a premise, rejecting it with a sanad is allowed. This lesson teaches the etiquette of disputation and the importance of not prematurely employing arguments.

السند

C) Because it always involves lying or deception.
D) Because it is prohibited by a clear textual evidence (nas).

Key Takeaway
Ghasb is prohibited because it disrupts the roles in disputation; however, if the mu‘allil has already presented evidence, rejecting with a sanad is allowed.

Definition and Threefold Division of Sanad
Author defines sanad and classifies it into three types based on the form of its premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of sanad in a jadal argument and classify a given sanad as lammī, qaṭ‘ī, or ḥillī.

Core Idea
Sanad in jadal is the foundational support for rejecting a claim, and it takes three distinct forms.

In this passage, the author defines the term sanad and explains its different types. Understanding these types is crucial for participating in dialectical debates (jadal) where one must respond to objections with appropriate evidence. The sanad is not just any evidence—it is specifically what the listener (respondent) states with the belief that it implies the opposite of the claim being rejected.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of sanad
Identify what the listener (sāmiʿ) says, believing it contradicts the claim. The sanad is the foundational support (mustanad) used to invalidate the claim.
The author emphasizes that the listener believes the sanad entails the opposite (naqīḍ) of the claim.
2
Recognize the three types based on form
The author divides sanad into three types: causal (lammī), definitive (qaṭ‘ī), and analytical (ḥillī). This division is by the form in which the sanad is presented.
These names may differ from other classifications; the author focuses on the structure of the sanad statement.
3
Understand the naming of lammī
Al-lammī is attributed to the word 'lima' (why) because the sanad involves asking why the claim is false, implying a causal link.
This is analogous to burhān limmī in logic, which gives the cause.
4
Differentiate the three types
A lammī sanad provides a causal reason; a qaṭ‘ī sanad is based on certain evidence; a ḥillī sanad analyzes the claim or terms.
The passage does not elaborate on qaṭ‘ī and ḥillī further, but they are distinct in purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت أن منع مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل قد يكون مجردا عن السند كما مثلنا، وقد يكون مقرونا بالسند جوازا اتفاقا، إما مع إقامة صاحبها الدليل عليها، وقد يكون بدون إقامته الدليل عند من يجوز ذلك، فاعلم أن السند في اصطلاح أهل هذا الفن هو ما يذكره السامع معتقدا أنه يستلزم نقيض الدعوى التي يوجه إليها المنع. وإيضاح ذلك أنه هو المستند الذي يستند إليه المانع في إبطال الدعوى الممنوعة، ويسمى «المستند»، وربما قيل له: «الشاهد». واعلم أن السند المذكور قد تختلف تقسيماته باختلاف الاعتبارات فينقسم بالنظر إلى صورته التي يورد عليها إلى ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: السند اللمي، ويسمى «الجوازي». والثاني: السند القطعي. والثالث: السند الحلي بالحاء المهملة المفتوحة ثم تشديد اللام بعدها ياء النسب. أما اللمي فهو منسوب إلى لفظ «لِمَ»، المركبة من لام الجر، و«ما» الاستفهامية المحذوف ألفها على القاعدة، وإنما نسب هذا النوع من السند إلى «لِمَ» لأنها تذكر فيه، وضابط
And if you know that the rejection of a specific premise from the premises of the argument may be bare of a sanad, as we exemplified, and it may be accompanied by a sanad permissibly and by agreement, either with the author of the premise providing evidence for it, or without him providing evidence in the view of those who permit that. Know that the sanad in the terminology of the people of this art is what the listener mentions, believing that it entails the contradictory of the claim towards which the rejection is directed. Clarification: it is the foundation upon which the rejecter relies in invalidating the rejected claim, and it is called 'al-mustanad', and it may be called 'al-shahid'. Know that the mentioned sanad may have various divisions according to different considerations; it is divided with respect to its form in which it is presented into three parts: first: the causal sanad (al-sanad al-lammī), also called 'al-jawāzī'; second: the definitive sanad (al-sanad al-qaṭ‘ī); third: the analytical sanad (al-sanad al-ḥillī), with the unpointed ḥā’ opened, then shadda on the lām, followed by yā’ of attribution. As for al-lammī, it is attributed to the word 'lima', composed of the preposition lām and the interrogative 'mā' whose alif is elided according to the rule. This type of sanad is attributed to 'lima' because it is mentioned in it, and its rule...

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the sanad in dialectical argumentation. The author first notes that a rejection of a premise may be done without a sanad (as shown in an earlier example) or with a sanad, either when the opponent provides evidence or not. Then the core definition: sanad is what the listener (the respondent) states, believing it implies the opposite of the claim being rejected. It is essentially the support (mustanad) or witness (shahid) used to invalidate the claim. Next, the author divides sanad by its form into three types: (1) al-sanad al-lammī (causal sanad), also called al-jawāzī (permissive?), named after the question 'lima' (why); (2) al-sanad al-qaṭ‘ī (definitive sanad); (3) al-sanad al-ḥillī (analytical sanad). The division is based on the form in which the sanad is presented. The author then explains that the first type is derived from 'lima' because it involves asking why the claim is true, implying a causal link. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the classification is complete. This lesson helps the student understand the different ways a respondent can support a rejection, which is crucial for effective dialectic.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Sanad in Jadal: Definition and the Lami Sanad
Author defines sanad, enumerates its three divisions, and explains sanad lami with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of sanad in jadal and explain how the lami sanad is used to challenge a proof by appealing to contrary possibility.

Core Idea
In jadal, the sanad is the objector's wedge to break a proof; the lami sanad uses 'why not?' to open the door of possibility.

In dialectical debate (jadal), when a prover presents an argument, the objector can challenge it by providing a sanad – a basis for rejecting the proof. The author defines sanad and divides it into three types: lami, qat'i, and hilli. The most subtle is the lami sanad, which appeals to the intellect's permission of a contrary possibility. In this lesson, you will learn how to recognize and deploy this powerful dialectical tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of sanad
Find where the author defines sanad as 'what the listener mentions, believing that it entails the contradictory of the claim'. Note the key components: it is something the objector brings, it entails the negation of the claim, and it is the basis for invalidating the proof.
Sanad in jadal is not the original evidence but a meta-objection.
2
List the three types of sanad by form
The author divides sanad into al-lami (why-based), al-qat'i (definitive), and al-hilli (adornment-based). Focus on al-lami, which is also called al-jawazi (permissive). Memorize these names and their rationale.
The division is based on the form the sanad takes in the debate.
3
Understand the mechanism of the lami sanad
The lami sanad works by pointing out that the intellect permits a state contrary to the prover's claim. Because a contrary is possible, the evidence is not decisive. The objector asks 'lima?' (why not?) to introduce a counter-possibility.
The term 'lami' comes from 'lima' (why). The sanad is called 'jawazi' because it appeals to permissibility (jawaz).
4
Analyze the first example: the rational/human syllogism
The prover says 'That figure is a human' and proves by 'It is rational; every rational is human'. The objector rejects the minor premise 'It is rational' and uses a lami sanad: 'Why might it not be a stone?' This suggests the figure could be non-rational, so the premise is not established.
The objector does not prove the figure is a stone; he only shows that it is possible, which is enough to block the proof.
5
Practice with the second example: the heir/son syllogism
The prover says 'This person is an heir' and proves by 'He is his son; every son is an heir'. The objector would similarly use a lami sanad by asking 'Why might he not be a non-son?' or 'Why might he not be excluded from inheritance?'
The same pattern applies: introduce a permissible alternative to break the certainty of the premise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أن السند في اصطلاح أهل هذا الفن هو ما يذكره السامع معتقدا أنه يستلزم نقيض الدعوى التي يوجه إليها المنع . وإيضاح ذلك أنه هو المستند الذي يستند إليه المانع في إبطال الدعوى الممنوعة «المستند»، وربما قيل له : «الشاهد». واعلم أن السند المذكور قد تختلف تقسيماته باختلاف الاعتبارات فينقسم بالنظر إلى صورته التي يورد عليها إلى ثلاثة أقسام : الأول : السند اللمي، ويسمى «الجوازي». والثاني : السند القطعي . والثالث : السند الحلّي بالحاء المهملة المفتوحة ثم تشديد اللام بعدها ياء النسب . أما اللمي فهو منسوب إلى لفظ «لِمَ»، المركبة من لام الجر، و«ما» الاستفهامية المحذوف ألفها على القاعدة المشار إليها بقول ابن مالك في الخلاصة : و (ما) في الاستفهام إن جُرَتْ حُذف أَلفُها وأولها الها إن تقف (۱) وإنما نسب هذا النوع من السند إلى «لِمَ» لأنها تذكر فيه، وضابط هذا النوع من السند أن المانع يمنع من دليل المعلل مثلاً، ويستند في ذاك المنع إلى أن العقل يجيز أن يكون الأمر على خلاف ما ذكره المعلل، وإذا كان العقل يجيز خلافه فلا يتعيّن هو مع تجويز خلافه؛ لأن تطرق ذلك الاحتمال سند في عدم نهوض ذلك الاستدلال، ولهذا كان اللمي يسمى الجوازي لما ذكرنا . ومن أمثلته أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : ذلك الشبح إنسان، ثم يقيم الدليل على هذا التصديق فيقول : لأنه ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان، ينتج من الشكل الأول: هو ـ أي الشبح المذكور - إنسان، وهذا هو عين التصديق المدعى الذي أقيم عليه الدليل . فيمنع السائل صغرى هذا القياس الاقترانية التي هي : هو ـ أي الشبح المذكور - ناطق، ويقرن هذا المنع بالسند اللمي المقتضي جواز كون ذلك الشبح غير ناطق فيقول : لا نسلم أن ذلك الشبح ناطق، وهذا هو معنى منعه صغرى الدليل، ثم يأتي بالسند اللمي فيقول مثلاً : لم لا يكون حجرًا، أو يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون غير ضاحك، أو : لم لا يجوز أن يكون غير ناطق، وتجويز كونه حجرًا سند لكونه غير ناطق، وكذلك تجويز كونه غير ضاحك سند لكونه غير ناطق، وكونه غير ناطق واضح أنه سند لمنع كونه ناطقا كما ترى . ومن أمثلته أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : هذا الإنسان وارث لهذا الإنسان قطعًا، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه ابنه ، وكل ابن وارث لأبيه ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا الابن [وارث ](١)
Know that al-sanad in the terminology of the practitioners of this art is what the listener mentions, believing that it entails the contradictory of the claim to which the objection is directed. Its clarification is that it is the basis (al-mustanad) upon which the objector relies in invalidating the objected claim; it is called 'al-mustanad', and sometimes called 'al-shahid'. Know that the mentioned sanad may have different divisions according to different considerations, and it is divided by consideration of its form into three divisions: First: al-sanad al-lami, also called 'al-jawazi'. Second: al-sanad al-qat'i. Third: al-sanad al-hilli (with the unpointed ha' opened, then shaddah on the lam, followed by ya' of nisba). As for al-lami, it is attributed to the word 'lima', composed of the preposition 'lam' and the interrogative 'ma' whose alif is dropped according to the rule referred to by Ibn Malik in al-Khulasa: 'And (ma) in interrogation, if it is preceded by a preposition, its alif is dropped and you prefix it with ha' if you stop.' This type of sanad is attributed to 'lima' because it is mentioned in it, and the definition of this type of sanad is that the objector rejects the evidence of the prover, for example, and relies in that rejection on the fact that the intellect permits the matter to be contrary to what the prover mentioned. And if the intellect permits its contrary, then it is not determined along with the permission of its contrary; because the entry of that probability is a sanad for the failure of that inference. For this reason, al-lami is called al-jawazi, as we mentioned. Among its examples: the prover, the possessor of tasdiq, says: 'That figure is a human', then he brings evidence for this tasdiq by saying: 'Because it is rational, and every rational is human', which yields from the first figure: 'It—i.e., the mentioned figure—is a human', and this is exactly the claimed tasdiq for which evidence was brought. Then the questioner rejects the minor premise of this conjunctive syllogism, which is: 'It—i.e., the mentioned figure—is rational', and accompanies this rejection with the lami sanad requiring the permissibility of that figure being non-rational, and says: 'We do not concede that that figure is rational.' This is the meaning of his rejection of the minor premise of the evidence. Then he brings the lami sanad by saying, for example: 'Why might it not be a stone?' or says: 'Why is it not permissible that it be non-laughing?' or: 'Why is it not permissible that it be non-rational?' And the permission of its being a stone is a sanad for its being non-rational, and likewise the permission of its being non-laughing is a sanad for its being non-rational, and its being non-rational is clearly a sanad for rejecting its being rational, as you see. Among its examples: the prover, the possessor of tasdiq, says: 'This person is definitely an heir of this person', then he brings evidence for that by saying: 'Because he is his son, and every son is an heir of his father', which yields from the first figure: 'This son is an heir.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'sanad' in the dialectical art of jadal, then divides it into three types, and elaborates extensively on the first type, al-sanad al-lami (also called al-jawazi). The author states that as-snad is what the listener (the questioner/objector) mentions, believing that it entails the contradictory of the claim being objected to. In other words, it is the basis (mustanad) upon which the objector relies to invalidate the claim. It is also called 'al-mustanad' or 'al-shahid'. The author then explains that sanad can be divided according to its form into three kinds: (1) al-sanad al-lami (causal/why-based), also called al-jawazi (permissive); (2) al-sanad al-qat'i (definitive); and (3) al-sanad al-hilli (adornment-based). The focus is on the first. The term 'lami' is derived from the word 'lima' (why), composed of the preposition 'lam' and the interrogative 'ma'. The author cites Ibn Malik's rule about dropping the alif of 'ma' when preceded by a preposition. The defining characteristic of this type of sanad is that the objector rejects the prover's evidence by appealing to the fact that the intellect permits the matter to be contrary to what the prover stated. Because the intellect allows an alternative, the evidence does not decisively determine the claim; the possibility itself is a sanad for the failure of the inference. Hence it is called 'jawazi' (permissive). The author gives two examples. In the first, the prover claims 'That figure is a human' and proves it by the syllogism: 'It is rational; every rational is human; therefore it is human.' The objector rejects the minor premise ('It is rational') using a lami sanad: 'Why might it not be a stone?', 'Why might it not be non-laughing?', or 'Why might it not be non-rational?' The mere permissibility of these alternatives undermines the certainty of the premise. In the second example, the prover claims 'This person is an heir' and proves it by 'He is his son; every son is an heir of his father; therefore he is an heir.' The objector would similarly use a lami sanad to reject the minor premise or the link. The author illustrates how the lami sanad operates as a dialectical move to halt the proof by introducing counter-possibilities. All examples are from jurisprudential reasoning (heirship, rational vs. non-rational), staying within Islamic sciences.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Three types of sanad in munazara: lam'i, qat'i, halli
Author classifies sanad into three types and exemplifies each in a dialectical exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of sanad (lam'i, qat'i, halli) in a dialectical exchange and identify which type is used in a given objection within an Islamic legal debate.

Core Idea
The three sanads determine how strongly you can challenge a premise: doubt (lam'i), certainty (qat'i), or analysis (halli).

In munazara, objecting to a premise (mani') requires support (sanad). The author classifies sanad into three types based on the kind of support: probative doubt (lam'i), definitive contradiction (qat'i), and analytical diagnosis (halli). This lesson unpacks each type with examples from inheritance and fasting debates, enabling you to evaluate the force of objections in scholarly arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's claim and proof
Find the tasdiq (the proposition the mu'allil is defending) and the syllogism he uses to prove it. In the first example, the tasdiq is 'this son inherits from this father' and the proof is a categorical syllogism.
The mu'allil always defends a tasdiq (assent).
2
Detect the mani' (objection)
The questioner rejects either the major or minor premise of the mu'allil's syllogism. In the first example, he rejects the major premise 'every son inherits from his father'.
The mani' is always paired with a sanad.
3
Classify the sanad
Examine the kind of support paired with the mani'. If the questioner raises a possibility using 'why not' (lima la), it is sanad lam'i. If he asserts a certain fact that directly contradicts the mu'allil's premise, it is sanad qat'i. If he aims to pinpoint the source of error in the mu'allil's reasoning, it is sanad halli.
Look for key phrases: 'lima la' for lam'i, 'how can...?' with a certain fact for qat'i, and analytical diagnosis for halli.
4
Verify with the author's definitions
Compare the example with the controlling principles (dawabit) the author provides. Lam'i implies possibility without certainty; qat'i cuts off doubt; halli focuses on locating the mistake.
The author explicitly states these principles before the examples.

And know that our intention with the examples in which the imams disagreed is always simply an example to understand the principle, and it is not our intention to discuss the evidences of their statements and to prefer the more preponderant among them by evidence. And the matter is that the example is not objected to, for the assumption and probability suffice. As for the third type of the types of sanad, which is the sanad halli, and sometimes they call it al-hall, its controlling principle is that the intent of the questioner (the one who makes the mani') is to clarify the source of the error of the mu'allil, the owner of the tasdiq, and to specify the location of his error, and this type is only introduced

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of the types of 'sanad' (support) that a questioner (sā'il) pairs with a 'mani'' (objection) in a debate (munāḍara). The author defines three types: sanad lamī (probative support), sanad qaṭ'ī (definitive support), and sanad ḥallī (analytical support). 

The first type, sanad lamī, involves the objector rejecting a premise (typically the major or minor) of the proponent's syllogism and providing a reason why that premise might not hold, without definitively disproving the proponent's claim. The author illustrates this with an inheritance example: the opponent rejects the major premise 'every son inherits from his father' by suggesting possibilities that prevent inheritance, such as the son being a disbeliever or a slave, or the father being a disbeliever or a slave. These possibilities are sufficient to cast doubt on the conclusion without certainty. 

The second type, sanad qaṭ'ī, is stronger: the objector uses a definitive argument that directly contradicts the proponent's claim. The author gives two examples. The first: the proponent says a certain phantom (shabah) is not human, arguing that it is a stone, and stones are not human. The objector definitively states that the phantom is a speaker (nāṭiq), which is a trait of humans, thus contradicting the proponent's claim and rejecting the minor premise 'it is a stone' with certainty. The second example involves a Hanafi asserting that fasting Ramadan is valid with intention before noon, arguing that intention suffices before noon overall. The Shafi'i objector rejects the minor premise by citing hadith: 'Deeds are by intentions' and 'No fasting for one who does not determine it from night,' definitively showing that intention must be made before dawn, not just before noon. 

The author then clarifies that these examples are only illustrative and not intended to weigh the positions of the schools. 

Finally, the third type, sanad ḥallī, aims not to refute but to analyze the source of error in the proponent's reasoning. Here the objector seeks to identify where the mu'allil went wrong, providing a deeper critique. The passage ends abruptly but indicates this type is introduced to pinpoint the mistake. 

All examples are firmly rooted in Islamic law (fiqh) and dialectic (jadal), demonstrating practical application of logical principles in scholarly debate.

Key Terms
السند (al-sanad): The support or evidence that the questioner provides alongside the mani' (objection) to justify the rejection of a premise in munazara. [Note: In earlier jadal works, sanad could refer to evidence in an analogical argument; here it is specifically paired with mani' in dialectical exchange.]
المنع (al-mani'): The act of objecting to a premise of the opponent's syllogism, often by denying its truth or applicability. [Note: No significant shift; stable in dialectical tradition.]
المعلل (al-mu'allil): The proponent who gives a proof (ta'lil) for a claim, i.e., the one defending a tasdiq. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, mu'allil often means the one who identifies the 'illa; here it is simply the one arguing for a proposition.]
السند اللمي (al-sanad al-lami): Probative support that introduces a possibility or doubt without certainty, often using 'why not' (lima la) to show a defect in the opponent's premise. [Note: Not found in earlier logic texts; this is a term specific to later dialectical manuals like Adab al-Bahth.]
السند القطعي (al-sanad al-qat'i): Definitive support that provides a certain contradiction to the opponent's claim, using a necessary truth that the opponent cannot deny. [Note: Also used in usul al-fiqh for definitive evidence (qat'i al-dalalah); here it is specifically within dialectical refutation.]
السند الحلي (al-sanad al-halli): Analytical support that aims to pinpoint the exact source of error in the opponent's reasoning, rather than simply refuting the conclusion. [Note: This appears to be a specialized term in this tradition; not commonly found in earlier mantiq.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example provided for sanad qat'i, what does the Shafi'i objector claim to show that the Hanafi's minor premise is false?
A) That intention before noon is valid for all fasts.
B) That the hadith 'Deeds are by intentions' only applies to supererogatory fasts.
C) That the fast must be intended from the night, not just before noon.
D) That the Hanafi's major premise is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
In munazara, the strength of an objection depends on the sanad: lam'i casts doubt, qat'i asserts certainty, and halli analyzes the error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the inheritance example: the mu'allil claims 'this son inherits from this father' using the syllogism: all sons inherit from their fathers, this is a son, therefore he inherits. Identify which type of sanad the questioner uses when he asks 'why is it not permissible that the son be a disbeliever?' Explain why it is lam'i and not qat'i.
?
In the Hanafi-Shafi'i fasting debate, the Shafi'i says 'How can intention suffice before noon when it was begun in an invalid manner due to lack of intention from the night?' Is this sanad qat'i or lam'i? Justify using the author's definition.

Dialectic -- Jadal

The Hili Support and Denial with Causal Support in Jadal
Author defines the essential (hili) support and gives an example of denial with causal support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hili support in a dialectical exchange and distinguish it from a sababi support, and explain the role of denial with support in jadal.

Core Idea
The hili support reveals the root error in an opponent's reasoning; denial must be accompanied by a support to be valid.

In the discipline of jadal (dialectical debate), knowing how to pinpoint the exact error in an opponent's reasoning is crucial. The author introduces the concept of 'essential support' (al-sind al-hili) as a tool to unmask conceptual confusions. He also provides an example of denial with a causal support, showing how a questioner can challenge a premise not by mere rejection but by offering a plausible alternative. Let's explore these two types of support and their application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hili support definition
Find in the passage what the hili support does: it clarifies the source of the error, e.g., confusion of man and horse. The questioner specifies the mistake and shows the confusion of concepts.
The hili support often follows a general refutation (naqd ijmali) but is not always required.
2
Analyze the example of confusion
In the example, someone confuses a man for a horse. The questioner identifies the root: the opponent mistakenly thought a non-speaking being (horse) is a speaking being (man). This is a conceptual error.
3
Examine the denial example with sababi support
The proponent argues: rise of river water causes rise of well water; every cause necessitates its effect; therefore necessitation. The questioner denies the minor premise and provides a sababi support: 'Why is it not possible that the rise of well water is due to another cause?' This challenges the causal exclusivity.
The sababi support offers an alternative explanation rather than identifying a conceptual confusion.
4
Compare hili and sababi supports
Hili support targets conceptual errors (e.g., mistaken identity), while sababi support targets causal claims by proposing alternative causes. Both are forms of denial with support, but they address different aspects of an opponent's argument.
Knowing which support to use depends on the nature of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن محل ذلك فيما لو كان غير ناطق والحال أنه ناطق. فقد اشتبه على المعلل شبح الإنسان بشبح الفرس، فظن أن الإنسان فرس، فأقام الدليل المذكور على ذلك [ فبين] (١) السائل منشأ غلطه [وأنه ](٢) اشتباه شبح الفرس عليه بشبح الإنسان وعيّن موضع غلطه وهو زعمه أن الإنسان فرس . وهذا النوع الذي هو السند الحَلي أكثر ما يقع بعد النقض الإجمالي، الآتي تعريفه إن شاء الله - تعالى (۳)، وليس بلازم أن يُذكر قبله النقصُ الإجمالي على كل حال .
-
ومثال المنع المقترن بسند في التنبيه أن يقول صاحب التنبيه : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سبب في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، وكل سبب مستلزم مسيَّبه، ينتج من الشكل الأول : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار مستلزم مسبّبه، أي وهو ارتفاع ماء الآبار.
فيقول السائل أمنعُ صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سبب في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، ثم يقرن منعه بالسند المي مثلا، فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ارتفاع ماء الآبار لسبب آخر غيرِ ارتفاع ماء الأنهار .
وقد ذكرنا أنا نقلل الكلام على التنبيه لقلة الفائدة فيه، ونبسطه على الاستدلال .
Because the place for that is when it is not speaking and the state is that it is speaking. So the explainer confused the semblance of man with the semblance of a horse, and thought that man is a horse, and he established the mentioned proof for that. Then the questioner clarified the source of his error and that it is confusion of the semblance of a horse with the semblance of man, and he specified the place of his error, which is his claim that man is a horse. And this type, which is the essential (hili) support, mostly occurs after the general refutation (naqd ijmali), whose definition will come, God willing, and it is not necessary that the general refutation be mentioned before it in every case.

And an example of denial accompanied by a support in a reminder (tanbih): that the proponent of the reminder says: The rise of river water is a cause of the rise of well water, and every cause necessitates its effect, which yields from the first figure: The rise of river water necessitates its effect, i.e., the rise of well water. Then the questioner says: I deny the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is your statement: The rise of river water is a cause of the rise of well water, then he accompanies his denial with a causal (sababi) support, for example, saying: Why is it not possible that the rise of well water is due to another cause other than the rise of river water?

And we have mentioned that we shorten our discussion on the reminder due to its little benefit, and we expand on inference (istidlal).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage serves two purposes. First, it defines the 'hili support' (al-sind al-hili), which is an essential support that identifies the root of an error in a debate. The author illustrates this with a case where a confused debater mistakes a man for a horse, and the questioner pinpoints the error. The hili support typically follows a general refutation (naqd ijmali) but is not required. Second, it gives an example of 'denial with a causal support' (al-manع bi al-sind al-sababi) in a categorical syllogism. The proponent (sahib al-tanbih) argues: 'The rise of river water causes the rise of well water; every cause necessitates its effect; therefore, the rise of river water necessitates the rise of well water.' The questioner denies the minor premise ('The rise of river water is a cause...') and provides a causal support by suggesting the possibility of another cause. The author then notes that they will focus on inference (istidlal) rather than reminders, highlighting the limited usefulness of the latter in debate.

Key Terms
السند الحلي
المنع
التنبيه
الاستدلال
النقض الإجمالي

Checkpoint
In the example provided, what type of support does the questioner use when denying the minor premise about river and well water?
A) al-sind al-hili (essential support)
B) al-sind al-sababi (causal support)
C) al-sind al-limmi (rational support)
D) al-sind al-qat'ii (decisive support)

Key Takeaway
The hili support exposes conceptual errors, while denial with sababi support offers alternative explanations; both require the debater to provide a reason, not just negate.

Denial with Rational and Decisive Support in Conditional Syllogisms
Two examples of denial with rational and decisive support in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between rational (limmi) and decisive (qat'ii) support in a dialectical denial of a conditional syllogism, and identify which premise is being denied.

Core Idea
Denial in dialectic can be supported either by rational possibility or decisive observation.

In jadal, the questioner can deny a premise of the proponent's conditional syllogism. But a mere denial is weak; it must be accompanied by a support (sind). This passage presents two types: limmi (rational) and qat'ii (decisive). Understanding the difference is crucial for effective dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's conditional syllogism
In each example, locate the if-then statement and the minor premise that leads to the conclusion. The proponent uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) to prove his claim.
The first example is modus ponens; the second is modus tollens.
2
Determine which premise the questioner denies
The questioner explicitly states whether he denies the major premise (the conditional) or the minor premise (the exception). In the first example, he denies the major; in the second, the minor.
3
Examine the type of support provided
The questioner accompanies his denial with a support (sind). In the first, he gives rational possibilities (horse, mule) showing the conditional is not necessary. In the second, he gives a decisive observation (speaking) that directly contradicts the denied premise.
Limmi support points to alternative possibilities; qat'ii support is based on certain fact.
4
Assess the strength of the support
Rational support opens the door for doubt but does not refute definitively; decisive support aims to refute with certainty. Understand which type is appropriate based on the opponent's claim.
In dialectic, limmi support forces the proponent to defend his conditional; qat'ii support may end the exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال المنع بالسند اللمي في الاستثنائي أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : ذلك الشبح إنسان، ثم يقيم على دعواه دليلاً استثنائيا فيقول : لو كان ذلك الشبح حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا ، لكنه حيوان، ينتج فهو إنسان .
فيقول السائل : أمنعُ كبرى دليلك هذا، وهي الشرطية، أعني قولك : لو كان هذا حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا ويقرن منعه إياها بالسند اللمي فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون حيوانًا فرسًا، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون حيوانا بغلا، وهكذا.
ومثال المنع بالسند القطعي في الاستثنائي أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : ذلك الشبح ليس بإنسان، ثم يقيم على دعواه دليلاً استثنائيًا فيقول لو كان ذلك الشبح إنسانًا لكان حيوانا، لكنه غيرُ حيوان ينتج فهو غير إنسان .
فيقول السائل : أمنعُ صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي الاستثنائية، أعني قولك : لكنه غيرُ حيوان، كيف يكون غير حيوان والحال أنه ناطق .
And an example of denial with rational (limmi) support in a conditional (istithna'i): The explicator, who is the proponent of the affirmation, says: That apparition is a man. Then he establishes a conditional proof for his claim: If that apparition were an animal, it would be a man; but it is an animal; therefore, it is a man.
Then the questioner says: I deny the major premise of this proof of yours, which is the conditional, i.e., your statement: If this were an animal it would be a man. And he accompanies his denial of it with a rational support, saying: Why is it not permissible that it be an animal that is a horse, and why is it not permissible that it be an animal that is a mule, and so on.
And an example of denial with decisive (qat'ii) support in a conditional: The explicator, who is the proponent of the affirmation, says: That apparition is not a man. Then he establishes a conditional proof for his claim: If that apparition were a man, it would be an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore, it is not a man.
Then the questioner says: I deny the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is the conditional premise, i.e., your statement: But it is not an animal. How can it be not an animal when the state is that it is speaking?
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides two examples of denial with different types of support in conditional (istithna'i) syllogisms. In the first example, the proponent claims 'That apparition is a man' using a conditional argument: 'If it were an animal, it would be a man; but it is an animal; therefore it is a man.' The questioner denies the major premise (the conditional 'If...then...') and gives a 'limmi' (rational) support: he suggests that the apparition could be a horse or a mule, showing that the conditional is false because the antecedent (being an animal) does not exclusively lead to the consequent (being a man). This is 'rational' because it points to an alternative possibility that breaks the logical connection.

In the second example, the proponent claims 'That apparition is not a man' with the conditional: 'If it were a man, it would be an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore it is not a man.' The questioner denies the minor premise ('but it is not an animal') and gives a 'qat'ii' (decisive) support: he states that the apparition is speaking (natiq), which is evidence that it is an animal (since speaking implies life and animality). This is 'decisive' because it directly contradicts the premise with a certain fact. The distinction between 'limmi' (rational, based on possibility) and 'qat'ii' (decisive, based on certainty) is key to understanding the types of support in dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
السند اللمي
السند القطعي
الاستثنائي
المعلل صاحب التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of denial with rational support, what premise does the questioner deny?
A) The minor premise (but it is an animal)
B) The major premise (if it were an animal, it would be a man)
C) The conclusion (therefore it is a man)
D) The initial claim (that apparition is a man)

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of a denial in jadal depends on the type of support: rational support opens possibilities, while decisive support directly contradicts with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the first example, explain why the questioner's support is rational (limmi) rather than decisive. Then construct a similar rational support for denying the minor premise in the second example (i.e., deny 'but it is not an animal' using a possible alternative).
?
Identify a real dialectical exchange from a kalah text (e.g., between a Mu'tazili and an Ash'ari) and determine whether the denial uses limmi or qat'ii support. Cite the specific premise denied and the support given.
